# OXFORD_HISTORICAL_MONOGRAPHS

**Original File:** `OXFORD_HISTORICAL_MONOGRAPHS.pdf`
**Total Pages:** 303
**Processed on:** 2026-03-03T17:26:31.183444

根据您提供的索引列表，这是一份关于森林和林业历史的参考文献。以下是对其中一些关键主题的简要概述：

**历史背景**

- **古典时期**：从泰奥弗拉斯塔斯（Theophrastus）到维也纳克勒（Vieriklee），希腊哲学对自然环境和人类活动的早期研究。
- **中世纪和文艺复兴**：森林作为国王领土的一部分，以及木材、皮草和其他森林产品在经济中的重要性。
- **启蒙时代**：科学家如冯·伯格（Edmond von Berg）和冯·泽林格（Franz von Zallinger）对森林生态学的研究。

**林业发展**

- **技术进步**：从传统的手工艺到现代的机械化，以及新技术在林业中的应用。
- **教育和培训**：例如，耶鲁森林学院（Yale Forest School）和其他森林学校的重要性。
- **政策和法规**：森林管理的法律法规，如英国和美国对森林保护的法律。

**森林管理和可持续性**

- **可持续林业**：从传统的利用方式到可持续发展的现代理念。
- **生态系统服务**：森林在调节气候、保持水源和防止土壤侵蚀等方面的作用。
- **生态学和环境问题**：例如，沃尔斯特林（Worster）关于欧洲殖民主义对非洲森林的影响的研究。

**地理分布**

- **全球范围**：从西欧到南美，再到非洲和亚洲的林业发展。
- **区域重点**：如英国、美国、加拿大、澳大利亚和新西兰等国的森林管理。

**社会和文化影响**

- **经济和社会结构**：木材和皮草产业对经济增长和社会发展的贡献。
- **文化和传统**：不同地区对森林资源的传统利用方式和价值观。

**当代问题**

- **环境保护运动**：从早期环保人士如沃森（Captain Watson）到现代的环境保护组织和政策。
- **全球化和国际合作**：联合国等国际组织在森林保护和可持续林业中的作用。

这份索引列表涵盖了广泛的主题，为研究森林和林业历史的学者提供了一个全面的信息来源。

To Amma and Appa

--- Page 7 ---

Acknowledgements
The book you are about to read would never have been possible without
the kindness and generosity of scores of critters. Over the next few pages,
I would like to acknowledge the wonderful beings that have inspired,
enabled me to respire, and often, made me perspire, in the journey to get
this manuscript off my desk and into your hands.
Point Reyes, where I am writing this, is stunningly beautiful, and its every-
day magic serves as a reminder to begin by thanking all the non human enti-
ties that have enriched my life and this writing experience. Just this very
moment, as I look out of the window, in a house literally at the edge of the
earth, I see a fully clothed mountain, with trees swaying to and fro in the
breeze, and in the distance, the blue of the mighty Paciﬁc and some of the few
remaining secrets that the terra-aqueous globe poses to humanity. I ﬁnd
myself humming what I remember of an old Australian environmental song:
‘Open your eyes to the sights of the forest, Cascades of color and
form . . . Every small place you look around you, a new kind of feeling is
born . . . See the tall trees reach up to the sky, Seedlings that spring from the
earth. You are the one, now must decide. How much is this pretty one?’
Over the years, I have been extremely fortunate to have had many such
sublime moments, and to have encountered, experienced, and in some
cases, befriended, snakes and kangaroo rats, otters and sea lions, horses
and donkeys, tigers and bobcats, dogs and monkeys, hornbills and ravens,
and sal, teak, and redwood forests, complete with their sounds, sights and
smells. Perhaps more than anyone else, it is the dignity, grace, and some-
times, just the very presence of these friends—for they are friends—that
has inspired and nourished, and made it possible to sustain a long-term
writing project such as this. Barely a couple of hours ago, for example, as
I was wading in the cold blue ocean water, I noticed, quite suddenly, the
friendly eyes of a harbour seal, barely a few meters away, its whiskers
twitching in an inquisitive and welcoming manner. The seal and I ended
up spending the morning together, communicating, without uttering a
single spoken word. I am especially grateful to have had the opportunity
to get to know and share a bit of my life with a number of speciﬁc indi-
viduals. They include Maharajah, Jesse, Cho Cho, the one and only
Karpa, Chinki, Kutti, Panda, Patch Cat, Snow White, Catomite, Hee
Hee, Ayuka, and of course, my two little darlings, Ali and Jo.

--- Page 8 ---

Having come of age when ecology, rather than terror, was the looming
public issue, these seemingly innocent emotive sensibilities evolved, in
the company of some truly special people, into a full blown social, political
and economic understanding of the character of environmental problems
and crises. As a young teenager, I was fortunate to have found an extremely
talented group of friends with whom I discovered India by foot, bus,
train, and truck top. It is during the years spent with these people, my
erstwhile colleagues in the environmental action group, Kalpavriksh, and
in forests, dam sites, and vast expanses of beauty and waste, that the sen-
sitivities informing this book took root. I would therefore like to thank
everyone in the organization that the historian, Ram Guha, has called the
Kalpavriksh school of social ecology. I am especially grateful to Pradeep
Malhotra, who taught me to write and edit; Prathiba Pande, Ashish
Kothari, Mahesh Rangarajan, Himanshu Malhotra, Jagdish Krishnaswami,
Ghazala Shahabuddin, Mahendra Vyas, Seema Bhatt, Sharad Gaur,
Himanshu Uberoi, Sunita Narain, Rohan D’Souza, E. Somanathan, Una
Mansingh, Priti and Niti Anand, Rahul Ram, and Amita Baviskar. I
would also like to acknowledge everyone with whom I spent more than

particular, Anil and Mira Sadgopal, Satinath Sarangi, Sadhna Karnik,

¹ See e.g. C. Jefferies, A Review of Colonial Research 1940–1960 (London, 1964);
E. B. Worthington, The Ecological Century: A Personal Appraisal (Oxford, 1983); id., Science
in Africa (Oxford, 1938); id., Middle Eastern Science (London, 1945); Lord Hailey, An
African Survey (London, 1956); Inter-Faculty Committee for African Studies, University of
Oxford Symposium on Africa in the Colonial Period I—The Conservation and Development of
Natural Resources (Oxford, 1975) (hereafter IFCAS Symp.). It is worth noting here that even
the then emergent science of ecology was employed for imperial ends. The work of Arthur
Tansley is particularly relevant in this context. Tansley was a pioneering plant ecologist and
coined the term, ‘ecosystem’. He was one of the founders of the British Ecological Society,
and, as Sherardian Professor of Botany at Oxford, attempted to expand the scope of plant
ecological studies worldwide. To this end, Tansley collaborated with scientists in many of the
colonial services. Amongst the most fruitful of these collaborations was the one with
T. F. Chipp, a forest ofﬁcer who worked on the Gold Coast and elsewhere in Africa. Perhaps
the best known of the products of these collaborations was: A. G. Tansley and T. F. Chipp
(eds.), Aims and Methods in Study of Vegetation (London, 1926). Tansley and Chipp made a
strong case for ecological studies in the service of resource management and exploitation in the
empire. Other noteworthy colonial inﬂuences in early ecology include studies by botanists,
soil scientists, and hydrologists such as A. F. Wilhelm Schimper, Johannes Mildbraed, Homer
Le Roy Shantz, Curtis Fletcher Marbut, André Aubreville, T. F. Chipp, G. P. Milne, Fernand
Moreau, Bernard Dearman Burtt, and Clement Gillman (R. W. J. Keay, IFCAS Symp. 83).
For two recent works on the subject of the history of colonial ecology, see Peder Anker,
Imperial Ecology: Environmental Order in the British Empire, 1895–1945 (Cambridge, Mass,
2001) and Helen Tilley. ‘Africa as a Living Laboratory: The African Research Survey and the
British Colonial Empire: Consolidating Environmental, Medical, and Anthropological
Debates, 1920–1940’ (D.Phil. thesis, Oxford University, 2001).
² J. McCracken, ‘Experts and Expertise in Colonial Malawi’, African Affairs, 81
(1982), 102.
1
Introduction
Although more known as the age of empires and great wars, the hundred
years 1850–1950 were also the era of ecological transformation on a global
scale. During the nineteenth century, colonial governments established a
number of scientiﬁc agencies to manage the landscapes of their empires.¹
In the ﬁrst half of the twentieth century they consolidated on this invest-
ment by creating an increasingly co-ordinated network of natural and
social scientiﬁc institutions (see Fig. 1).² They thereby bequeathed a
legacy of eco-development to what today is a considerable proportion of
the third world.

--- Page 19 ---

This enduring legacy has however been deeply contested. The protests
began during the colonial era as uprisings by peasants in villages and
hamlets. Over the ensuing decades, they have lingered and metamorph-
osed into full-blown critiques of post-colonial science policy.³ Given this
potency, it is reasonable to argue that historical investigations into the
agendas of colonial scientiﬁc environmentalist institutions are of practical
relevance to governments, policy makers, and activists. At the same time,
they have potential to inform our understanding of colonial and post-
colonial environmental history, and indeed, add important dimensions to
our knowledge of the impact of European imperialism on the third world.
These tangible policy-relevant and academic connections between the
past and the present provide the prime motivation for this monograph.
Introduction
2
³ For a general idea of the extent of the critique, see T. Hayter, Exploited Earth: British

Figure 1.1. The colonial network of scientiﬁc environmental management.

--- Page 20 ---

The emphasis in this study is forestry in the British Empire. This choice
stems from the fact that British colonial forestry was arguably one of
the most extensive imperial frameworks of scientiﬁc natural resource
management anywhere, and continues to be a key locus of environmental
conﬂicts across Asia and Africa. There is also a pragmatic reason for
the choice. Unlike other forms of colonial environmental intervention,
such as agriculture and irrigation, forestry is unique in that all its practi-
tioners from the second half of the nineteenth century were trained in one
clearly identiﬁable scientiﬁc tradition.⁴This institutional homogeneity is
extremely handy to undertake the kind of broad-based investigation into
the history of environmental management being attempted here.
The purpose of this chapter is to set the context for the argument to
follow. It begins by critically discussing what is involved in focusing on
science and technology in doing environmental history. It goes on to locate
the historiography of British colonial forestry within the context of this
analysis and thereby lays the groundwork for the ensuing chapters.
1.1. SCIENCE AND COLONIAL 
ENVIRONMENTAL HISTORY
During the past two decades, the evolving scholarship on colonial environ-
mental history has shed considerable light on British colonial attitudes to
natural resource management and the human and ecological impact of
the ensuing policies in a wide range of contexts. However, the question
of the role of science in shaping colonial environmental agendas and the
attitudes and values concerning nature and society implicit in scientiﬁc
resource management are largely unaddressed.⁵ With a few exceptions,
the scientiﬁc institutions, communities, or disciplines concerned with
colonial resource management remain largely understudied.⁶
Introduction
3
⁴ This point will be elaborated and developed further in the next section of this chapter.
⁵ For an elaborated version of the argument in this section see Ravi Rajan, ‘The Colonial
Ecodrama: Resonant Themes in the Environmental History of South Africa and South
Asia’, in S. Dovers, R. Edgecombe, and W. Guest (eds.), Perspectives on Southern African
Environmental History (Athens, Oh., 2003).
⁶ The notable exception is the work of Richard Grove. See R. H. Grove, Green
Imperialism (Cambridge, 1995); ‘Early Themes in African Conservation: The Cape in
the 19th Century’, and (with D. Anderson) ‘Introduction: The Scramble for Eden:
Past, Present and Future in African Conservation’, in D. Anderson and R. H. Grove
(eds.), Conservation in Africa: People, Politics and Practice (Cambridge, 1987); ‘Scottish
Missionaries, Evangelical Discourses and the Origins of Conservation Thinking in
Southern Africa 1820–1900’,Journal of Southern African Studies, 15/2 (1989); ‘The Origins

--- Page 21 ---

The reason for this general neglect, arguably, is that scholars have thus
far tended to subsume science within wider concerns that have driven
disciplinary enquiries in colonial history, economic geography, and, more
recently, environmental history—such as the political economy of
imperialism. In doing so, they base their work on the assumption that
science and scientiﬁc ideas were deeply embedded in and manifestations
of imperial rule rather than ‘an independent lever externally brought to
bear on contemporaneous political and economic events’.⁷ Science and
scientiﬁc institutions are consequently seen as ‘tools of empire’.⁸ Scientists
in turn are seen as servants of the colonial state. This attitude is clearly
manifest in the term ‘state science’ used by many scholars to explain the
nature of the colonial sciences of resource management.⁹ The principle
implicit in this ideal-type is that the efﬁcacy of the imperial scientiﬁc and
scientiﬁc institutions concerned with environmental management can
be explained deductively with reference to the political and economic
exigencies of imperialism.
Recently, a few scholars have begun to make the case that such an
approach conceals as much as it reveals. For one of the pioneers, Richard
Grove, the roots of conservation as a colonial scientiﬁc concern lay in the
role played by scientiﬁc communities in the colonial periphery in generat-
ing and propagating ideas of resource conservation. Grove is not explicitly
clear about what such conservation ideas actually meant in terms of agendas
and ideologies of resource management. Despite this, his work offers a
very important message for imperial environmental history and allied
fields. By identifying scientiﬁc communities and institutions as distinctive
entities in colonial society, it suggests that science is not merely a passive
tool of empire but that it has cognitive, ideological, and interventionist
Introduction

Hegemony and Popular Resistance: Towards a Global Synthesis’, in J. MacKenzie (ed.),
Imperialism and the Natural World (Manchester, 1990); ‘Imperialism and the Discourse of
Desiccation: The Institutionalisation of Global Environmental Concerns and the Role of
the Royal Geographical Society 1860–1880’, in M. Bell, R. Butlin, and M. Heffernan
(eds.), Geography and Imperialism (Manchester, 1995); and ‘Chiefs, Boundaries and Sacred
Woodlands: Early Nationalism and the Defeat of Colonial Conservationism in the Gold
Coast and Nigeria, 1870–1916’ (unpublished paper, 1993). Other important and relevant
work in this genre include Richard Drayton’s excellent book on the Royal Botanical
Gardens at Kew, Nature’s Government: Science, Imperial Britain and the ‘Improvement’ of the
World (New Haven, 2000).
⁷ MacKenzie, introduction to Imperialism and the Natural World.
⁸ See D. R. Headrick, Tools of Empire: Technology and European Imperialism in the
Nineteenth Century (Oxford, 1981).
⁹ See e.g. R. Guha, The Unquiet Woods: Ecological Change and Peasant Resistance in the
Himalaya (Delhi, 1989).

--- Page 22 ---

traditions distinct from others in colonial society, especially those of the
colonial bureaucracy.¹⁰ It also advances the idea that scientists were active
agents in the formation of environmental policy, often at loggerheads
with members of other colonial agencies.¹¹
This important insight is arguably quite critical for studies of the
colonial scientiﬁc resource management frameworks established in the
mid nineteenth century and later, and especially relevant to post-colonial
contexts. In order to appreciate its signiﬁcance, it is important to note
that many of the colonial scientiﬁc regimes of resource management had
ﬁrst developed within speciﬁc social, economic, and political milieus in
Europe. The transfer of these technical frameworks to colonial contexts
meant the introduction, in such settings, of their implicit values and
ideologies on nature and resource use priorities, their inherent modes of
production, and their approaches toward engineering speciﬁc forms of
ecological and human change.¹² The vehicles of such transfers, which
were indeed fraught with all sorts of contestations, including over uses of
nature and social engineering, were the scientiﬁc communities involved
in natural resource management. Trained in continental institutions, they
imbibed the traditions and perspectives of the European sciences. When
they went to the colonial empires, these scientists constituted ‘epistemic
communities’ or ‘agree-cultures’ carrying with them agendas of resource
use developed within a European context.¹³
There was however a rub. Although colonial governments ﬁnanced the
scientiﬁc frameworks of resource management and the training of scient-
iﬁc communities, they were not committed to abiding by all the priorities
identiﬁed by scientists. While ecological regulation was an important
issue, particularly by the late nineteenth century, it was by no means their
only major concern.¹⁴ Policies on environmental management advocated
Introduction
5
¹⁰ See especially, Grove, ‘Chiefs, Boundaries and Sacred Woodlands’.
¹¹ An excellent account of such contests is Grove, ‘Scottish Missionaries, Evangelical
Discourses and the Origins of Conservation Thinking in Southern Africa’. See also
William Beinart, ‘Introduction: the Politics of Colonial Conservation’, Journal of Southern
African Studies, 15/2 (1989).
¹² The dynamics and politics of such transfers has been an important theme of discussion
in the growing critique of science and technics referred to at the beginning of this chapter.
See e.g. Nandy (ed.), Science, Hegemony and Violence and Marglin and Marglin (eds.),
Dominating Knowledge: Development, Culture and Resistance.
¹³ The idea of scientists as epistemic communities occurs widely in the sociology of
science and has recently begun to be deployed in discussions of the international politics
of the environment. See e.g. A. Hurrell and B. Kingsbury, ‘The International Politics of the
Environment: An Introduction’, in Hurrell and Kingsbury (eds.), The International Politics
of the Environment (Oxford, 1992). For a description of ‘agree-cultures’ see Pat Roy
Mooney, ‘Private Parts, Public Parts’, paper presented to The Yale Agrarian Studies Seminar.
¹⁴ Beinart, ‘Introduction: The Politics of Colonial Conservation’.

--- Page 23 ---

by scientists usually had ramiﬁcations such as civil unrest, which colonial
governments were not willing to accept. Colonial environmental politics,
as in environmental politics in other contexts, therefore often involved
contests between scientists and scientiﬁc institutions, on the one hand,
and colonial governments and government departments (usually another
scientiﬁc department), on the other. At stake were material priorities and
ideological visions. Moreover, there were conﬂicts between scientists
and indigenous peoples and between scientists and governments over
what to do with local populations.¹⁵
Against this backdrop, a focus on science in colonial environmental
history involves asking three broad questions. At the outset, there is the
issue of the priorities and objectives of natural resource management of
the various imperial scientiﬁc institutions. Important here is an under-
standing of the European origins of these sciences and the nature and
extent to which this background informed the goals and priorities of
colonial environmental regimes. Secondly, there is the question of the
ideological orientation of these sciences—what these agendas meant in
terms of attitudes concerning nature and toward indigenous populations.
Last, but by no means least, the differences in attitudes and priorities
between scientists and others in colonial society, and the resultant tensions
and conﬂicts are worthy of investigation. It is the ﬁrst two of these
issues and, peripherally, the third which will constitute the main themes
of this book. The next section provides an overview of British colonial
forestry, the topic of the study, and identiﬁes the contexts and institutions
that will be the objects of its focus.
1.2. THE CASE OF BRITISH COLONIAL 
FORESTRY
1.2.1. Historical Overview
British colonial forestry developed over four distinct stages: the phase
prior to the mid-nineteenth century; the period 1860–1900; the ﬁrst two
decades of the twentieth century culminating in the immediate aftermath
Introduction
6
¹⁵ None of these conﬂicts is unique to environmental history. Indeed, the issue of
conﬂicts between colonial scientiﬁc communities, their governments, and local peoples
has been a major theme in the historiography of colonial science. See e.g. Roy Macleod,
‘Scientiﬁc Advice for British India’, Modern Asian Studies, 9/3 (1975), 343–84; R. Stafford,
Scientist of Empire (Cambridge, 1989); M. Harrison, Public Health in British India: Anglo-
Indian Preventive Medicine 1859–1914 (Cambridge, 1994).

--- Page 24 ---

of the First World War; and the post-First World War era, leading up to
the present day.
As historians and foresters have ceaselessly pointed out, British
colonial administrators adopted an ad hoc attitude to forest management
up until the mid nineteenth century and even later.¹⁶ In India, govern-
mental involvement in questions concerning forest use began in 1805
when the court of directors of the East India Company (EIC) sent a
dispatch enquiring whether and to what extent the deﬁciency in England
of oak for the Royal Navy could be offset by Malabar teak. This enquiry
led to the appointment of a forest committee which reported that extens-
ive deforestation had taken place and recommended the protection of
the Malabar forests on grounds that they were valuable property. The
immediate fallout of the report was a general proclamation declaring that
the royalty right in teak trees claimed by former governments was vested
in the EIC and that all further unauthorized felling of such trees was
prohibited.¹⁷
TheBritish government, however, soon began to exert pressure to stepup
the extraction of teak to augment the strength of the Royal Navy. Captain
Watson, a police ofﬁcer who ostensibly knew the local conditions, was
appointed to improve the production of teak and other timbers suitable for
shipbuilding.¹⁸ He succeeded, within a couple of years, in establishing a
timber monopoly throughout Malabar-Travancore. Between 1806 and
1823, the forests of Malabar were protected by means of this monopoly
despite the fact that nothing was done for forest regeneration. By 1823,
however, the general discontent against Watson’s socially coercive conser-
vancy methods among various interests rose to such a pitch that the then
governor of Madras, Sir Thomas Munro, abolished the post of conservator.
Rapid deforestation ensued in the years that followed. Prices of timber
roseand as early as 1831 the navy recommended the re-establishment of the
conservatorship. Despite many representations which highlighted the state
of forests, the Madras revenue board in 1838 ruled out the establishment of
an independent authority to manage forests, stating that any interference
necessary for the conservation of teak forests should be exercised only by a
revenue ofﬁcer. The only major development over the next ten years was the
creation of a few strategically important teak plantations.¹⁹
Introduction
7
¹⁶ See e.g. E. P. Stebbing, The Forests of India (London, 1922–46); T. P. Sim, The Forests
and Forest Flora of the Cape Colony (Aberdeen, 1907); R. G. Sangster, ‘Forestry’, in IFCAS
Symp.
¹⁷ B. Ribbentrop, Forestry in British India (Calcutta, 1899).
¹⁸ Ibid. 64; Stebbing, The Forests of India, i. 70.
¹⁹ Ribbentrop, Forestry in British India, 66. Also, Stebbing, The Forests of India, i. 71–2.

--- Page 25 ---

Events followed a similar course in Tenasserim, Burma. In 1826,
Dr Nathaniel Wallich, a botanist employed by the Calcutta Botanical
Garden, was deputed to examine the forest resources of the country. He
declared them to be unrivalled when compared with other forests pos-
sessed by the EIC, but suggested that they be placed under systematic and
conservative management including strict ﬁre protection. Wallich’s advice
was only partially accepted. The forests were thrown open to speculators
in 1829 but were not otherwise interfered with. Fire protection was not
introduced. By 1837, anxiety about the future of the forests began to be
felt locally. Dr Helfer, who was deputed to examine them, found a great
absence of young growth and recommended the establishment of extensive
plantations. This advice was however not acted on.²⁰
It was not until the 1840s that the government in India began to
take measures to establish forest conservancy on a permanent basis. In
Tenasserim in 1841, it was proposed that all old leases and contracts
be cancelled and new agreements containing suitable conditions for safe-
guarding the future of the forests issued. This proposal was met with
considerable opposition but eventually accepted. In 1847, a small forest
department was organized.²¹ In the same year, the government of Bombay
appointed Dr Alexander Gibson, a medical doctor, as the conservator of
forests. Madras followed suit nine years later, appointing another doctor,
Hugh Cleghorn, in the same capacity.²²
Meanwhile, in the Cape Colony in Southern Africa, forest conserva-
tion began to emerge in the British colonial discourse in about 1811, when
the Royal Navy decided that the Cape woods were not good enough for its
needs. This decision led to some sections of the government beginning
to debate the issue of the state of the forests. In 1846, an ordinance was
promulgated for ‘the better preservation of the Cape Flats and Downs’.²³
In 1854 a commission was appointed by the Cape Legislative Assembly
to examine the forest issue. It condemned the structure of existing prop-
erty rights and timber concessions. The Assembly did not however accept
the recommendations of the commission in full, opting partially to retain
private property rights and to encourage competition between govern-
ment and private interests.²⁴ By mid-century, haphazard exploitation of
forests had resulted in the destruction of large areas of timber. The state
of pastures and scrubland surrounding Cape Town increasingly became
Introduction
8
²⁰ Ribbentrop, Forestry in British India, 70.
²¹ Ibid. 70–5.
²² Stebbing, The Forests of India, i. 205.
²³ H. B. Darnell, The Past, Present and Future Condition of the Forests of the George,
Knysna and Uitenhage Districts (Cape Town, 1866), 21.
²⁴ Ibid.

--- Page 26 ---

asubject of serious public concern. In 1856, conservancies were established
in some forest regions. In 1859, the Forest and Herbage Act was passed,
which was in many ways the foundation upon which future policy and
action would be based. Between 1860 and the late 1870s a number of
measures of forest management were undertaken. None of these however
became permanent.²⁵
The broad course of events followed a similar trajectory in Australia,
where the effects of deforestation began to be felt as early as 1803. Over
the next ﬁfty years some regulations on cutting were introduced by the
government. These regulations were promulgated in order to ensure
supplies of forest products for administration and revenue generation.
The general governmental attitude, however, was that as long as a licensee
complied with governmental regulations on the size and description of
trees to be cut and the terms and conditions for the cutting and removing
of timber, he could ‘do what he wished to do with the forest. He felled
what trees he liked, removed what pleased him and left on the ground to
rot what did not suit him.’²⁶ This early attempt to cash in on Australian
timber supplies predictably led to extensive deforestation. By 1820, timber
users faced some restrictions on the quantity and quality of timber they
could cut. All such actions were however ad hoc and poorly enforced.
The story was not much different in New Zealand. From the early days
of European settlement (the 1840s), there was an attempt to clear forests
rapidly to provide land for agricultural development. A predominant
perception was of the inexhaustibility of timber supplies. It was not until
the 1870s, by which time an acute timber crisis had set in, that any serious
attempts were made to assess the state of the forests.²⁷
The course of events was no different in Kenya and Uganda in eastern
Africa or in Gambia, Sierra Leone, Liberia, Ivory Coast, Gold Coast,
Togo, and Nigeria in western Africa.²⁸The reason for the ad hoc attitude
to forests among colonial ofﬁcials and governments in the ﬁrst half of
the nineteenth century was that forest products, in their view, could be
converted into money only under exceptional conditions. Trade in forest
products in this period was in its infancy and, as a result of poorly developed
markets, forests did not have economic value. There was also a widespread
belief that the colonial forest resources were inexhaustible. Moreover,
colonial administrators did not appreciate the protective importance of
Introduction
9
²⁵ Sim, The Forests and Forest Flora of the Cape Colony, 76–91.
²⁶ L. T. Carron, A History of Forestry in Australia (Canberra, 1985), 4.
²⁷ A. L. Poole, Forestry in New Zealand: The Shaping of Policy (Auckland, 1969), 7–8.
²⁸ Sangster, ‘Forestry’, 69–70.

--- Page 27 ---

forests and especially their inﬂuence on climate. These beliefs stemmed in
part from three factors: Britain’s naval supremacy, which allowed it readily
to supply its needs by importations, the abundance of coal, which supplied
most of its energy needs, and its situation within the inﬂuence of the Gulf
Stream, which regulated the climate and prevented the physical inﬂuence
of deforestation from being felt.²⁹
Against this backdrop, the general attitude to forests among colonial
ofﬁcials and governments was to view them either as timber mines or as
sources of other ‘minor’ forest products. There was also a widespread belief,
stemming from physiocratic thinking, that forests were an obstruction to
agriculture and therefore a limiting factor to increasing revenue. Forest
lands were thus seen as being ideal for conversion into agricultural zones
and remunerative cash-crop plantations.³⁰ In many parts of the empire,
efforts were consequently made to sell all the forests outright except for a
few that were reserved for the production of timber for public works. Many
early land settlements transferred large forest areas to landowners or cultiv-
ators who, like the government, did not realize their potential value. Even
where forests were not handed over to agriculturists, the rights of the forest
user were often abandoned in favour of the cultivator.
The ﬁrst tentative steps towards systematic forest management in the
empire were taken amidst this broad attitudinal and policy climate. The
initiatives were launched in the ﬁrst half of the nineteenth century by
botanists and doctors in government employment. These colonial scient-
ists promoted their goal of systematic forest management by exploiting
governmental anxieties concerning the agricultural implications of local
environmental change such as siltation and soil erosion. More often than
not, however, governmental interest did not outlive the immediate crisis.³¹
The history of British colonial forestry, however, took a decisive turn in
the post-1860 period, the second of the four phases in the development
of British colonial forestry. Following the revolt of 1857, the government
of India sought to pursue active interventionist policies aimed at economic
and social development. State support of forestry, with an emphasis on
ensuring permanent supply of forest products, was one such policy.³²
This was part of a wider experimental climate within the Indian govern-
ment in which it was believed that centralized science policy would
Introduction
10
²⁹ B. E. Fernow, A Brief History of Forestry in Europe, the United States and Other
Countries (Toronto, 1913), 366.
³⁰ C. Dawkins, ‘Sylviculture in the Tropical Rain Forest: An Historical Analysis of
Success and Failure’ (unpublished MS, Oxford, 1992), 3.
³¹ This point has been argued convincingly by Richard Grove in chapter 5 of Green
Imperialism.
³² See e.g. Stebbing, The Forests of India, i. 62–3.

--- Page 28 ---

yield rich economic dividends. Science was consequently embraced as
a responsibility of the state.³³ Experts were deployed as ‘scientiﬁc soldiers’
and new agencies established.³⁴
Governmental support for scientiﬁc forestry began in a modest way
with the appointment of some doctors and botanists to the posts of con-
servators of forests in various provinces of India. These men of science
were charged with three main objectives: to satisfy the complaints and
demands of the lessees of the forests; to assure the government the provision
of its full timber demands for the dockyards, gun-carriage factories, and
public works; and to restrict practices such as shifting cultivation.³⁵ Soon,
forest departments were established in several provinces. Signiﬁcantly,
these departments were led initially by German forestry experts. A cadre
of foresters trained in leading German and French forestry schools was
subsequently created as an imperial service.³⁶ A professional forestry
community thereby emerged in the British Empire. A permanent infra-
structure for forestry was soon established and continental European
systems of forest management introduced.
Around the turn of the century, governments in other parts of the
empire followed the Indian example and established forest departments.
These developments coincided with the emergence in the Colonial
Ofﬁce of a strategy that envisaged the deployment of science for the pur-
suit of imperial ends. Ofﬁcials around the turn of the century were thus
increasingly convinced that:
the quarter of the globe which was coloured red would be kept together ‘in part
by concession, in part by force, and in part by the constant intervention of new
scientiﬁc forces’ to deal with the growing difﬁculties of imperial rule.³⁷
In this new policy environment, colonial governments began to show
active interest in hiring scientiﬁc specialists to advise on scientiﬁc matters.
For forestry, such governmental interest in science and scientiﬁc expert-
ise often meant hiring Indian forestry professionals to set up forestry
agencies in different parts of the empire. As early as 1876, a forest depart-
ment was set up in New Zealand by Campbell Walker of the Indian
forestry service. Four years later, another Indian forester, R. Thompson,
set up the forest department in Mauritius. In 1882, the governments of
Ceylon, Cyprus, and the Cape Colony requested the services of the Indian

11
³³ Macleod, ‘Scientiﬁc Advice for British India’, 347.
³⁴ Ibid. 348.
³⁵ Stebbing, The Forests of India, i. 219–20.
³⁶ Ribbentrop, Forestry in British India, 76–96.
³⁷ Macleod, ‘Scientiﬁc Advice for British India’, 349.

--- Page 29 ---

forest experts, and forest departments were established in these countries
by F. d’A. Vincent, E. Dobbs, and D. E. Hutchins respectively.³⁸
This trend continued after the turn of the century with the founding of
the Nigerian forest department by H. M. Thompson of the Indian forest
service in 1903 and, at his instigation, of the Gold Coast forest department
in 1909. Sierra Leone followed in 1911. In East Africa, Kenya’s forest
department was created in 1902 and Uganda’s in 1917, both by profes-
sionals from the Indian forest service.³⁹ In 1900, the Straits Settlements
established a forest department under an experienced Indian forest ofﬁcer
and the Federated Malay States began to reserve forest areas for manage-
ment following a report by the Indian inspector-general. Indian foresters
also played an important part in the establishment of forest departments in
Southern Africa and Australia.⁴⁰In Canada, forest departments began to be
established in the various provinces during the last two decades of the nine-
teenth century under the inﬂuence of the forestry movement of the United
States which in turn had been inﬂuenced by German and Indian forestry.⁴¹
By the end of the nineteenth century a large percentage of forested land in
almost every part of the empire was under the management of foresters
originating in the Indian forest department, members of which had in turn
been trained strictly within the continental tradition of forestry. The empire
was thus united by a deﬁnite system of ecological praxis.
The various governmental initiatives in establishing forestry in the
empire did not necessarily result in foresters or their institutions being
provided with adequate resources to undertake their proposed activities.
As an ex-colonial forester put it, these departments were ‘always at the far
end of the queue for governmental funds’.⁴² Moreover until the First
World War, British colonial forestry was composed of a number of forest
departments in different parts of the empire, working ‘quietly and in
complete isolation’ from each other.⁴³ Information exchange among
foresters, to the extent it existed, took place through either personal con-
tacts or journals such as the Indian Forester. No serious attempt was made
to co-ordinate the work of these remote forestry agencies. There was no
central institution for empire forestry and no co-ordinated empire-wide
Introduction
12
³⁸ D. Brandis, ‘Progress of Forestry in India’, Transactions of the Scottish Arboricultural
Society, 10/3 (1884), 25.
³⁹ Sangster, ‘Forestry’, 70.
⁴⁰ Fernow, A Brief History of Forestry, 438–9.
⁴¹ See e.g. C. Leavitt, ‘The Progress of Forestry in Canada’, Canadian Forestry Journal,
16/3 (1920); H. R. T. MacMillan, The Profession and Practice of Forestry in Canada,
1907–1957 (Ontario, 1958); R. P. Gillis and Thomas Roach, ‘The American Inﬂuence on
Conservation in Canada: 1899–1911’, Journal of Forest History, 30/4 (1986).
⁴² Sangster, ‘Forestry’, 70.
⁴³ Ibid.

--- Page 30 ---

forestry agenda even though timber and forest products were by then
important components of trade within the empire.
The setting up of forest departments in different parts of the empire
however resulted in the creation of a homogeneous and assertive pan-
colonial community of foresters. Having been trained in a few select
schools in Germany and France, this community shared a common intel-
lectual tradition. It therefore had a clear and speciﬁc vision of how to
manage forests. Moreover, it ﬁrmly believed that the continental approach
was the best method of forest management. It was also convinced that
strict forest management along continental lines was strategically an
imperial necessity. During much of the period between the turn of the
century and the First World War (the third phase in the development of
British colonial forestry), colonial foresters, who had begun to network
amongst themselves by then, systematically lobbied governments in the
empire and in Britain to adopt scientiﬁc forestry and declare a permanent
forest policy.⁴⁴
It took the First World War for governments in Britain and the empire
to take these foresters seriously. The timber shortage and lack of substi-
tutes experienced during the war dramatically demonstrated the need to
co-ordinate and develop global agendas for forestry education, research,
and utilization. It also pressed home the utility of institutionalizing
frameworks for exchanging information and perspectives on forest
management on an international scale. With governments showing
increasing awareness of an impending timber crisis, foresters began to be
taken seriously and their opinions consciously solicited. Empire foresters
began to lobby governments, especially the Colonial Ofﬁce, to support
their agenda for the development of imperial forestry in the twentieth
century.⁴⁵
An important consequence was the organization, starting in 1920, of
periodic Empire Forestry Conferences. These conferences brought together
senior foresters, civil servants, and politicians interested in forestry from
across the empire. The ensuing pan-colonial debate enabled foresters from
all parts of the empire to compare notes, co-ordinate goals, and devise
agendas for lobbying governments on forest policy, using their credibility as
experts. The conferences resulted, for the ﬁrst time, in the co-ordination,
planned development, and, to an extent, centralized control of empire
forestry as a whole.
Introduction
13
⁴⁴ See N. D. G. James, A History of English Forestry (Oxford, 1981), especially chapters
9 and 10.
⁴⁵ See Jefferies, A Review of Colonial Research; Worthington, Science in Africa.

--- Page 31 ---

With the conferences serving as an informal co-ordinating agency,
a massive international institutional framework for the management of
colonial forests was built. By 1960, there were, in addition to the various
scientiﬁc establishments in various parts of the Commonwealth, seven such
agencies in Britain and the international arena.⁴⁶The period after the First
World War thus saw the emergence of a complex web of forestry agencies
located in the metropolis and the periphery. With formal decolonization,
these institutions changed nominally, with the word ‘Commonwealth’
often substituting for ‘Empire’—but continue to be inﬂuential to this day.
1.2.2. Historiographical Overview
British Empire forest history has evolved greatly since its early days in the
late nineteenth century when articles in the Indian Forester and books by
foresters celebrated the onset of forestry in the British Empire with
detailed chronologies of events. Colonial forest historiography has, in
recent times, concentrated on the British colonial periphery in the nine-
teenth and twentieth centuries and engaged in a number of issues and
debates of which two are especially relevant to the story you are about
to read.⁴⁷
The ﬁrst broad debate relevant to this book grapples with the reasons
and causes for the rise and spread of colonial forestry. There are, on the one
hand, some scholars who claim that it arose out of imperial revenue and
strategic needs, especially the growing timber demands of an expanding
empire.⁴⁸ As opposed to this, the aforementioned Richard Grove has
sought to locate the origins of the colonial interest in forest conserva-
tion in the ‘evangelist discourses’ produced by botanists and doctors who,
on the evidence of the impact of deforestation on climatic change in
fragile environments, made a forceful case for conservation.
A second big historiographical debate informing this book concerns
the ideologies of British colonial forestry and the impact of colonial forest
policy on nature and societies. An important thrust in the literature has
been to analyse historically the ecological changes resulting from the
Introduction
14
⁴⁶ They were the Empire Commonwealth Conferences, and the various institutions
that were established on its recommendation, especially the Imperial Forestry Institute, the
Commonwealth Forestry Bureau, the Forest Products Research Laboratory, the Tropical
Products Institute, the Timber Development Association, and the British Paper and Board
Industry Research Association.
⁴⁷ The term ‘colonial forest history’ is used to describe the work by historians, anthro-
pologists, sociologists, and foresters who have undertaken historical studies of colonial
forestry.
⁴⁸ See e.g. Guha, The Unquiet Woods.

--- Page 32 ---

introduction of scientiﬁc forestry in speciﬁc regions of India and Africa.⁴⁹
Another approach has been to explore the social, economic, and political
impact of this introduction and examine the implicit systems of resource
extraction and resource use priorities. A related genre has focused on
understanding the nature of the consequent conﬂicts between colonial
systems and traditional frameworks of forest resource use and exploring
whether the introduction of European methods resulted in an ecological
watershed. A third broad trend has been in examining the methods and
idioms of resistance and the attempts by colonial states at co-opting and
coercing popular protest.⁵⁰ A particular emphasis has been on detailing
the essential features of the ‘classic’ or colonial approaches to natural
resource management and conservation and explicating the ideologies
associated with imperial resource management efforts.⁵¹
In describing various facets of the human and ecological impact of
forest management in the British Empire, historians have thereby focused
on advancing state power, the market imperative, aspirations by states to
control and discipline, contests between the state and local communities,
and colonial attitudes to indigenous populations. This focus has provided
a useful and important picture of colonial environmental policies and
their impacts.
The historiography of British colonial forestry, however, reﬂects the
gaps in colonial environmental history alluded to at the outset. One
major absence is the general neglect of scientiﬁc communities, scientiﬁc

--- Page 33 ---

institutions, and the discipline of forestry. Although the literature tells us
a great deal about various facets of colonial forestry, the discussion sub-
sumes the science of forestry within wider perspectives such as imperial
interests. An important and pioneering book on colonial forestry in India,
for example, devotes no more than three pages to scientiﬁc forestry, and
that too with only a ﬂeeting reference to agendas stemming from science.⁵²
Similarly, a string of recent monographs on colonial forest policy make no
distinction between foresters and others in the colonial administration:
all ofﬁcials are treated as representing one predominant interest, that of
the state.⁵³The generality of the category state forestry adopted in this body
of work thereby conceals the differences, in motivations and perspectives,
between scientiﬁc communities such as foresters, whose cultural back-
grounds stem from the traditions of forestry developed in a European
setting, and other state ofﬁcials. These differences are, however, arguably
as important to an understanding of the emergence of colonial agendas of
forest management as are factors such as state expansion and the market
imperative.
Related to this is another important gap in the literature on British
colonial forestry. The idea that the science of forestry could bring with it
a historical baggage of agendas stemming from its European past is
not adequately acknowledged in the existing work. There has thus been
no attempt to study the continental European prehistory of imperial
forestry and the question of whether and how this background has inﬂu-
enced its development in the colonial setting. The work on the forest
history of the British Empire in the ﬁrst half of the nineteenth century, for
example, does not explore the intellectual links between the colonial
campaign for forest conservation and the scientiﬁc work on deforestation
and efﬁcient forest use among European scientiﬁc communities.⁵⁴ It fails,
further, to examine the relationships between this campaign and the
agendas of colonial science during this period, more widely.⁵⁵ Similarly,
Introduction

⁵² Guha, The Unquiet Woods.

and K. Sivaramakrishnan, Modern Forests: Statemaking and Environmental Change in
Colonial Eastern India (Stanford, Calif., 1999).
⁵⁴ See e.g. C. Glacken, ‘Changing Ideas of the Habitable World’, in W. J. Thomas (ed.),

--- Page 34 ---

the literature on the second half of the nineteenth century does not locate the
development of colonial forestry during the period against the backdrop
of what is known about the history of continental forestry.⁵⁶ An import-
ant consequence of this neglect is that historians of British colonial
forestry have concluded that empire forestry was distinctively ‘colonial’.
In the absence of a wider comparative analysis taking into account contin-
ental prehistories, such a claim is, however, difﬁcult to sustain.
A third important gap in the existing body of historical work on British
colonial forestry is that in concentrating on examining the development
of forestry in particular regions of the British Empire, none of these
historiographical approaches has attempted to study imperial forestry as
a pan-colonial phenomenon. While there are thus a number of studies on
speciﬁc periods and contexts, there has as yet been no attempt to tran-
scend this limited focus and gain a wider perspective on imperial forestry.
1.3. THE FRAMEWORK OF THE BOOK
The objective of this book is to obtain a panoramic view of the agendas
and ideologies of the science of forestry across the empire in each of the
four stages of British colonial forestry. It seeks to get a wide-angled snap-
shot of the development of empire forestry that transcends the narrow
spatial and temporal foci of the existing genre of regional case studies,
while simultaneously concentrating on examining forestry from the per-
spective of the objectives of the scientiﬁc community and discipline.
The book attempts, ﬁrst of all, to revisit the nineteenth century and
look at the agendas and ideologies of empire forestry science in each of its
two stages. An important objective is to examine the question of the extent
to which European scientiﬁc attitudes to forest use and frameworks of
forest management affected the development of forestry in the British
Empire. Put differently, was British colonial forestry, as a science of
Introduction
17
⁵⁶ See e.g. Fernow, A Brief History of Forestry; F. Heske, German Forestry (New Haven,
1938); K. Mantel, ‘History of the International Science of Forestry with Special
Consideration of Central Europe: Literature, Training, and Research from the Earliest
Beginnings to the Nineteenth Century’, in J. A. Romberger and P. Mikola (eds.),
International Review of Forestry Research, i. (New York, 1964); H. E. Lowood, ‘The
Calculating Forester: Quantiﬁcation, Cameral Science, and the Emergence of Scientiﬁc
Forestry Management in Germany’, in T. Frängsmyr, J. L. Heilbron, and R. E. Rider (eds.),
The Quantifying Spirit in the Eighteenth Century (Berkeley and Los Angeles, 1990);
S. Pincetl, ‘Some Origins of French Environmentalism: An Exploration’, in Forest and
Conservation History, 37 (Apr. 1993).

--- Page 35 ---

resource use, ‘colonial’, or was it simply an instance of a European system
of environmental management in a colonial context? Such a focus—on
science, on the one hand, and on the inﬂuence of European environmental
traditions on the other—will help address the important gaps in the
historiography of British colonial forestry in the nineteenth century ident-
iﬁed in the previous section.
The book seeks further to undertake an examination of the development
of the agendas of British colonial forestry in the twentieth century. In keep-
ing with its broad goals, its basic objective is to look at empire forestry as a
science and as a pan-colonial phenomenon. It therefore focuses on what
may be described as the ‘parliament of imperial forestry’ in this period—the
Empire Forestry Conferences.⁵⁷ The conferences, as argued earlier, were
looked upon by the empire forestry community as the single most import-
ant forum for co-ordinating an empire-wide forest policy. They also brought
together the most senior foresters from across the empire on a regular basis.
An examination of the debates and the inﬂuence of these conferences
therefore enables a focused and yet a panoramic, pan-colonial picture of the
agendas and ideologies of imperial forestry in the twentieth century. The
signiﬁcance of the conferences as a ‘centre of calculation’ in the twentieth
century makes such a study important in its own right.⁵⁸
In examining these contexts and institutions, the book focuses primarily
on the themes identiﬁed earlier in this chapter. At the outset, it is concerned
with the agendas and priorities of resource management implicit in colonial
forest science. In addition to documenting these agendas, it seeks to ident-
ify the problems and dilemmas faced by colonial foresters and their dif-
ferences in perspective on speciﬁc issues. The book aims also to identify
the resource management priorities common to the empire (and com-
monwealth) as a whole and to detail the social, political, and economic
considerations cited by the main actors in these institutions in articulating
these agendas. An important issue underlying both these questions is the
extent to which European frameworks of environmental management
affected British colonial forestry and its successor regimes.
The book also engages the question of the meaning of the agendas of
the scientiﬁc communities engaged in colonial and post-colonial forestry.
This includes their attitudes to nature and resource, and to indigenous
peoples, and their beliefs, technologies, and practices concerning resource
Introduction
18
⁵⁷ I am indebted to Dr John Darwin for the idea that the Empire Forestry Conferences
can be looked on as the parliament of colonial foresters.
⁵⁸ The term ‘centre of calculation’ is used in the sense in which Bruno Latour used it in
Science in Action: How to Follow Scientists and Engineers through Society (Cambridge, 1987).

--- Page 36 ---

use. It also includes the manner in which the formation and justiﬁcation of
these attitudes were affected by perspectives on colonial and post-colonial
development and exploitation, and, more widely, on the relationship
between science, technology and human progress. Peripherally, the book
grapples with the issue of the tensions between colonial foresters and
other sections of state administrations and society, and the distinctiveness
of scientiﬁc traditions in colonial environmental management.
The scope of the book is delimited by concentrating throughout on the
ideas and intellectual paradigms of natural resource management implicit
in forestry science. It is primarily a context-bound intellectual history
that seeks to understand how foresters, as a scientiﬁc community with a
distinct cognitive tradition, generally approached resource management.
The book, however, does not attempt to examine the changing econom-
ics of timber production in different areas in different times or the impact
of economic forces on the development of forest policy in particular
regions. It does not attempt further to compare or contrast the subtle dif-
ferences in forest practice from one region to another. Moreover, the book
does not attempt to examine the inﬂuence foresters have had within their
colonial and national contexts and the extent to which they have been
able to implement their objectives in particular operative conditions. It
does however focus speciﬁcally on what has been common to the empire
(and commonwealth) forestry community as a whole in terms of their
broad paradigmatic and disciplinary approach to forest use and manage-
ment. The purpose of the exercise is to arm scholars working on narrower
spatial and regional contexts with an awareness of the world view of the
forestry community on natural resource management, particularly as
stemming from its disciplinary ethos.
1.3.1. The Outline of the Chapters
As mentioned earlier, the book has two main parts, respectively on the
nineteenth and the twentieth centuries. The purpose of the ﬁrst part is to
undertake a revisionist historical examination of the two epochs in British
colonial forestry in this period, with a focus on science and against a back-
drop of European environmental traditions. Chapter 2 sets the stage by
providing an overarching account of the emergence of the idea of forest
conservation in continental Europe from the mid eighteenth to the mid
nineteenth centuries. In doing so, it attempts creatively to synthesize a
wide range of printed primary and secondary sources that have hitherto
been largely ignored by scholars.
Introduction
19

--- Page 37 ---

Chapter 3 describes the growth of the idea of forest conservancy in the
British Empire throughout the nineteenth century. It begins by examin-
ing the period 1800–60, when colonial scientists in India, and to an
extent the Cape, began to lobby their respective governments to establish
permanent forest conservancy. The primary method in this section of the
chapter is prosopography. By compiling a collective biography of the key
scientists involved in the campaign in India and the Cape, as identiﬁed
from historical accounts and publications, it builds a picture of their
intellectual networks and thereby explores their motivations and agendas.
The second half of the chapter concentrates on India in the second half of
the nineteenth century and examines the agendas of the Indian forestry
community, culminating in the publication of Schlich’s Manual of Forestry.
A central concern throughout the chapter is to determine whether and to
what extent the European forestry tradition inﬂuenced the formation of
agendas in the British Empire.
The second part of the book uses content analysis to examine the
Empire Forestry Conferences that were held regularly in the aftermath of
the First World War and, thereby, the development of British Empire
forestry as a pan-colonial phenomenon in the twentieth century. The pur-
pose of Chapter 4 is twofold. First, it seeks to understand what the origins
of the Empire Forestry Conferences were and in particular what the role
of the imperial forestry community, and its allies in the United Kingdom,
was in bringing the conferences about. Secondly, it seeks to examine the
agendas of the ﬁrst two conferences, as they pertain to forest policy.
Chapter 5, in turn, concentrates on the attitudes to natural resource
management implicit in the discussions at the conferences, with particular
reference to two perpetually vexing problems—soil erosion and shifting
cultivation. Finally, Chapter 6 summarizes the key ﬁndings of the entire
study and explores some of its wider implications concerning the contested
legacy of colonial eco-development.
Introduction
20

--- Page 38 ---

¹ W. Schlich, A Manual of Forestry, i (London, 1889), 13.
² For more on Schlich, read Chapter 3.
2
A Contract with Nature
In his classic textbook, A Manual of Forestry, William Schlich, the former
head of the Indian forestry service, wrote:
Forests are, in the economy of man and nature, of direct and indirect value; the
former chieﬂy through their produce, and the latter through the inﬂuence which
they exercise upon climate, the regulation of moisture, the stability of the soil, the
healthiness of a country and allied subjects.¹
Schlich was formally educated in the continental European forestry
tradition and as his textbooks on forestry indicate, remained a strong
adherent.² As the chief forestry instructor at the Cooper’s Hill and Oxford
forestry schools, he was also responsible for training the cadres who effect-
ively staffed the forestry departments of the British Empire from the late
nineteenth into the early twentieth century. Any attempt at understanding
the mindset of the leaders and practitioners of colonial forestry during this
period must therefore begin by grasping the world view underlying
Schlich’s theoretical paradigm. The purpose of this chapter is to do just
that. It has two broad sections. The ﬁrst traces the emergence of the idea
that forests offered physical, or indirect climatic beneﬁts. The second
explores the evolution of the idea of direct utility, with particular refer-
ence to some of the traditions of political economy and state making
co-terminous with the rise of scientiﬁc forestry in Europe.
2.1. THE CLIMATIC BENEFITS OF FORESTS
An enduring dogma in environmentalist thought is the idea that modernity
brought with it a domineering or imperial attitude toward the natural
world. According to one fairly widespread narrative, the notion of human
beings as controllers of nature crystallized in Europe between the ﬁfteenth

--- Page 39 ---

and the seventeenth centuries.³ It was ostensibly born out of a marriage
between the religious concept that the human species had dominion over
the earth, and the idea that, with tools and knowledge, they were improv-
ing the earth as much as they were improving themselves.⁴ The narrative
proceeds to make the claim that such attitudes ultimately set the stage
for the twentieth-century environmental crisis. Besides making the argu-
ment that can be caricatured by the phrase, ‘modernity begat the death of
nature,’ the above narrative also draws up a genealogy of philosophies of
care and respect for the earth and its creatures, variously described as
‘Arcadian’ or ‘feminine’. In effect, the argument goes, this counter-trend
constituted the very antithesis of the ‘imperial’ mindset and, in doing so,
provided the basis of the critique of industrial society.
Such narratives, however, fail to acknowledge two important facts.
First, there was considerable acknowledgement, from as early as the
thirteenth century, if not earlier, of the environmental effects of defor-
estation. The evidence is enshrined in local laws: between 1535 and 1777,
322 forests were proclaimed protected by ofﬁcial ordinances.⁵ Secondly,
the writings of George Perkins Marsh, John Croumbie Brown, Franklin
Benjamin Hough, Élisée Reclus, and several other luminaries of late
nineteenth-century eco-evangelism make the case for the argument that
from the early modern period onward, there had begun a systematic body
of scientiﬁc observations and research programmes that culminated,
by the mid nineteenth century, in the recognition that human beings are
geological agents, capable of making undesirable and, from the stand-
point of human societies, catastrophic changes in nature.⁶
A Contract with Nature

Philosopher of Modern Science: A Non-Western View’, in Nandy (ed.), Science, Hegemony
and Violence; Shiva, Staying Alive; Henryk Skolimowski, Eco-Philosophy (New York, 1981).
⁴ Glacken, Traces on the Rhodesian Shore, 495.
⁵ Joseph Kittredge, Forest Inﬂuences: The Effects of Woody Vegetation on Climate, Water
and Soil (New York, 1948), 6.
⁶ G. P. Marsh, Man and Nature (Madras, 1882); É. Reclus, The Ocean, Atmosphere, and
Life: Being the Second Series of a Descriptive History of the Life of the Globe (New York, 1873);
J. C. Brown, Forests and Moisture: Or the Effects of Forests on Humidity of Climate (Edinburgh,
1877); Franklin Benjamin Hough, Report on Forestry (Washington, DC, 1878–80). I owe
my awareness of this body of work primarily to the writings of Clarence Glacken, on which
I draw considerably in this chapter. By carefully tracing the footnotes of these authors,
I came to the realization that these books were, in effect, elaborate literature surveys and
thereby captured the essence of the scientiﬁc work on the environmental consequence of
various anthropogenic activities that had been conducted from the mid 18th to the mid
19th centuries. The argument in this section is but a brief exposition of some of my
ﬁndings. A longer paper will follow in due course.

--- Page 40 ---

The roots of such scientiﬁc work can arguably be traced at least to
the seventeenth century. One prominent example is John Evelyn’s book
Sylvia, published in 1664, which argued, among other things, that grazing,
glass-making, iron smelting, and naval building posed great threats to
forests.⁷ Another equally cited example is the forest ordinance of Jean
Baptiste Colbert in France in 1669, which was similarly sparked by
concerns with the consequences of deforestation.⁸ In addition to worries
about economic costs, Evelyn and Colbert were also aware of the wider
environmental effects of deforestation, especially its impact on regional
climates.⁹
The tradition of scientiﬁc concern with the effects of deforestation
continued and developed further during the eighteenth century. In 1764,
a German scientiﬁc article claimed that there was a causal connection
between forests and precipitation.¹⁰ As the century progressed, a number
of scientists and explorers, including Fernando Colon, George Louis
Leclerc, Comte de Buffon, and F. A. L. von Burgsdorf, were actively
involved in investigating the consequences of forest clearance and defor-
estation.¹¹The French naturalist Buffon was in many respects illustrative
of this cohort of proto-environmentalists. He contrasted the appearance
of uninhabited with inhabited lands and argued that in building habita-
tions human beings destroyed woods, drained lakes and marshes, made
mountains bare, and soils less fertile. Buffon claimed further that defor-
estation led to climatic change and to the heating up of the earth; that
lands with forests were cold; and that it was more difﬁcult to cool than
to heat the earth. In making his case, Buffon cited many examples of
A Contract with Nature
23

Instauration: Science, Medicine and Reform, 1626–1660 (London, 1975), 465–88, 495.
⁸ Glacken, ‘Changing Ideas of the Habitable World’, 74–5.
⁹ Glacken, Traces on the Rhodian Shore, 487. It is important to note that Evelyn and
Colbert were not outliers in making such arguments in the 17th century. Other, equally
inﬂuential advocates included Ralph Austen, the author of Treatise of Fruit Trees (1653)
and The Spiritual Use of an Orchard (1657). Moreover, there were many in Europe writing
letters to governments and ofﬁcials demanding urgent action aimed at forest conservation.
For a particularly impressive letter dating back to 1608 in Venice, see Kittredge, Forest
Inﬂuences, 6–7. On the importance of early modern Venice as a precursor to modern envir-
onmental policy sensibilities, see Karl Apuhn, ‘Inventing Nature: Forests, Forestry, and
State Power in Renaissance Venice’, Journal of Modern History, 72 (Dec. 2000).
¹⁰ Heske, German Forestry, 173.
¹¹ Ibid. 174. They were inﬂuenced in part by the writings of the ancients, especially of
Theophrastus and Pliny. They were also inﬂuenced by the growing interest in climate in
Europe. For an account of the impact of the climatic theories among 18th-century
European scientiﬁc communities, see the discussion on the inﬂuence of John Arbuthnot
and Montesquieu in Glacken, Traces on the Rhodian Shore, 551–622.

--- Page 41 ---

these effects from travel accounts from Quebec, Cayenne, and the
Guineas.¹²
By the late eighteenth century, research on the environmental impacts
of deforestation had become an established domain for scientiﬁc enquiry.
Scholars focused particularly on its effects on rivers and streams. For
example, von Burgsdorf, speaking before the German Academy of Sciences
in 1790, argued that forests were responsible for conserving water.¹³
Subsequent studies further deepened the understanding of the environ-
mental importance of forests and, consequently, of the realization that
environmental destruction adversely impacted political economy.
Perhaps nothing is more illustrative of this than the case of the torrents
of the French and Austrian Alps and the work of Jean Antoine Fabre, the
chief engineer of bridges and highways in the Département du Var in
France. Based on his observations in Var, Basses-Alpes, and Bouches-
du-Rhône, and his studies of the courses of the Rhône and Durance,
Fabre pronounced in 1797 that deforestation and forest clearance in the
high Alps were the two main causes of the sudden and overwhelming
Alpine torrents which ﬂooded the farms and settlements of the lowlands.¹⁴
Almost reminiscent of a modern political ecological analysis, Fabre wrote
that deforestation and forest clearance in high mountainous areas brought
about seven kinds of disaster:
The ﬁrst disaster produced by the two causes of which we have just spoken is the
ruin of our forests. The second disaster is the destruction in a great many places
of the bed of vegetable soil with which our mountains were covered. The third
disaster is the ruin of the domains which lie upon the rivers. The fourth disaster
is the drainage, experienced in the navigation of the rivers, by the divisions in the
water-courses, which are the consequence of great ﬂoods. The ﬁfth disaster
A Contract with Nature
24
¹² Glacken, ‘Changing Ideas of the Habitable World’, 76. See also Comte Buffon,

¹³ Heske, German Forestry, 173. See also F. A. L. von Burgsdorf, Forsthandbuch, 2 vols.
(Berlin, 1800).
¹⁴ J. A. Fabre, Essai sur la théorie des torrens [sic] et des rivières . . . (Paris, 1797), cited in
Glacken, Traces on the Rhodian Shore, 698. An important historiographical controversy is
brewing among Swiss and German historians over whether there was indeed a relationship
between deforestation and torrents. Some, who deny such causal links, remark that such
environmental complaints ‘served as the means for the dominant Swiss Lowlands to gain
control over the mountain forests’. See, for example, Joachim Radkau, ‘Wood and Forestry
in German History: In Quest of an Environmental Approach’, Environment and History,
2 (1996), 63–76 (68–9). Without getting embroiled in this debate, it is adequate, for the
purposes of this section, to remark that there was a long and sustained scientiﬁc tradition
that did make such claims—and that this genealogy of thought eventually formed one
important strain of the emerging conservationist impulse.

--- Page 42 ---

consists in the strifes and contentions, between the proprietors on opposite banks
of the river, to which the divisions in these water-courses give rise. The sixth disaster
results from the deposits which they make at the mouths of the streams, which often
intercept the navigation. In ﬁne, the seventh disaster consists in the diminution of
the sources which feed the streams and rivers in their ordinary state.¹⁵
Fabre’s work sparked a series of French studies, pursued by foresters,
engineers, and agronomists, who were intimately associated with the
reforestation efforts undertaken by the French government in the Hautes-
Alpes during the late eighteenth and through the nineteenth centuries.
Fabre’s successor, Alexandre Surrel, who continued his studies, announced
that Alpine torrents appeared when forests disappeared and that they dis-
appeared when forests were restored.¹⁶ Similar research was conducted on
the Austrian Alps and in Italy, where scientists had been interested in
torrents, the control of watercourses, and soil erosion from the days of
Leonardo da Vinci, who had noted that streams were muddier when they
passed through populated districts.¹⁷ By the early nineteenth century,
a substantial body of literature had been produced on the relationship
between deforestation and torrents.¹⁸ It is important to note, here, that a
considerable proportion of this research was conducted in the countries
bordering the Alps, which were, in effect, the site of some of the crucial
early research on anthropogenic environmental change.¹⁹
A Contract with Nature

--- Page 43 ---

A related set of studies connecting deforestation with agrarian
political economy involved lakes in the Alpine mountains in the late
1770s. These works posited an explicit connection between deforestation
and decline in rainfall and, consequently, in water tables. Of particular
importance was the work of Horace-Bénédict de Saussure, who measured
the water levels of Lake Neuchâtel, Lake Bienne, and Lake Morat.
Another important ﬁgure was the French chemist J. B. Boussingault, who
had studied Saussure’s materials and concluded that water levels had
lowered in modern times due to the cutting of woods.²⁰ ‘My opinion’,
wrote Boussingault, ‘is that the felling of trees over a large extent of
country has always had the effect of lessening the mean annual quantity
of rain . . . Great clearings diminish the quantity of spring water in a
country.’²¹
Another issue that sparked the interest of scientiﬁc investigators was
the relationship between forest clearance and shifting sand. During the
sixteenth and seventeenth centuries, many attempts were made in Europe
and Japan to prevent the shifting of sand dunes through decrees and ﬁx
them by planting vegetation. By the early eighteenth century, this problem
gained scientiﬁc scrutiny, and reports were published and commissions of
dunes created to execute the recommendations therefrom. Between 1850
and 1892, in France alone, 1,750,000 acres of moving sand was ﬁxed by
planting pine forests.²²
By the early nineteenth century, the European scientiﬁc environmental
tradition brimmed with the idea that clearing, grazing, transhumance,
torrents, and declining water tables were all interrelated parts of the greater
problem of deforestation. With these scientiﬁc ﬁndings providing a back-
drop, Alexander von Humboldt, around the turn of the nineteenth
century, devoted much time to studying the relation between forest clear-
ance and climatic change, using the lakes of the New World and of Central

Themes in the Shaping of Knowledge(London, 1998), 39–63; J. S. Rowlinson, ‘Our Common
Room in Geneva’ and ‘The Early Exploration of the Alps of Savoy’, Notes and Records of the
Royal Society of London, 52 (1998), 221–35; Giorgio Vittorio dal Piaz, ‘Alpine Geology and

Territorial Ambitions and the Gardens of Versailles (Cambridge, 1997).
²⁰ Glacken, ‘Changing Ideas of the Habitable World’, 78.
²¹ Boussingault, cited in Brown, Forests and Moisture, 159–60.
²² Kittredge, Forest Inﬂuences, 8–10. See also Marsh, Man and Nature.

--- Page 44 ---

Asia as examples. He wrote:
By felling trees which cover the tops and sides of mountains, men in every climate
prepare at once two calamities for future generations—the want of fuel and the
scarcity of water . . . Plants exhale ﬂuid from their leaves, in the ﬁrst place, for
their own beneﬁt. One of these is maintaining a suitable portion of humidity in
the air. Not only do they attract and condense the moisture suspended in the air,
and borne by the wind over the earth’s surface, which, by falling from their leaves,
keeps the ground below moist and cool; but they can, by means of their roots, pump
it up from a very considerable depth, and, raising it into the atmosphere, diffuse it
over the face of the country. Trees, by transpiration from their leaves, surround
themselves with an atmosphere, constantly cold and moist. They also shelter the
soil from the direct action of the sun, and thus prevent evaporation of the water
furnished by rains.²³
Both von Humboldt and Boussingault were inﬂuential in promoting
the scientiﬁc investigation of the relation between agriculture, forest
clearance, and climatic change. Boussingault argued that extensive forest
destruction diminished the amount of running water, that the diminution
might be a result of less average annual rainfall or more active evaporation
or both, and that the quantity of running water of countries having no
agricultural encroachment on forests was regular and did not seem to
change perceptibly. He argued further that forests regularized stream ﬂow
by impeding evaporation, that agriculture in a dry country which lacked
forests dissipated an additional portion of running water, and that limited
clearings of forests caused a diminution of springs.²⁴
Scientists working in Europe and America during the ﬁrst three
decades of the nineteenth century came to similar conclusions. Among
them were the German scientist Berghaus, who conducted a historical
examination of the depths of water in the Elbe and the Oder. There were
also a host of others, including A. C. Becquerel, Moreau de Jormis,
Dureau de la Malle, Dominique François Arago, Gustav Wex, and Noah
Webster.²⁵ By the early nineteenth century, there were a substantial number
of scientists working internationally on the relation between deforesta-
tion and climatic change, and especially on changes in water levels and
water ﬂows. These scientists, as the citations in their publications reveal,
clearly knew each other’s work. There was thus an international ‘invisible
A Contract with Nature
27
²³ A. von Humboldt, Aspects of Nature in Different Lands and Different Climates: With
Scientiﬁc Elucidations (Philadelphia, 1849), 232.
²⁴ Glacken, ‘Changing Ideas of the Habitable World’, 79. See also J. B. Boussingault,
Rural Economy, in its Relations with Chemistry, Physics and Meteorology: Or Chemistry Applied
to Agriculture (New York, 1845), 507.
²⁵ Brown, Forests and Moisture, 111.

--- Page 45 ---

college’ of scientists actively working on the impact of deforestation on
water ﬂows and water tables.²⁶ Moreover, they were united around a set
of questions, a good illustration of which is Becquerel’s memoir on the
climatic inﬂuences of forests, ﬁrst published in 1853, translated into
English in the annual report of the Smithsonian Institution in 1869, and
republished in 1877 in Franklin Benjamin Hough’s‘Report upon Forestry’.
Becquerel, in effect, argued that there were six main questions:²⁷
1. What is the part that forests play as a shelter against the winds or as
a means of retarding the evaporation of rainwater?
2. What inﬂuences do the forests exert through the absorption of their
roots or the evaporation of their leaves in modifying the hygro-
metrical conditions of the surrounding atmosphere?
3. How do they modify the temperatures of a country?
4. Do the forests exercise an inﬂuence upon the amount of water falling
and upon the distribution of rains through the year, as well as upon
the regulation of running waters and springs?
5. In what manner do they intervene in the protection of mountains
and slopes?
6. What is the nature of the inﬂuence that they may be able to exercise
upon public health?
In addition to research on the impact of forest clearance on water
tables, shifting sand dunes, temperature, and erosion, the nineteenth cen-
tury also saw the emergence of another scientiﬁc issue concerning forest
clearance: the effect of deforestation on vegetation. This new concern was
spearheaded by a set of botanists whom a historian of ecology has called
‘Humboldtian Plant Geographers’.²⁸ The term owes its origins to
Alexander von Humboldt’s emphasis, in the ﬁrst half of the nineteenth
century, on studies of vegetation, especially the relation between types of

interrelations between environmental and societal relations, see Franklin Thomas, The
