3
The Empire of Nature
The ﬁrst half of the nineteenth century saw a great deal of forest destruc-
tion in different parts of the British Empire. Colonial forests were
exploited extensively for timber and a host of minor forest products.
Forested lands were also increasingly converted to remunerative cash-
crop plantations of sugar, cardamom, bananas, tea, coffee, and rubber.¹
Much of the forest destruction in the British Empire in this period took
place with the tacit, if not explicit, approval of colonial governments. For
most colonial administrative ofﬁcials, forests were a vast and seemingly
limitless reservoir of resources for imperial expansion and a hindrance to
agricultural development. As the following lament made by a leading
proponent of forestry in the nineteenth century illustrates, there was little
recognition of the importance of forest conservation:
In some of our colonies, extensive forests are treated as beds of onions, leeks,
cabbages and turnips in the kitchen garden. Trees deemed suitable for some pur-
pose desired have been felled, others around them have been left standing, or have
been cut down to allow of the felled timber being brought out; and the results
have been scarcely less destructive than the forest clearings in the Western world.²
By the end of the nineteenth century, however, colonial governmental
attitudes to forests had, in principle, if not in actual practice, undergone a
sea change. Governments throughout the empire began to set up forest
departments staffed by personnel trained in forestry schools in Germany
and France. Where there had once been state-sponsored forest destruction,
there were now extensive state-sponsored regimes of scientiﬁc resource
management. The purpose of this chapter is to explore the role played by
expatriate scientists in bringing about this change in colonial governmental
approaches to forest use and the transformation of attitudes to forestry and
land use brought about by the import of European techniques.

--- Page 73 ---

The earliest attempt at grappling with these questions was made by
colonial forester-historians who at the beginning of the twentieth century
began to write histories of colonial forestry celebrating the great march of
their discipline and profession.³ Their explanation of the change in colo-
nial attitudes to forests marked the beginning of a myth of origin for
empire foresters which, with constant repetition, has now assumed the
status of a truism amongst the international forestry community. A mod-
ern version of this myth captures its central argument succinctly:
In many parts of the world a sequential pattern of use of the forest resource can be
demonstrated. Initially, the forest is seen as an almost unlimited resource, with little
danger of exhaustion and little need of conservation. Trends in forest use and area are
perceived in neutral or positive terms: a reduction in forest area may be welcomed in
allowing an expansion of the agricultural area. As this phase of resource destruction
proceeds, some voices begin to call for conservation. Initially they are largely ignored,
but from the faint stirrings a clamour may arise and may eventually lead to legisla-
tion or other government action aimed at halting the trend. The effectiveness of
these voices has been variable. In some instances, forest destruction was halted while
signiﬁcant forest areas still survived. In others it continued to a point where the for-
est resource was all but exhausted. In some instances, near-exhaustion was followed
by attempts at the re-creation of the resource, and in a few of these cases, the expan-
sion of the forest area has recently attracted adverse reaction.⁴
Most colonial forester-historians explained the origins of forestry in
the British Empire in similar terms. The onset of empire forestry in their
account could be dated to a single event, the establishment in India in the
mid nineteenth century of departments staffed by ofﬁcers trained in
Germany and France. The setting up of such forestry services was in their
view a consequence of a campaign by ‘wise’ voices within colonial admin-
istrations reacting to the pace of destruction of empire forests and
urgently lobbying for planned and scientiﬁc management.⁵
This historiographical tradition has persisted in modern studies of
colonial forest history. While otherwise critical of colonial forester-
historians, most contemporary work on imperial forest history has
tended to accept their account of the origins of forestry in the empire.⁶
The Empire of Nature
56
³ See e.g. Ribbentrop, Forestry in British India; Stebbing, The Forests of India; Sim, The
Forests and Forest Flora of the Cape Colony.
⁴ A. S. Mather, Global Forest Resources (London, 1990), 30.
⁵ Ribbentrop, Forestry in British India, 63–75; Stebbing, The Forests of India, i. 60–8;
Sim, The Forests and Forest Flora of the Cape Colony, 76–91; M. Grut, Forestry and Forest
Industry in South Africa (Cape Town, 1965), 6–9.
⁶ See e.g. R. Guha, ‘Forestry in British and Post-British India: A Historical Analysis’,
Economic and Political Weekly (29 Oct., 5–12 Nov. 1983); Guha, The Unquiet Woods.

--- Page 74 ---

Indeed, modern historians of colonial forestry have used the explanation
of the origins of forestry in the British Empire given by colonial forester-
historians to support their own claims about the nature of imperial
environmental intervention. According to this argument, the emergence
of regimes of environmental regulation, such as forestry, was a direct
result of the rapidly growing imperial resource needs of the colonial state.
Colonial forests in the ﬁrst half of the nineteenth century were thus cut
down extensively to fuel the resource demands of the imperial shipbuild-
ing industry, make way for remunerative cash-crop plantations, and
extend revenue-producing agriculture.⁷ In the second half of the century,
conservation was introduced to sustain the imperial economic engine in
the face of a potential increased demand for and decline in supply of
resources.⁸
Although reasonably correct in their chronology, such explanations of the
origins of colonial forests leave out one important detail. Missing crucially
from such historical accounts are the motivations of the people behind the
ostensibly‘wise’ voices advocating conservation, and their agendas regarding
resource use, elements that could provide context to an otherwise linear
rendering of the history of the onset of forestry in the British Empire.
An important exception to such an analysis has been the work of
Richard Grove.⁹ Unlike other historians of British colonial forestry,
Grove’s focus has explicitly been on the role and perspectives of the people
behind the movement for conservation, whom he persuasively identiﬁes
as being colonial scientists. His main contention is that the roots of colo-
nial ecological intervention do not necessarily lie in the commercial
aspirations of the imperial state, as argued by many historians and
environmentalists, but in humanitarian and ecological impulses latent in
colonial society. Demonstrating a long genealogy of conservationist
concerns among European colonial scientists in the tropics, Grove argues
that the origins of forestry in the British Empire lay in the emergence of
members of the colonial scientiﬁc community as environmental comment-
ators who canvassed new scientiﬁc insights about the environmental,
species extinction, aesthetic, and social effects of deforestation.
The motivations of the colonial scientiﬁc communities campaigning
for conservation, Grove claims, stemmed mainly from two main sources.
The Empire of Nature
57
⁷ See e.g. R. G. Albion, Forests and Sea Power: The Timber Problem of the Royal Navy,
1652–1862 (Cambridge, 1926).
⁸ See e.g. Guha, ‘Forestry in British and Post-British India’.
⁹ Grove, ‘Early Themes in African Conservation: The Cape in the 19th Century’, (with
D. Anderson) ‘Introduction: The Scramble for Eden’; Green Imperialism; ‘Scottish

--- Page 75 ---

The ﬁrst was a concern with the impact of deforestation on climatic
change which arose as a result of the spread of scientiﬁc theories linking
deforestation with desiccation. The second source of the conservationist
concerns of empire scientists, according to Grove, was aesthetic inclina-
tions, including various religious and ritualistic ideas. Important among
these was ‘edenism’, the wish to protect the natural environment as a
special kind of ‘Eden’ for the purposes of the European psyche. Other
aesthetic inﬂuences included worries about species extinction and the
ideas of the neo-romanticist school of landscape painting.¹⁰
Although Grove argues that a major objective of the campaign for con-
servation launched by colonial scientists was to regulate the exploitation of
forests by private timber interests, he tends to discount the signiﬁcance of
economic factors such as the decline in timber supply in inﬂuencing the
growing campaign for conservation.¹¹The principal motive of the colonial
scientist-conservationists, he argues, stemmed from their environmentalist
concerns which ‘valued the natural environment in terms wider than the
commercial’.¹² He contends that it was such an outlook that underlay the
emergence of forestry regimes and other protective institutions such as laws
to conserve ﬂora and fauna in different parts of the empire throughout
the nineteenth century. Grove sees a distinct political relevance in his
explanation of the origins of colonial conservation. ‘If modern ideas of con-
servation did emerge initially as a direct response by a coterie of humanitar-
ian scientists to environmental consequences of colonialism as seems
increasingly likely,’ he argues, ‘a substantial revision of some of the premises
of contemporary environmentalist thinking will be necessary.’¹³
Grove is certainly right in his contention that colonial scientiﬁc
communities played a crucially important role in the emergence and
development of a regime of scientiﬁc forest management in the British
Empire. The historical and historiographical evidence clearly corroborates
his argument that scientists in the ﬁrst half of the nineteenth century were
an important factor motivating colonial governments to set up forestry
The Empire of Nature
58
Missionaries, Evangelical Discourses and the Origins of Conservation Thinking in
Southern Africa’; ‘The Origins of Environmentalism’; ‘Colonial Conservation, Ecological
Hegemony and Popular Resistance’; ‘Imperialism and the Discourse of Desiccation’;
‘Chiefs, Boundaries and Sacred Woodlands’.
¹⁰ See e.g. Grove, ‘The Origins of Environmentalism’.
¹¹ See e.g. Grove, ‘Early Themes in African Conservation: The Cape in the 19th
Century’, 22.
¹² R. H. Grove, ‘Conservation and Colonial Expansion’ (Ph.D. thesis, University of
Cambridge, 1988), 286.
¹³ Grove, ‘Early Themes in African Conservation: The Cape in the 19th Century’, 36.

--- Page 76 ---

regimes. In a context of general governmental lack of concern, colonial
scientists articulated an alternative discourse about forest use which
emphasized the economic and social utility of forests and the importance
of conservation. Moreover, they lobbied other state ofﬁcials on the place of
forests in the national economy and on the importance of forest conserva-
tion. Indeed, it was as a direct consequence of their campaign that the ﬁrst
forest departments staffed by the scientiﬁc protagonists of forestry began
to be set up in different parts of the empire in the 1840s.¹⁴
By demonstrating the efﬁcacy of colonial scientiﬁc communities in
the emergence of forestry in the British Empire, Grove has therefore done
much to question the determinism and the ostensible inevitability of the
forester’s ‘myth of origin’ alluded to earlier. In responding to Grove,
however, historians have tended to posit his arguments against those of
other environmental historians of empire, especially those who claim that
imperial economic interests underlay colonial attempts at ecological
intervention. The ensuing debate has centred on the question of whether
economic and imperial factors or ideological interests were responsible
for the onset of colonial forestry.¹⁵While this debate rages, two important
issues have remained largely ignored.
Theﬁrst of these concerns the agendas and ideologies of natural resource
management of the colonial scientiﬁc community that lobbied for forest
conservancy. It raises questions such as: What were the attitudes to nature
and human communities of the colonial scientist-conservationists who
worked for conservation? What were their agendas of natural resource use
and management? What modes of production were their environmental
interventions intended to engender? What was the relation between their
conservationism and their role as colonial scientists: was there something in
the agenda of colonial science in this period that can explain their campaign
for forest conservation? To what extent were their conservationist ideas
inﬂuenced by European scientiﬁc environmentalist and forestry traditions?
What in short, was their conservationism about?
Answers to such questions will not in themselves provide reasons for
colonial governments supporting environmental regimes. However, by
clarifying the character of the resource management agendas of the colonial
scientist-conservationists and thereby attempting to reconcile the material
and the ideological motives underlying the origins of colonial conservation,
they could provide an interesting and new perspective on the problem.
The Empire of Nature
59
¹⁴ See Rajan. ‘The Colonial Ecodrama’ for a more complete discussion of the existing
historiography.
¹⁵ See e.g. Rangarajan, Fencing the Forest, preface, 4–7.

--- Page 77 ---

They could, in particular, potentially identify areas of convergence in
perspective between the colonial scientists who argued for conservation, and
members of the colonial civil administrations. Grove’s writings are unfortu-
nately not clear on these issues. Despite his allusions to inﬂuences such as
edenism, orientalism, the Hippocratic ideal, and physiocracy as the ideas
motivating conservationism among the colonial scientiﬁc communities,
Grove’s argument falls short of an unambiguous statement setting out what
the agendas of the colonial scientist-conservationists were actually about.¹⁶
The second important historiographical issue that has remained unad-
dressed is the question of whether the campaign for conservancy had
purely ‘colonial’ origins or whether it was inspired by ideas and develop-
ments elsewhere, particularly by the continental European tradition of
scientiﬁc concern with the effects of deforestation. Grove’s analysis of this
question is again not conclusive. In his bid to demonstrate the efﬁcacy of
colonial scientists in bringing about regimes of forest conservancy in the
British Empire, he has avoided any comparisons between their concerns
and those of their counterparts in Europe.
This neglect is particularly glaring given that he identiﬁes scientists such
as Humboldt and Boussingault as important inﬂuences for the colonial
protagonists of forest conservancy in the ﬁrst half of the nineteenth century.
It is noteworthy, however, that he underplays their research in Europe while
emphasizing their New World work. Indeed, Grove has built his argument
around the thesis that the motivations of colonial scientiﬁc campaigns for
forest conservancy came from the colonial periphery, rather than the centre.
‘While it is tempting to conceptualise the development of the colonial
conservation movement in terms of the “European system” set out by
Immanuel Wallerstein’, he reasons, ‘the notion of a European-centred
system fails as an explanatory device, particularly in Western India and
West Africa.’¹⁷ The nub of his argument is that it was observations of
environmental decline in different parts of the empire, and especially in
small, environmentally vulnerable island colonies, that engendered worries
about phenomena such as droughts. Underlying these concerns, Grove
claims, lay a ‘fundamental and subliminal anxiety about the ability of the
European settler to survive in an alien land, or indeed, his right to do so’.¹⁸
The Empire of Nature
60
¹⁶ It is important to note here that although Grove mentions such inﬂuences, he does
not go one further step and analyse what they mean in terms of perceptions and agendas of
resource use and conservation.
¹⁷ Grove, ‘Colonial Conservation, Ecological Hegemony and Popular Resistance’,
17–18.
¹⁸ Grove, ‘Early Themes in African Conservation: The Cape in the 19th Century’, 35.

--- Page 78 ---

Grove contends further that it was in a colonial context that early
environmentalists were ﬁrst able to lobby governments and exert an inﬂu-
ence inhibiting environmental changes they did not like. He claims that
they were able to do this long before this was politically palatable in Europe.
In particular, it was in the colonial context, in Australia and North America
as well as in Africa, that the natural environment was ﬁrst perceived as
threatened speciﬁcally by European economic forces and where govern-
ments ﬁrst incorporated conservation as part of their accepted role.¹⁹
In the absence of an attempt to locate colonial environmentalist cam-
paigns in the context of concerns among the scientiﬁc community with the
social and environmental effects of deforestation in Europe, however, any
attempt, such as Grove’s, to argue that they were a result of a purely colonial
concern is open to challenge. Although his central thesis—that scientiﬁc
communities played an important role in the emergence and development
of colonial forestry regimes—is valid, his analysis of the origins and charac-
ter of their conservationist motivations therefore needs to be re-examined.
The task of this chapter is to undertake such a revision. In doing so, it
continues with the two broad themes in forest conservation history
described in Chapter 2, which were concerned, respectively, with the ‘indir-
ect’, or the climatic effects of woodlands, and the ‘direct’, or their commer-
cial use. The chapter traces the emergence of the idea of forest conservancy
in the British Empire by following the development of these two intellectual
trends, through the two distinct periods in colonial forest history in the
nineteenth century—the ﬁrst half, when expatriate colonial scientists
lobbied for scientiﬁc conservancy, and the second, when the system of
continental forestry was established in British India with state support.
3.1. THE CAMPAIGN FOR FOREST 
CONSERVANCY IN THE FIRST HALF OF 
THE NINETEENTH CENTURY
One way to understand the motivations of the forest conservation prot-
agonists of the ﬁrst half of the nineteenth century is to use the method of
collective biography to examine their interests and activities as scientists
of empire.²⁰ Such an analysis, contextualized within an understanding of
the nature of colonial science in the nineteenth century, could also help
The Empire of Nature
61
¹⁹ Grove and Anderson ‘Introduction: The Scramble for Eden’, 6.
²⁰ For an analysis of prosopography as a research tool in the history of science, see
S. Shapin and A. Thackray, ‘Prosopography as a Research Topic in History of Science: The

--- Page 79 ---

explain the agendas and ideologies underlying their conservationism.
In undertaking such a study, this section will concentrate in large meas-
ure on India. It will however seek to compare the Indian case with
developments in the Cape Colony, which, like India, witnessed a strong
campaign for conservancy in the ﬁrst half of the nineteenth century.
The early proponents of forest conservancy in India were all East India
Company (EIC) employees, working either in the botanical gardens, the
medical service, or the army. Their character proﬁles and writings help us
understand the motivations underlying their campaign to conserve
forests.²¹ A critical fact in this regard is that a great many of the EIC
surgeons and scientiﬁc ofﬁcials had been educated in botany and medi-
cine in Edinburgh, which was, at that time, one of the big centres of the
Scottish enlightenment and linked extensively with the enlightenment
scientiﬁc centres of Germany and France.²² Equally noteworthy is the
fact that other supporters of scientiﬁc forestry in India had been educated
in leading European schools.
One signiﬁcance of the continental connection is that the scientists
of the EIC were aware of the latest scientiﬁc work in Europe and had been
exposed to the European scientiﬁc theories concerning the deleterious
effects of deforestation.²³ Another consequence of the continental con-
nection is that they were cognizant of a range of intellectual currents in
Europe, including German Naturphilosophie and French physiocracy.²⁴
The latter is particularly relevant because of its emphasis on the primacy
of land and on improving agricultural productivity. It is important to
note here that a great deal of work in the Asiatic Society was inﬂuenced by
the physiocratic doctrine.²⁵
The Empire of Nature
62
British Scientiﬁc Community 1700–1900’, History of Science, 12 (1974), 1–28. See also
G. Werskey, The Visible College: A Collective Biography of British Scientists and Socialists of
the 1930s (London, 1988). For an excellent illustration of the use of prosopography in the
history of colonial science see H. Kuklick, The Savage Within: The Social History of British
Anthropology, 1885–1945 (Cambridge, 1991), especially chapter 5.

Period 1800–1850. See also IOR L/PWD/8/190.
²² See especially Harrison, Public Health in British India, 94.

Journal of Literature and Science, Edinburgh New Philosophical Journal, Journal of the
Agricultural and Horticultural Society of India, Edinburgh Botanical Society Transactions,
Journal of the Asiatic Society of Bengal, and Bombay Agricultural and Horticultural Society.
²⁴ Harrison, Public Health in British India, 94.
²⁵ See e.g. George Watt, A Dictionary of the Economic Products of India, 6 vols. (n.p.,

--- Page 80 ---

The emphasis on ‘improvement’ led many colonial scientists to argue that
science was vitally important for social and national development. A com-
mon justiﬁcation for state support for science, therefore, was ‘on the ground
of self-preservation even if there were no higher wants of an ideal character
to be satisﬁed. No nation which would be independent and inﬂuential can
afford to be left behind in the race.’²⁶ They were particularly interested in
using science to exploit the resources of the earth and especially the produce
of land for societal beneﬁt. For example, one of them commented:
in contemplating the sources and examining the various substances which con-
stitute the wealth of nations, whether these be the necessities, the conveniences,
or the luxuries of life, and which, by the exercise of industry have been rendered
of value both to ourselves and to others, we observe that the greater number have
been derived from the earth.²⁷
They saw India as a country which was ‘fruitful in soil and rich in climate’
but which displayed ‘an unaccountable discrepancy between its natural
riches and the quality of the different productions when compared with
similar products from other countries’.²⁸
They believed that the reason for this was that India did not exploit its
natural resources and, in particular, the produce of its land adequately.
More importantly, its people lacked the knowledge necessary for the
purpose. Whereas in Europe, ‘arts of culture’ had been promoted by
sovereigns, by non-professional individuals, and by the ‘union of men in
societies’, in India, they had been neglected.²⁹ India had made the fatal
mistake of including ‘the sciences and the arts amongst those subjects
which were treated of in the sacred writings of the Hindoos; a circum-
stance which has been hitherto an insurmountable obstacle to improve-
ment’.³⁰ India, it was therefore believed, was now ‘inferior to the West in
skill’. Therefore, ‘skill alone’ was required in India.³¹
These scientists saw themselves and justiﬁed their colonial presence as
agents of change—experts who would use their knowledge to improve the
material conditions and human resources of the country they were
employed in. They studied indigenous texts on the arts of manufacture
published in various Indian languages to determine the extent to which
The Empire of Nature
63
²⁶ E. Balfour, Vydian and the Hakim (London, 1864), frontispiece. The quote is attrib-
uted to Helmholtz.
²⁷ Ibid. 1.
²⁸ J. F. Royle, Essay on the Productive Resources of India (London, 1840), 5.
²⁹ Ibid. 275–9.
³⁰ W. Ainslie, Materia Medica of Hindoostan and Artisans and Agriculturalists
Nomenclature (Madras, 1813), 21.
³¹ Royle, Essay on the Productive Resources of India, 289.

--- Page 81 ---

they could learn from these sources.³²They explored the manner in which
natural resources were being exploited by the local people, and in particu-
lar the indigenous ways of extracting, preparing, and using metals and
materials. They examined medicinal plants and local drugs, the formulae
of various compounds used therein, and their methods of preparation.
Of particular interest to this community was the question of how to
make Indian agriculture as remunerative to the East India Company as
possible.³³They examined various agricultural products and their uses.³⁴
They attempted to introduce useful plants from Europe and the rest of
the world in to India and to improve experimentally the productivity
of, among many other things, indigenous sheep and cattle.³⁵They exper-
imented with improving the productivity of proﬁt-generating cash crops
such as hemp, cotton, and tea.³⁶ Moreover, they engaged in research
on using European science to realize the potential of India’s natural
resources.³⁷
The campaign for conservancy mounted by these scientiﬁc communit-
ies stemmed partly from the scientiﬁc tradition of concern with the envir-
onmental impact of deforestation. It also grew, to a degree, upon the
colonial science ethos in early nineteenth-century India, especially its
commitment to using science to ‘improve’ what they saw as a stagnating
and underdeveloped agrarian economy. The pages to follow will explore
each of these motivating factors for conservation in turn.
3.1.1. Forests and Climate
As Richard Grove has argued, concerns with the environmental
effects of deforestation played an important role in the emergence of a

conservationist impulse among the colonial forestry community in early
nineteenth-century India. An important culmination of this concern was
the production, by a team of colonial scientiﬁc protagonists of forest conser-
vancy in the mid nineteenth century, of a report on the economic and
environmental effects of tropical forest deforestation for the British
Association for the Advancement of Science.³⁸The concern with the phys-
ical effects of forests was not however a result of insular colonial discourses on
the effects of deforestation, as Grove contends. On the contrary, it was
strongly inﬂuenced by the European scientiﬁc concerns with deforestation
described in Chapter 2.
Many of the proponents of forestry amongst the scientiﬁc community
in India during the ﬁrst half of the century closely studied the work of sci-
entists concerned with deforestation in Europe and the United States.³⁹
Surgeon Edward Balfour, a keen follower of the European scientiﬁc liter-
ature on deforestation and one of the most vocal proponents inIndia of the
theory that deforestation leads to desiccation, for example, compiled a
bibliography of the international scientiﬁc literature on the inﬂuence of
trees on rainfall and the preservation of moisture for the Madras govern-
ment in 1840.⁴⁰ Hugh Cleghorn, celebrated by many as the ‘father’ of
forestry in India, maintained close links with the great American conser-
vationist George Perkins Marsh.⁴¹ Moreover, he too actively followed the
research on the Continent on the effects of forest clearance.⁴² Late in
his career, he was a central ﬁgure in designing and supervising forestry
training for trainees bound for the Indian forestry and Colonial Ofﬁce

--- Page 83 ---

services.⁴³ Alexander Gibson, the ﬁrst forest conservator of the Bombay
Presidency, similarly was a keen student of the global literature on defor-
estation. Citing the European literature, he said that trees were ‘chemical
instruments for the formation and distillation of dews and rain’.⁴⁴The fact
that the colonial scientist-conservationists actively invoked the work of
Humboldt, Boussingault, and other European authors on the relation
between deforestation and climatic change therefore indicates that their
ideas were not only derived from local colonial experiences but also drew
upon a global ‘invisible college’ of environmentalists. Moreover, rather
than being isolated voices in the colonial wilderness generating insular
conservationist discourses, as Grove has claimed, they were carriers of a
growing European environmentalist consciousness.
The importance of international exposure to the campaign for conserva-
tion launched by EIC botanists and doctors in the ﬁrst half of the nineteenth
century is evident in the issues they chose to articulate while advocating
forest conservancy. One such issue was that of the relation between defor-
estation and water ﬂows. Citing the European literature, they argued that
events in many parts of India corroborated what had happened in Europe.
In the Bombay Presidency, for example, rivers and creeks of the Malabar
coast where ships once used to ride at anchor had been silted up in the living
memory of people.⁴⁵ In the Deccan Highlands and the Eastern Ghats,
denudation had again resulted in the gradual silting up of rivers. The story
was similar in the Coromandel coast and the rivers Godaveri and Krishna.⁴⁶
In addition to siltation, deforestation had caused the destruction of fertile
valleys as a result of erosion and avalanches in many parts of India.⁴⁷
An associated issue was the question of water supply. Local water
supplies, especially springs, the colonial scientist-conservationists pointed
out, had dried up in many parts of India. Again, water levels in the big
rivers had, they claimed, perceptibly declined. Citing the European work,
they argued that forests improved soils and maintained the permanency of
springs.⁴⁸ It is pertinent to note here that a great deal of the focus on the
environmental effects of forests by the colonial scientists was, as in the case
of the work done in the French, Italian, and Austrian Alps, concentrated

66

on mountain ecosystems. As the report to the British Association men-
tioned earlier put it:
if the forests had no other effect than to protect the clay soil of the mountains
from the action of the sun’s rays, this ought alone to be sufﬁcient to secure their
careful preservation. It is in this soil that the waters which supply all the streams
of the island, and which percolate downwards to the lower lands, are enclosed.⁴⁹
In addition to worries about water ﬂows and water levels, there was, as in
Europe, a concern with the relation between the destruction of vegetation
and the incidence of drought. Citing the European research on this subject,
the colonial scientist-conservationists argued that the droughts in different
parts of the empire were related to forest clearance. They concluded, for
example, that the periodic droughts in Penang and the Eastern Archipelago
were a result of deforestation.⁵⁰ Again, the ‘sultry atmosphere and dreadful
droughts’ of the Cape Verde Islands were seen as a consequence of defor-
estation.⁵¹ In India, they pointed out, with the exception of a few localities
in the south, the whole country ‘seems destitute of trees . . . nothing so
much strikes the attention as their general scarcity’.⁵²In large parts of India,
they thus argued, ‘climate and irrigation have rapidly deteriorated from a
similar cause’.⁵³ They claimed further that it ‘admits of no question’ that
‘such droughts will again and again happen’.⁵⁴They contended further that
with the destruction of leafy forests ‘every drought will prove more baneful
than the preceding’.⁵⁵ The concern with drought among the scientists
campaigning for conservancy was accentuated by their commitment,
mentioned earlier, to ensure the productivity of agriculture. To quote
Edward Balfour, ‘we can not but look upon the cultivation of trees as of vital
importance in such a country as India, where . . . the fertility of the land is
so much dependent on the supply of water’.⁵⁶
The agenda of the colonial scientiﬁc protagonists of forest conservancy
in the early nineteenth century was to bring to the colonial world the
kinds of protective regimes that were beginning to emerge in Europe.
In an inﬂuential report published in 1860, some of the most vocal of the

colonial scientist-conservationists especially referred to European-style
environmental regimes as ideal for the empire:
In Germany and France there are especial laws for the protection and extension
of forests. They have the same in all the Italian States. So far back as 1475 the
subject attracts the attention of the famous Venetian Council of X., by which a
law was passed on the 7th of January that year, regulating in great detail the clear-
ance of the forests on terra ﬁrma. The mountain forests especially were protected
by judicious regulations, which were renewed from time to time down to the very
year of the extinction of the old republics. Tuscany and the Pontiﬁcal govern-
ments were equally provident.⁵⁷
The colonial context in the nineteenth century was thus not a setting for
the implementation of ideas that could not get state sanction in Europe, as
Grove argues.⁵⁸ Rather, the onset of environmentalism as a state concern in
the empire in this period was facilitated by an explicit reference to European
precedents. It is clear from the educational backgrounds of colonial
scientist-conservationists, their personal associations, the literature they
cited, and the kinds of issues they raised in support of their campaign for
forest conservancy that they were inﬂuenced considerably by the body of
scientiﬁc work on the environmental effects of deforestation that had been
conducted in continental Europe. The thrust of the campaign for conser-
vancy was to get colonial governments to adopt the technological and legal
regimes of environmental management that were already in existence in
Europe.
3.1.2. The Ethos of Colonial Science and the Economic
Argument for Conservation
The concern with the physical and environmental effects of deforestation
was however only a part of the campaign for forest conservancy. As in
Europe, the colonial scientiﬁc community in early nineteenth-century
India looked upon forest conservation as part of a wider commitment to
The Empire of Nature
68
⁵⁷ Cleghorn et al., ‘BAAS Report on Tropical Forest Destruction’, 91.
⁵⁸ It is important to note here that Grove’s argument about insular environmental
discourses could well be valid in the period prior to the 19th century (see e.g. Grove, ‘The
Origins of Environmentalism’). Indeed, the impact of developments in the colonial
periphery on European ideas needs to be more thoroughly investigated. By the 19th
century, however, as argued in the previous section, there already was a strong global scient-
iﬁc tradition of concern with deforestation. Moreover, European states, as shown earlier,
had already set important precedents concerning forest conservation.

--- Page 86 ---

efﬁcient resource use. At the outset, most of them were active in what
today are called bio-prospecting activities, engaging in inventory surveys
of the biotic potential in India’s forests.⁵⁹They also took part in scientiﬁc
research on various aspects of forests and forest products.⁶⁰ On the basis
of such work, they began to argue that forests were potentially a vast
reservoir of natural resources. As the BAAS report put it:
it is not only in affording indigenous woods of wonderful variety, serving all the
purposes to which timber is applied, that the Indian forests claim our attentive
consideration. In them Nature presents to us other sources of wealth, which
under judicious management may yield a considerable increase to the present
revenue. Gums, drugs, dyes, resins abound, as gutta-percha, caoutachouc, kino,
gamboge, camphor, dammer, piney, varnish, wood-oil, with many other prod-
ucts not sufﬁciently known or appreciated, but which, as the light of European
science penetrates these partially explored regions, will be applied to many useful
purposes in the arts and the sciences.⁶¹
It is important to emphasize here that the colonial scientists campaign-
ing for forestry in the early nineteenth century stressed the commercial
potential of Indian forests. A number of their early initiatives were there-
fore about utilizing forests for commercially valuable timber. An example
of this is a report by Dr Nathaniel Wallich in 1841 which argued that
‘dense jungle’ was an inhibitor for teak and recommended the cutting
down of ‘every other kind of timber (other than teak)’, allowing the teak to
‘spring up naturally’.⁶² Tables were compiled by colonial scientists setting
out in detail the histories of revenues from woods in different parts of the

give examples of discoveries made by scientists of the medicinal and other uses of various
forest products.

ilarly ‘wild’ claims made by a few—a good example was an enduring claim that deforesta-
tion is good for water supply. See ‘Copy of All Correspondence between the Secretary of

--- Page 87 ---

country.⁶³ A great deal of research was conducted to discover the proper-
ties of Indian timbers and woods. The capacities of different species for
producing marketable minor forest products such as wood oil, resins, and
dammar gums used widely in European as well as local industry were also
explored. Moreover, the conducting of systematic topographic and timber
surveys was strongly recommended.⁶⁴ Forests were also seen as useful for
providing woods for engraving, furniture, and ornamental purposes.⁶⁵
In addition to their commercial beneﬁts, forests in the Indian context
were also seen as playing an important function in the economy by
‘furnishing fuel to all the classes’ and serving other needs of the local
populations.⁶⁶ Moreover, there was an appreciation of indigenous Indian
systems of plantations:
The planting of such trees as are desirable from the fruit which they afford, or
grateful from the shade which they yield, is an act which has been held in high
esteem in eastern countries, especially India, from very early times. The eastern
appreciation of the luxury of shade led to the banks of the canals, constructed by
the Mohameddan emperors, being planted, and the waysides of the imperial
roads being lined with trees of various kinds.⁶⁷
The scientist-conservationists were, however, worried that, contrary to
what was commonly believed by ofﬁcials in the company administration,
the productive potential of Indian forests and especially their capacity
to supply timber had severely diminished. An inﬂuential report thus
argued that
From the number and extent of the forests and jungles of India, it might be inferred
that timber was abundant in all parts, not only for home consumption, but that a
supply might be obtained for foreign commerce: this is far from being the case.
Though forest lands are extensive, their contents in accessible situations are not of
a nature, or sufﬁciently abundant, to supply even the ordinary demands.⁶⁸
There was therefore a great deal of concern among EIC scientists all over
the country with establishing plantations, especially of commercially
The Empire of Nature
70

Relating to the Cultivation of Chichona Plants, from April 1866 to 1870’ (Ordered by the
House of Commons to be printed, 9 Aug. 1870).
⁶³ Cleghorn et al., ‘BAAS Report on Tropical Forest Destruction’, 88–98. See also
Royle, Essay on the Productive Resources of India.
⁶⁴ Dawkins, ‘Sylviculture in the Tropical Rain Forest’, 14–18.
⁶⁵ Balfour Timber Trees, 10. Also Cleghorn, Forests and Gardens of Southern India.
⁶⁶ Balfour, Timber Trees, 10.

--- Page 88 ---

valuable species such as teak and sissoo. An important epitome of this
concern was the setting up of ‘The Plantation Committee’ in 1825 which
produced more than 1,060 pages of manuscript on forest cultivation,
especially of sissoo.⁶⁹ In addition to plantation and production there was
a concern with effective utilization of forests. It was argued that in order
to be valuable, timber must not only be of the proper kind and age but be
made easily accessible.⁷⁰
The diagnosis of the causes of deforestation made by the colonial
scientist-conservationists was, like their understanding of the effects of
forest clearance, rooted in the international environmentalist literature of
the period. An important early report on deforestation in western India
thus prefaced its argument on observations made in another continent:
It is a sort of rage in America . . . to destroy the forest as rapidly as possible. The
poor emigrant coming from Europe where wood is very valuable, and where he
never probably owned a good walking stick, conscious that the treasure thus
placed at his unlimited disposal is inexhaustible, without much reﬂection
proceeds to clear the wilderness for the cultivation of his farm. The incalculable
beneﬁt that would arise to his cattle, and to himself, by having patches of forest
trees here and there, never troubles him. He uses the trees as men use water of a
river, merely because there is plenty of it.⁷¹
The theory that human beings caused deforestation due to ignorance
was extended to the Indian context. It was argued that the growing demand
for forest products and the increasingly unregulated commercial exploita-
tion of Indian forests was responsible for their rapid destruction. There was
consequently a great deal of concern about the impact of the steadily
increasing demand for forests stemming from the extension of cultivation
and the onset of the railways. Soon, such fears metamorphosed into the
argument that demand was far in excess of possible supply. Many colonial
scientist-conservationists urged the setting up of plantations exclusively for
railway use. They also urged the use of timber substitutes, such as iron, for
railway sleepers.⁷²There was also a concern with the wastefulness of private
timber interests and, especially, unregulated commercial exploitation by
corrupt contractors. As one article on the forests of the Malabar put it:
This work of destruction is conducted by a company of Parsee merchants, who take
a certain number of the natives from Mangalore at the proper season for felling,
and, without consideration for the future, cut all sorts of peon-spars, saplings as well
The Empire of Nature
71
⁶⁹ Ibid. 81.
⁷⁰ Ibid. 80–1.
⁷¹ Gibson, ‘Extracts from a Report on the Teak Forests of Western India’.
⁷² Cleghorn, Forests and Gardens, 32.

--- Page 89 ---

as large trees, to the great injury of the forests. There were hundreds of small spars
from ﬁve to nine inches diameter, and thirty-ﬁve to seventy-feet long, actually
decaying on the beach at Mangalore at the time I was there; from which circum-
stance in the course of a few years these valuable forests must be exhausted. The
whole of this trade is in the hands of a combined party of these people, who never
fail to take advantage of any particular demand that might occur.⁷³
Moreover, unlike Europe, where ‘soft wood is thinned out and the hard
wood left’, it was argued that in India, ‘the valuable kinds are removed and
the scrub left’.⁷⁴ Indeed, laissez-faire economic policies in the realm of
forests were seen as giving rise to the free-rider problem and creating a
tragedy of the commons that would be ultimately destructive to both
agriculture and industry.⁷⁵
Another human-related reason for forest destruction cited by the colo-
nial scientists campaigning for forest conservation in India in their analysis
of the causes of deforestation in India was what they perceived to be the
‘careless rapacity of the native population’.⁷⁶ A major target for attack was
shifting (Coormi) cultivation which was seen as causing ‘the most rapid
destruction of forests’ some of which, like sandalwood, were very valuable
economically. Shifting cultivation was also seen as destructive of soil and
thus affecting the cultivation of major cash crops like coffee. Moreover, it
was seen as attracting the lower classes away from cultivation and thereby
directly affecting revenue besides producing in those who took to it ‘lawless
and vagabond habits’.⁷⁷The local people were also seen as felling trees inju-
diciously and, in not seasoning them properly, using them inoptimally.
In addition to human causes, the colonial scientist-conservationists also
argued that deforestation was caused by natural reasons, especially ﬁre and
insect attacks.⁷⁸
To sum up the argument thus far, the early initiatives on forest
management by botanists and doctors in India were motivated by a
perception of the direct and indirect uses of forests similar to that which
had evolved in Europe. Forests were seen as providing both indigenous
populations and the company with resources they respectively needed.

--- Page 90 ---

Forests were also perceived as safeguarding environmental stability and,
thereby, the health of European savants.⁷⁹ Surgeon Edward Balfour, for
example, speciﬁcally mentioned the writings of Priestley which, he
claimed, directed his attention to the inﬂuence of trees on the health of
man.⁸⁰The main thrust of the campaign for conservancy was to broaden
the governmental perspective on forests and in particular to get it to
recognize that forest plantations were potentially as economically remu-
nerable a land use system as agriculture. Indeed, the principal thrust of
the writings of most of the scientist-conservationists of this period was
their advocacy of the establishment of plantations of ‘useful’ species.⁸¹
The campaign also emphasized that parts of India had been witnessing
a severe shortfall in forest products. Calcutta and Madras had in fact taken
to importing timber, respectively from Burma and Ceylon. An important
item in the agenda of the campaign for forest conservancy was therefore to
curb waste due to reckless deforestation, and, where possible, to optimize
production and exploitation. A basic objective was to get the government
to establish a permanent framework for forest management with effective
safeguards to minimize the occurrence of forest ﬁres. Such a framework
involved the replacement of laissez-faire economic policies with those that
advocated regulation and restriction of private capitalist interests on
forested lands.⁸² It also meant strict regulation of forest use and the ban-
ning of practices such as shifting cultivation. As Grove has argued, the
campaign played on occasional governmental worries about this impend-
ing timber crisis.⁸³ It also attempted to expoit the increasing govern-
mental concern with the droughts in southern India in the 1840s by
arguing that there was a clear link between deforestation and drought.⁸⁴
The European tradition was an important inﬂuence for this campaign.
The arguments about the impact of deforestation on climatic change were
made with explicit reference to scientiﬁc theories that stemmed from

of Montesquieu (see M. Harrison, ‘Public Health and Medical Research in India,

Europe. Similarly, the attitudes to forest use among the colonial scientiﬁc
communities arguing for deforestation mirrored those of their European
predecessors. The ‘colonial’ campaign for forest conservation in early
nineteenth-century India thus had explicit European roots.
With the appointment of the colonial scientist-conservationists as
forest conservators in the mid nineteenth century, this continental
connection was taken one step further. Their concern with scientiﬁc
management of forests led them to look to Europe for examples of how
this could be done. The writings of the scientist-conservationists during
this period therefore regularly cited the work of continental foresters like
du Breuil and Meaume in France and Hartig in Germany. Moreover,
these protagonists of forestry took great pains to procure and read books
on arboriculture and silviculture produced in the continent. This was to
play a crucial role in the development of British colonial forestry in India
and the empire in the second half of the nineteenth century and
beyond.⁸⁵
3.1.3. The Campaign for Forest Conservancy in the Cape
The campaign for forest conservancy in India was reﬂective of the wider
movement for conservation in other parts of the empire. The motivations
of proponents of forestry elsewhere were in signiﬁcant ways similar to that
in India. Indeed, in almost every case the broad ideological framework of
the propagandists of forestry, the glasses through which they understood
the relation between science and development and between human
beings and the environment, was similar to that among the scientist-
conservationists in India. This can be illustrated clearly by examining the
movement for conservancy in the Cape Colony, which like India
witnessed a strong, if less successful campaign, and especially the work of
two people, Dr Ludwig Pappe and Dr John Croumbie Brown, who, it has
been argued, led the campaign for forest conservancy in the Cape.⁸⁶
Like the Indian protagonists of forestry, both Pappe and Brown were
scientists. Pappe was a surgeon, medical practitioner, and botanist and
was appointed by the government to the newly created ofﬁce of Cape
botanist in 1858. Brown was a trained botanist who had been a mission-
ary in the Cape Colony in the 1840s and who subsequently taught botany
The Empire of Nature
74
⁸⁵ See section 3.2.
⁸⁶ Grove, ‘Early Themes in African Conservation: The Cape in the 19th Century’, and
‘Scottish Missionaries, Evangelical Discourses and the Origins of Conservation Thinking
in Southern Africa’.

--- Page 92 ---

at Aberdeen University between 1853 and 1862. He succeeded Pappe as
Cape botanist after the latter’s death in 1862.⁸⁷
Pappe and Brown, like their colleagues in India, were very concerned
about the environmental role played by forests. Pappe was greatly interested
in the relationship between deforestation and soil erosion and the impact
of this on agriculture. He was also aware of the relation between forest
depletion and rainfall.⁸⁸ It is important to note here that Pappe’s associate
R. W. Rawson was a close friend of Louis Bouton, the secretary of the
Mauritius Royal Society and a keen advocate of the theories of Humboldt
and Boussingault.⁸⁹ Similarly, Brown was also concerned with the relation
between deforestation, soil erosion, and decline in rainfall. He conducted
extensive surveys of the scientiﬁc literature of the day and wrote proliﬁcally
on the subject.⁹⁰ Moreover, his writings on the environmental effects of
deforestation were the most comprehensive amongst the deforestation
theorists in the British Empire and, with the exception of the American
Franklin Benjamin Hough, in the entire world during the period.⁹¹
Like those of their Indian counterparts, the writings of Pappe, Rawson,
and especially Brown reveal a strong awareness of the European scientiﬁc
debate on the effects of deforestation. Their concerns with deforestation
were thus by no means insular. On the contrary, their constant allusion to
the wider global scientiﬁc environmental literature reﬂects the fact that
the Cape scientists campaigning for forestry were, like their Indian coun-
terparts, part of a global ‘invisible college’ of scientists concerned with the
impact of deforestation.
Pappe, like his Indian counterparts, was interested in using scientiﬁc and
in particular botanical knowledge to further the interests of the colonial
state. He undertook botanical mapping, assessing the economic potential
of various vegetational products. Through the 1840s and 1850s he spent
a great deal of time studying the ﬂora of the Cape and collaborated with
others in exploring the Cape ﬂora for potential economic uses, especially
R. W. Rawson, with whom he co-authored the Filicum Africae Arboralis,
The Empire of Nature
75

⁸⁹ Grove, ‘Early Themes in African Conservation: The Cape in the 19th Century’, 26.

Reboisement in France; id., Water Supply in South Africa, and Facilities for the Storage of it
(Edinburgh, 1877).
⁹¹ Hough, Report on Forestry.

--- Page 93 ---

a description of hitherto unknown southern African ferns.⁹² In addition to
research in economic botany, Pappe also undertook research on other aspects
of the Cape’s natural riches. In 1853, he published a Synopsis of the Edible
Fishes of the Cape of Good Hope.⁹³ Pappe also played an active role in cam-
paigning for the establishment and maintenance of botanical gardens in the
Cape, stressing their economic role.⁹⁴ Indeed, he was for a great part of his
career in active correspondence with members of the Kew establishment in
Britain and in particular with Sir William Hooker, the then director.⁹⁵
Like Pappe, Brown attempted to use scientiﬁc knowledge to further
economic interests. He argued that science, which was systematic know-
ledge, ought to be applied to tackle various developmental problems such
as irrigation, agriculture, arboriculture, horticulture, and forest economy.
He also advocated the setting up of experimental farms across the colony
that would deploy science for the development of natural resources.⁹⁶
Both Pappe and Brown shared their Indian counterparts’ conception
of the social and economic utility of forests. They held that forests were
necessary to serve the economic needs of the state and local populations.
A great deal of Pappe’s work consisted in the identiﬁcation of the
economic potential of forests. Pappe painstakingly compiled a collection
of timber specimens which formed the majority of the specimens in the
Museum of Economic Botany at the Royal Botanical Gardens at Kew and
various South African museums until early in the twentieth century .⁹⁷
He also prepared a descriptive catalogue of South African timbers for the
Paris Universal Exhibition of 1855 and the International Exhibition of
London, 1861, under the title of Silva Capensis.⁹⁸
Brown shared Pappe’s interest in the efﬁcient use of forests. The basic
purpose of forests, he thus wrote, was to ‘utilise land well’.⁹⁹ He was
particularly concerned that no revenue had been obtained from the
Cape forests in excess of expenses incurred in connection with them.¹⁰⁰
Moreover, Brown was a keen advocate of extensive plantations. He

suggested the creation of farm plantations and, like Cleghorn in India,
the establishment of railway fuel plantations.¹⁰¹ In addition to such con-
cerns, Brown was interested in other uses, such as medicinal, to which
forests could be put.¹⁰²
The diagnosis of the problem of deforestation in the Cape made by the
forestry experts there was similar to that of their Indian counterparts, as
was their understanding of the social and environmental role of forests.
To begin with, there was a great deal of emphasis on the rate of deforesta-
tion in the Cape. Soon after taking over as colonial botanist in 1858,
Pappe toured the colony, especially the Eastern Province, extensively, and
strove to educate the public and the government on the effects of ﬁre and
maltreatment.¹⁰³ Moreover, he published ofﬁcial reports from 1859
to 1961 in which he examined the forest question in detail, emphasizing
the economic and environmental importance of forests.¹⁰⁴ Again Brown,
like his Indian counterparts, saw the problem of deforestation in the
Cape as arising out of natural and human factors. Natural factors
included ﬁre, epiphytic and parasitic vegetable growths, and attacks by
insects, birds, and beasts. Man-made causes included reckless and wasteful
fellings made worse by corruption by supervisory ofﬁcials, government-
sponsored land clearing for agriculture, and damage inﬂicted by local
populations and their herds and ﬂocks.¹⁰⁵ As in India, a great part of the
campaign for conservation in the Cape was over the issue of whether or
not laissez-faire policies should be encouraged in the realm of forests.
There was a vocal body of opinion, led by A. B. Darnell, MLA for
Queenstown, that was strongly critical of government conservation.
Darnell’s inﬂuential pamphlet of 1866 entitled ‘The Past, Present, and
Future Condition of the Forests in the George, Knysna, and Uitenhage
Districts’, for example, argued that it was private interests, and not the
government, which could ensure protection against ﬁre and prevent the
exploitation of forests when the season was over.¹⁰⁶ Against such opinion,
scientists like Brown argued that it was only by effective and authoritative
management along scientiﬁc lines that forests could be saved and supplies
assured for different resource users in the long run.¹⁰⁷
The Empire of Nature
77
¹⁰¹ Ibid. 82.
¹⁰² See e.g. J. C. Brown, African Fever and the Culture of the Blue Gum Tree to Counteract
Malaria in Italy (Aberdeen, 1890).
¹⁰³ Sim, The Forests and Forest Flora of the Cape Colony, 82.
¹⁰⁴ Ofﬁcial Reports of the Cape Botanist (Cape Government, 1859–61).

¹⁰⁶ Sim, The Forests and Forest Flora of the Cape Colony, 82.
¹⁰⁷ Ibid. 83.

--- Page 95 ---

The conservation agenda in the Cape, like in India, was about curbing
waste and ensuring efﬁcient and sustained use. Brown himself emphas-
ized four points: protection against the destruction occurring irrespective
of man’s agency, protection against destruction through man’s careless-
ness, protection against destruction due to man’s dishonesty, and, most
importantly, forest management to effect ‘an improved, economic and
less wasteful forest exploitation combined with forest restoration for
environmental reasons’.¹⁰⁸The latter point is crucial to an understanding
of the Cape conservation movement. Although there was a great deal of
emphasis on the environmental effects of forests in Brown’s writings,
there was in addition a considerable interest in exploiting forests to meet
the resource needs of society and the state. As colonial botanist, Brown’s
agenda on Cape forests was to ensure that the government adopted a
programme of planned scientiﬁc management. His appeal for planned
forest use led to the setting up of a Select Committee on forest manage-
ment in 1865.¹⁰⁹Testifying to this committee, Brown strongly advocated
the procurement of basic information about the kinds and extent of forest
use in the Cape as a precursor to planned scientiﬁc management. Like his
Indian counterparts, he urged the setting up of forest surveys and the
preparation of stock statements of the forests of the Cape.¹¹⁰
By the mid nineteenth century, Croumbie Brown, among other
colonial scientists, was fully convinced that the best way of managing the
forests of the Cape was to introduce continental European forestry
methods. Like the Indian pioneer Hugh Cleghorn, Brown believed that
European-style forestry was the only way of seriously addressing the issue
of forest management. After being retrenched in 1866 following the
adoption of economy measures by the Cape government in the 1860s,
he devoted most of his time to studying and writing about the develop-
ment and evolution of different systems of forestry in several East and
West European countries.¹¹¹
The Empire of Nature

--- Page 96 ---

In their campaigns, Pappe and Brown, like the Indian foresters, played
on the sensibilities of the colonial state, particularly by stressing the issue
of the depletion of timber supply and by linking deforestation with the
droughts of 1847 and 1862. They were only partially successful, however:
while their campaign did much to bring the issue of forestry to govern-
mental attention, they themselves did not have the political clout to
persuade the government to accept their policy programmes. Indeed, it
was not until the Indian forest department began to show ‘success’ in
providing the state with its forest needs on an assured basis that the Cape
government seriously acted on the recommendations of Brown and his
colleagues and established forest departments with the help of Indian and
continental forestry experts.
3.2. THE ONSET OF CONTINENTAL 
FORESTRY IN THE BRITISH EMPIRE
The second half of the nineteenth century marked an important water-
shed in the history of British colonial forestry. The creation of a govern-
mental forest service in India in the mid nineteenth century set in motion
a programme to change systems of forest management and recast them in
the continental mould. Over the next ﬁve decades, the Indian forest
department, staffed by personnel trained in Germany and France,
systematically erected a framework of resource use modelled along
European lines. Laws restricting resource use were passed, silvicultural
systems inaugurated, and new approaches to forest utilization launched.
By the last two decades of the nineteenth century, the Indian forestry
community could claim success in supplying the government with its
forest products needs. This ostensible success, combined with effective
lobbying by forestry supporters in Britain and the empire, led to govern-
ments in other regions of the colonial periphery following the Indian
model. Between 1876 and 1900, forest departments had been set up with
the assistance of the Indian forest department in New Zealand,
Mauritius, Cyprus, Ceylon, the Cape Colony, Nigeria, Sierra Leone,
The Empire of Nature
79
Mountains in Eastern Russia (Edinburgh, 1884); id., Forests and Forestry in Northern Russia
and Lands Beyond (Edinburgh, 1884); id., The Forests of England and the Management of
Them in Bye-gone Times (Edinburgh, 1883); id., Schools of Forestry in Germany, with
Addenda Relative to a Desiderated British National School of Forestry (Edinburgh, 1887). For
an excellent recent analysis of Brown see William Beinart, The Rise of Conservation in South
Africa: Settlers, Livestock, and the Environment 1770–1950 (Oxford, 2004).

--- Page 97 ---

Gold Coast, Kenya, Uganda, the Straits Settlements, Malaya, and
Australia. By the turn of the century a large pan-colonial scientiﬁc com-
munity was in existence, trained in the German and French tradition of
forestry, and therefore sharing a common intellectual ethos and practical
outlook.¹¹² An important feature of this community was its homogen-
eity, brought about by the shared professional ethos of its members, all of
whom had been trained either in the Nancy forest school or in forestry
institutions in Germany until the mid 1880s.¹¹³
The purpose of this section is to examine the impact of this professional
ethos on the development of forestry in India and other parts of the British
Empire in the second half of the nineteenth century. At the outset, it seeks
to examine the extent to which the agendas and ideologies of colonial
forestry in this context bore similarities to its continental parent. To this
end, it focuses on the two key aspects of forestry: its agendas on forest
management and its attitude to society, especially local peoples. Since
India was the central theatre in which professional forestry emerged in the
British Empire in the nineteenth century, the argument in this section will
concentrate on this context. It will, however, seek in conclusion brieﬂy to
locate the Indian case in the wider scenario of empire forestry as a whole.
3.2.1. Indian Foresters and Forest Management
In order to understand the objectives of the Indian forestry profession on
forest management in the second half of the nineteenth century, it is
important, at the outset, to note the continuities between them and
the scientist-foresters of the earlier era. Like the early forestry advocates,
The Empire of Nature
80
¹¹² For a chronology of the development of British colonial forestry, see Chapter 1.
¹¹³ Of particular utility here is the ideal-type of ‘profession’ and ‘professionalism’ devel-
oped by sociologists who have attempted to characterize the concept by identifying traits
common among different professions. Sociologists following such an approach have
described a profession as a full-time vocation deﬁned by the following characteristics: the
possession of skill based on systematic, theoretical, and esoteric knowledge; the provision
of exacting and specialized training; procedures for testing and certifying the competence
of members; organizations, often self-regulating and state sanctioned, to enforce stand-
ards, maintain a strong sense of corporate identity, and exert a degree of monopoly and
adherence to the norm of altruistic, though remunerated, service to clients and to society
at large. Professions, they argue, existed in part to deal with the ‘vital practical affairs of the
general public and of individual lay clients, via the application of esoteric knowledge’
(J. Morell, ‘Professionalisation’, in R. C. Olby et al. (eds.), Companion to the History of
Modern Science (London, 1990), 981). The reason for using this deﬁnition of profession-
alism and not any other is purely heuristic: it eminently suits the analysis being carried out
in this section. The question of the type of professional community the colonial foresters
constituted is a topic of discussion in its own right and is beyond the scope of this book.

--- Page 98 ---

the founders and leading lights of Indian forestry in the second half of the
century, including Hugh Cleghorn, Dietrich Brandis, and Frederick
Bailey, were, to use a term currently in vogue, bio-prospectors who sought
optimally to exploit the riches of the subcontinent’s forests.¹¹⁴ Their
point of departure, however, was the fact that rather than attempt to
manage forests on a piecemeal basis, as their predecessors did, they
embraced the continental tradition unequivocally. Hugh Cleghorn, for
example, toured European forestry schools quite extensively and wrote
about their relevance for the training of Indian foresters. Similarly,
Dietrich Brandis, about whom there will be more discussion in the pages
to come, strongly advocated the training of Indian foresters in Europe,
going so far as to recommend that the trainees be placed under the direct
supervision of Prussian master-foresters.¹¹⁵ Moreover, both Brandis and
Cleghorn wrote several memos to the India Ofﬁce and the government of
India lobbying heavily for the provision of continental forestry training
for Indian forest ofﬁcers. It is noteworthy that government favourably
received their proposals.¹¹⁶
One of the earliest attempts at imparting European training was the
scheme, from 1867 to 1884, wherein Indian forestry trainees were sent to
the Nancy forestry school in France. As shown in Appendix 1, which out-
lines the syllabus and coursework programme at Nancy, the training they
received there was quintessentially representative of the continental
approach, stressing silviculture, engineering, law, economics and man-
agement, and wider environmental aspects of forestry, including the
ﬁxation of dunes and the control of mountain streams.¹¹⁷ Although the
forestry curriculum at Nancy was deemed perfectly adequate by those at
the head of Indian forestry, a debate loomed by 1880 about the feasibility
of shifting the training to a location in the British Isles.¹¹⁸The reason was

--- Page 99 ---

a letter by H. Nanquette, the director of the Nancy forestry school,
complaining that the English students there were negligent and inattent-
ive to their studies.¹¹⁹ The ensuing inquiry found that with the rise of
English students at Nancy from around 4–5 in 1873 to about 18 by 1880,
the need for them to mix with their French colleagues was not pressing.
Consequently, their knowledge of the French language deteriorated,
hampering their ability to follow the lectures and undertake their studies
adequately.¹²⁰ Some of those engaged in the debate, like Col. F. Pearson,
explained these sociological changes and argued that at the end of the day:
it would be a great error to suppose that the Frenchmen as a body would prefer
our young fellows as their constant companions to their own countrymen. The
tastes and habits of the two races are too different.¹²¹
And so, the locus of training for Indian foresters moved, in 1885, to the
Royal Indian Engineering College at Cooper’s Hill.¹²² Ironically, the
western slope of the hill was witness to the last fatal duel in England in
1852, in which the loser was a French exile named Frederick Cournet.¹²³
The French, however, ultimately had the last laugh, because the distin-
guishing feature of forestry in the second half of the nineteenth century in
British India was its strong continental thrust.¹²⁴ In order to appreciate
why this was the case and understand the ideology of Indian forestry with
respect to nature and human societies, it is useful to appreciate the work
of the ﬁrst two inspectors-general of forests for India—Dietrich Brandis
and Wilhelm Schlich.
Brandis
Dietrich Brandis (1824–1907), considered the founder of Indian
forestry, studied botany in Copenhagen under Schouw, at Göttingen
under Grisebach and Lantzius-Benings, and at Bonn where he got his

--- Page 100 ---

Ph.D. under Treviranus. In 1854, he married Rachel Marshman, who
was a sister of Frederick Bailey, an important forestry pioneer in India and
a member of the Indian forest department 1871–90. Marshman was also
the sister of the wife of General Havelock, a close friend of Lord
Dalhousie, the then governor-general of India. On Havelock’s recom-
mendation, Brandis was put in charge of the forests of Pegu in Burma
in 1856 and was subsequently appointed inspector-general of forests of
India. During 1863–5 and 1868–70 he toured India extensively and
established forest management there along continental lines. As men-
tioned earlier, he arranged for the training of prospective Indian foresters
in Germany and France in 1866 and set up a continental-style training
school for Indian subordinate foresters in Dehra Dun in 1878. He also
trained prospective foresters of the forest department of the USA, includ-
ing Gifford Pinchot.¹²⁵ He retired in 1883 and wrote some major books
on botany, the most important of which was Indian Trees (1907).¹²⁶
The primary objective of Brandis from his early days in Pegu in Burma
was to recast Indian forests along European lines. Thinking back about
his work setting up the Indian forest department, he wrote:
The formation of the government forest domains in India and the organisation
of their management, has been a large undertaking and the progress which has
been made in this work could not have been accomplished had the experience
gained in forest administration in Europe not been utilised; and in the future it
will be necessary to maintain an intimate connection between forest administra-
tion in India and in those countries of Europe where scientiﬁc forestry is based
upon the experience of centuries. Climate and the species of trees are different
in India but the principles upon which systematic forestry is based, are the same
in all countries, and the aim in the future must be, as it has been in the past, to
build the system of forestry in India, not upon the ideas and theories of individ-
ual men, but upon the results which long experience has furnished in those coun-
tries of Europe where scientiﬁc forestry is oldest and best understood.¹²⁷
Like the foresters of Germany and France, Brandis argued that forests
offered direct and indirect beneﬁts. He wrote that forests beneﬁted soci-
ety by providing timber and other forest products to meet the needs of
industry and agriculture. Forests also had an environmental utility—they
The Empire of Nature
83
¹²⁵ Signiﬁcantly, Brandis continued to advise Pinchot on the organization of a forest
service there. See his 20-page letter dated 14–18 Feb. 1897 datemarked Bonn, Library of
Congress, obtained from the Forest History Society.
¹²⁶ See Dictionary of National Biography, 1901–1917, 217–18 and A. Rawat, ‘Brandis:
The Father of Organised Forestry in India’, in A. Rawat (ed.), Indian Forestry: A Perspective

¹²⁷ Brandis, ‘Progress of Forestry in India’, 16–17.

--- Page 101 ---

helped regulate climate and protect soils, thereby preventing destructive
phenomena such as ﬂoods and torrents.¹²⁸
In his years in charge of Indian forests, Brandis systematically set about
applying the principles of forestry developed within the continental
tradition to the tropical forests of the Indian subcontinent. Echoing
European foresters, Brandis stated that the objectives of forest manage-
ment involved ensuring effective protection of forests against natural and
human destruction; devising a good system to secure the regeneration of
the forest, either naturally or artiﬁcially; the development of a well-
considered and methodically arranged form of working; and the installa-
tion of good lines of communication to facilitate protection, the working
of the forest, and the expansion of produce.¹²⁹ The aim of forestry, he
wrote, was ‘to steadily improve their condition [the condition of forests],
and never to cut more than the annual production by natural or artiﬁcial
means will justify’.¹³⁰ Implicit in this mandate was the basic principle of
German forestry—sustainable production and harvesting with the long-
term needs of the economy at the fore. The essence of Brandis’s work
therefore was to organize forestry in India and Burma along these lines.
Brandis’s commitment to continental European forestry methods is
evident from his earliest work in Pegu in the 1850s, where he developed a
method known as the ‘Linear Valuation Survey’. In a system reminiscent
of Beckmann and his army of marching assistants with coloured nails
described in Chapter 2, Brandis and his men walked along certain prede-
termined lines—roads, ridges, or streams. As they walked, they carefully
observed the forest 50 feet on either side of the line they traversed. The
object of this exercise was to count the number of teak trees (chosen because
it was the most remunerative of the local species) in different size classes in
the area observed and, on the basis of this, extrapolate the total quantity of
teak in the forest. The observations were carefully recorded by carving
pieces of bamboo representing different size classes.¹³¹ Having thus
obtainedﬁgures on the size composition of the teak trees of the Pegu forests,
Brandis proceeded to calculate the rate of growth of the diameter of trees
in each of the size categories. In addition, he estimated the proportion of
trees of every class likely to attain the dimensions necessary to be grouped in
the category above it. Armed with data on the size composition, rate of
The Empire of Nature
84
¹²⁸ Brandis, ‘Progress of Forestry in India’, 25–6.
¹²⁹ Ibid. 15–16.
¹³⁰ Ibid. 15.
¹³¹ Carving information into pieces of bamboo ensured that it would not be erased in
the event of rain or dew. D. Brandis, Report on the Pegu Teak Forests, 1857–60 (Calcutta,
1861). See also Rawat, ‘Brandis: The Father of Organised Forestry in India’, 86–7.

--- Page 102 ---

diameter growth, and the probability of the survival and maturity of the
forests, Brandis devised schedules for cutting and exploitation.¹³²
Prior to the arrival of Brandis the forests of Pegu were ‘typically polyspe-
ciﬁc, all-aged, “natural” wildwood, with the full tropical rainforest comple-
ment of weed-trees, stranglers, lianes, epiphytes, bamboo, canes and palms,
and a dominantly broad-leaved undergrowth’.¹³³ What Brandis’s method
achieved was to enable the forester to ignore these ‘arbitrary details of nature’
and concentrate on producing approximations of the extent of teak, the pre-
dominant commercial species. Like German and French forestry, it thus lev-
elled across the inherent diversity of forests and produced an efﬁcient scheme
to exploit them. It also led eventually to the transformation of these forests
into commercially marketable monocultures. Brandis applied his method in
other parts of India where he worked, such as the North-East Provinces and
Oudh, from whence it spread to various parts of the equatorial tropics.¹³⁴
Brandis was committed to the principle of sustained yield forestry in
his approach to silviculture. He laid down strict rules that governed
felling, and merchants, lessees, and contractors had to adhere to a min-
imum felling diameter set by the forest department. The process of felling
had to be supervised by professional foresters on the ground to ensure that
forests were not destroyed. Moreover, forests had to be ‘treated’ prior to
felling to ensure the economy of labour, access, and supervision.¹³⁵ In
addition to enunciating ground rules on felling, Brandis also formulated
guidelines for the management of forests. He advocated regular repair,
tending, and pruning. He also suggested the replacement of felled trees by
dibbling seed or planting seedlings in the gaps, landings, and other forest
openings. Where such methods of diffuse replacement failed, Brandis
recommended the establishment of plantations of commercial species
such as teak and sal.¹³⁶ Another aspect of the guidelines for management
enunciated by Brandis was to mitigate factors that would be inimical to
forest growth, such as grazing, ﬁre, and vicious weeds.¹³⁷
Brandis’ silvicultural guidelines were calculated explicitly to prevent
either under- or overutilization of wood. In his ﬁrst working plan for the
The Empire of Nature

¹³³ Dawkins, ‘Sylviculture in the Tropical Rain Forest’, 31.

¹³⁵ Dawkins, ‘Sylviculture in the Tropical Rain Forest’, 27. Methods of treatment

--- Page 103 ---

Pegu, he stipulated that the exploitation of forests in a given period
should not exceed their increment. He thus laid down that the annual
yield should be based on the amount of growth they put on.¹³⁸This prin-
ciple subsequently became the cornerstone of all Indian forest working
plans.¹³⁹Like their German counterparts, foresters in India were expected,
in Brandis’s scheme, to evaluate the magnitude of the disturbances to the
equilibrium of the forest caused by both natural and human causes.
Having done so they were meant to prescribe means for restoring the
equilibrium of growth and yield over time.
In the period 1860–1900, Brandis and his successors went about imple-
menting these principles of forest management. Forests across India were
surveyed and inventories of forest species made.¹⁴⁰ Working plans and
silvicultural operations were devised and implemented for the entire
country along the guidelines established by Brandis.¹⁴¹ As in Germany
manuals with tables indicating yield and volume of different species over
time were compiled.¹⁴² Regimes for protecting forests from natural and
human destruction were established.¹⁴³ Communications were improved
and infrastructure for exploiting forests, such as roads, developed.¹⁴⁴
Forest industries were established to exploit various kinds of ‘Minor Forest
Produce’, such as pine-resin, paper pulp, tanning materials, essential oils,
lac, gum-resin, drugs and medicinal herbs, oil seeds, canes, and ﬁbres.¹⁴⁵
The upshot of the adoption of all these measures was that Indian forestry
by the turn of the century increasingly became a proﬁtable enterprise for
the state. Whereas revenue and expenditure respectively had been
£360,000 and £220,000 in the period 1864–5 to 1868–9, they had
climbed to £950,000 and £600,000 by 1882–3.¹⁴⁶
Schlich
Wilhelm Schlich was born in 1840 and educated at Darmstadt, Karlsruhe,
and at the University of Giessen, where he took a Ph.D. in 1866, the year
he was appointed to the Indian forest service. In April 1881, he succeeded
