--- Page 104 ---

Dietrich Brandis as the inspector-general of forests to the government of
India, and was responsible for the organization of the imperial working
plans branch, which ensured the preparation of working plans on
approved lines and their scrutiny by a central authority. He was the ﬁrst
honorary editor of the Indian Forester, a professional journal started in
1875. In February 1885 he was appointed to organize the forestry branch
of the Royal Indian Engineering College at Cooper’s Hill where he
trained candidates for the Indian forest service until 1905, when under
his recommendation the forestry branch moved to Oxford. While head of
forestry at Cooper’s Hill and Oxford he was responsible for training no
less than 272 of the 283 ofﬁcers who joined the Indian forestry service
during that period. Although he retired from his Oxford professorship in
1920 he continued to be an active contributor to forestry until his death
in 1925.¹⁴⁷
A plaque by the Belgian artist Alphonse Mauquoy in a room named after
him at the Oxford forestry department’s building notes that ‘To him, rather
than to any other man, belongs the credit of the spread throughout the
Empire of modern ideas on forest policy and silviculture.’ This recognition
was due in no small part to his authorship, between 1889 and 1895, of the
ﬁrst three volumes of the Manual of Forestry. The Manualwas the basic text-
book for the candidates for the Indian forest service who from 1885 began
to be trained at the Royal Indian Engineering College at Cooper’s Hill.
Its publication marked the crystallization and institutionalization of the
approach to forest management adopted by the Indian forest department
over the preceding four decades. Being one of the ﬁrst forestry manuals to
be written in the English language it also served in many respects as the basic
textbook of foresters in the empire and elsewhere in the English-speaking
world during the turn of the century and the ﬁrst two decades of the twen-
tieth century. As an American forester put it in the 1920s:
In the early days of education in forestry in the U.S. there was a dearth of tech-
nical books that could be used as texts, or to supplement oral instruction.
American foresters of those early days found in Schlich’s Manual of Forestry about
the only authoritative treatment of the subject in English.¹⁴⁸
Moreover, as a paradigm of forest management, the Manual went totally
unchallenged. As late as two decades after it was ﬁrst published, it formed
The Empire of Nature
87
¹⁴⁷ R. S. Troup, ‘Schlich, Sir William’, in Dictionary of National Biography, 1922–1930
(Oxford, 1937).
¹⁴⁸ Ralph Sheldon Hosmer, ‘Progress of Education in Forestry in the U.S.’, Empire
Forestry Journal, 2/1 (Apr. 1923), 92. See also the Oral History Interview with Hosmer con-
ducted by Bruce C. Harding of the Forest History Foundation on 4 Nov. 1957, in which

--- Page 105 ---

the basis of a Concise Manual of Sylviculture published and distributed by
the Indian forest government for its employees.¹⁴⁹ Schlich’s Manual of
Forestry thus offers an excellent insight into the nature and character of
Indian and indeed empire forestry in the period 1850–1900.
The essence of the framework enunciated in the Manual is contained
in its ﬁrst few pages, where Schlich grappled with the deﬁnition of ‘for-
est’. Rejecting a variety of deﬁnitions that ranged from descriptions of
forests as areas containing wild growing trees and deer to those subject to
special forest laws, he deﬁned the forest in the following terms:
an area which is for the most part set aside for the production of timber and other
forest produce, or which is expected to exercise certain climatic effects or to pro-
tect the locality against injurious inﬂuences; such areas are frequently subject to
special forest laws and legislations.¹⁵⁰
Implicit in this deﬁnition were the basics of the continental paradigm: a
conception of the direct and indirect uses of forests and the idea of the
forest as a timber mine. The three volumes of the manual gave this con-
ception a philosophical and practical shape.
Volume i of Schlich’s Manual was divided into two parts.¹⁵¹ The ﬁrst,
which took up almost 100 pages, was a treatise on the utility of forests.
It mirrored the continental forestry paradigm on the importance and
utility of forests, which were seen as important because they supplied
timber, fuel, and other ‘minor’ produce; offered an ‘opportunity for the
investment of capital and for enterprise’, and generated employment.
They also reduced the temperature of the air and soil, regulated climate,
increased the relative humidity of the air, increased rainfall, and regulated
water supply. In addition, they helped reduce violent ﬂoods, prevented
the occurrence of landslips, avalanches, and the siltation of rivers, and
arrested the velocity of air currents and thus protected agricultural ﬁelds.
Moreover, they assisted in the production of oxygen and ozone and thus
helped improve the health of the country.¹⁵²
The second part of volume i was on the fundamental principles of
silviculture. Here again, the basic approach was explicitly modelled on
The Empire of Nature
88
he says that, prior to Schlich’s Manual, ‘there was very little in English about forestry’. The
interview also draws out the European inﬂuence on British forestry (FHS: SAF # 107).
¹⁴⁹ Government of India, A Concise Manual of Sylviculture, for the Use of Forestry Courses
in India (Calcutta, 1906).

associated with it, see IOR L/PWD/8/129.

--- Page 106 ---

the continental forestry paradigm. Schlich wrote that the object of
silviculture was to ‘yield produce of a deﬁnite description’, such as trees
giving a certain kind of timber. Another purpose was to produce the
‘greatest possible quantity of wood, or other produce, per acre and year’.
A third objective was to produce the ‘highest possible money return per
acre and year’ and the ‘highest possible interest on the invested capital’. In
addition to these economic ends, silviculture was also meant to enable
certain indirect effects, such as regulating the drainage of a country and
preventing phenomena like avalanches.¹⁵³
The second and third volumes of Schlich’s Manual proceeded to trans-
late these fundamental principles into a set of practical instructions on
forest management. Volume ii was a technical handbook on the forma-
tion and tending of woods. It dealt with topics such as choice of species,
artiﬁcial and natural forms of forest regeneration, and the protection of
forests.¹⁵⁴ Volume iii outlined the techniques involved in implementing
the agenda of forest management. Its ﬁrst part was on forest mensuration.
It described the instruments used in forest mensuration, the methods of
measuring felled and standing trees, the method of determining the age
and volume of whole woods, and the analytical tools used to determine
the increment. Its second part was on how to evaluate the cost and sale
values of soils, the growing stock and rent of whole forests, and the ﬁnan-
cial results of a given forestry project. The third and fourth parts of
volume iii were on the principles and practice of forest working plans, the
basis of sustained yield forestry.¹⁵⁵ Between them, volume ii and the two
main parts of volume iii embodied the three classical principles of
German forestry.
On the issue of forest management, therefore, Schlich’s Manual of
Forestry, the epitome of forest practice in India and the rest of the British
Empire and indeed in the English-speaking world in the second half of
the nineteenth and the ﬁrst half of the twentieth centuries, was an erudite
explication of the continental European paradigm.¹⁵⁶ It is important to
note here that the principal sources cited by Schlich in his Manual were

3.2.2. Indian Forestry as an Attitude to Nature
The primary emphasis in the forest management agenda of Indian
foresters in the second half of the nineteenth century was on conserving
and planting what were perceived speciﬁcally as useful species. In places
where species with no direct utility prevailed, it was sought to put them to
use employing the best science possible.¹⁵⁹ Signiﬁcantly, the issue of
animal conservation ﬁgures very little in the writings of the members of
the Indian forest department. Inasmuch as animals entered their ofﬁcial
debates, it was either in the context of debates about farm–forest
conﬂicts, in discussions about beasts of burden or in discussions about
hunting and sport.¹⁶⁰
Members of the Indian forest department were closely in touch with
the American conservationist George Perkins Marsh, the author of Man
and Nature. A version of Man and Nature was issued with notes on forests
and rainfall in Madras by A. J. Stewart, an Indian forester.¹⁶¹ The Marsh
connection enables us to understand the attitude of the Indian foresters
to the natural world better. Marsh was perfectly content with the idea
of human domination of nature, provided it was careful and far
sighted. More than a third of Man and Nature was about forests and forest
The Empire of Nature
90
¹⁵⁷ Schlich was aware of what was going on in German forestry at that time. Of partic-
ular importance was the school of forestry that emerged in the last quarter of the 19th cen-
tury under the direction of Karl Gayer, professor of silviculture in Munich. While it was
very much within the general tradition of German forestry, it was opposed to monocul-
tures and argued for forestry based on mixed forests and in accordance with the biological
laws of nature. Gayer’s school was called the ‘Back to Nature’ movement. (Heske, German
Forestry, 40). Schlich took up Gayer’s ideas while discussing the advantages of mixed forests
(A Manual of Forestry, i. 180–3). His position in this discussion however indicates that he
was by no means an unqualiﬁed advocate of the ‘Back to Nature’ approach. He argued, on
the other hand, that pure woods were easier to manage than mixed woods (i. 178).
¹⁵⁸ The books were respectively on forest protection and forest utilization, originally
written by two contemporary German foresters, Dr Hess and Dr Karl Gayer.
¹⁵⁹ See, for example, D. Brandis, ‘The Utilization of Less Valuable Woods in the Fire-
Protected Forests of the CP, by Iron Making’, Indian Forester, 5/2 (1879), 222.
¹⁶⁰ Almost every issue of the Indian Forester during this period had an article or two on
wild animals marauding into agricultural terrains, and occasionally on man-eating cats.
The hunt was a major sporting pastime for foresters and their families. See M. M. Ledzion,
Forest Families (London, 1991).
¹⁶¹ Marsh, Man and Nature.

--- Page 108 ---

management. Based on a survey of European forestry practices, Marsh
recommended a mix between preserving forests in their natural state
where they were necessary to preserve soil moisture and plant cover, and
the substitution of artiﬁcial for natural forests where they were necessary
for timber.¹⁶² He was however concerned greatly with the complex inter-
relatedness of animal and vegetable life and warned that this was too
complicated a problem for human minds to solve: ‘we can never know
how wide a circle of disturbance we produce in the harmonies of nature
when we throw the smallest pebble in to the ocean of organic life.’¹⁶³
Marsh therefore proposed, as a corrective to previous human carelessness,
a ‘geographical regeneration’, a great healing of the planet beginning with
the control of technology. He also proposed that the human custodian-
ship of the planet was an ethical issue and not just an economic one.¹⁶⁴
The Indian foresters clearly accepted his advocacy of management. It is
however not very clear whether they also accepted his moral argument.
None of the writings of Brandis, Schlich, or anyone else, for that matter,
raises ethical questions of this nature. Moreover, some members of the
Indian forest service did not even accept Marsh’s contention that Man can
have a lasting adverse impact on nature:
Mr Marsh will hardly carry us with him when he states that ‘Nature is wholly
impotent against his energies,’ for with Nature centuries are but moments, and
after man has exerted all his destructive powers and produced a desert, Nature by
his forcible exclusion will be enabled in time to recover her normal condition.¹⁶⁵
By the end of the nineteenth century the utilitarian conservation
sentiment became a developmental ideology in its own right. Indian
foresters at the turn of the century were particularly impressed by increas-
ing support for forestry in America and were ardent supporters of the pro-
gressivist conservation movement of the United States which linked
conservation with the gospel of efﬁciency.¹⁶⁶
The Empire of Nature
91
¹⁶² Lowenthal, G. P. Marsh—Venerable Vermonter, 261.
¹⁶³ Marsh, Man and Nature, 279.
¹⁶⁴ R. Nash, The Rights of Nature (Madison, 1989), 54–8.
¹⁶⁵ Anon, ‘Extracts from G. P. Marsh’, Indian Forester, 9/2 (1883), 82.
¹⁶⁶ A number of books written by Indian foresters in the period 1900–20 were ded-
icated to or made glowing references to leading American conservation ﬁgures. See e.g.
T. Weber, The Forests of Upper India and their Inhabitants (London, 1910), a book
dedicated to Theodore Roosevelt for his support of forestry. See also Sainthill Eardley-
Wilmot, Forest Life and Sport in India (London, 1911), again dedicated to Theodore
Roosevelt ‘Forester and Naturalist in admiration of his success in the conservation of the
natural resources of his country’.

--- Page 109 ---

3.2.3. Indian Forestry and Society
Like their counterparts in Germany and France, colonial foresters in
India developed a commitment to a strong, autocratic control over forests
and forest enterprises. As was the case with forest management, the think-
ing of the Indian forestry community on the subject of control over
forests was encoded in Schlich’s Manual which declared:
History has proved that the preservation of an appropriate percentage of the area
as forests cannot be left to private enterprise in India, so that forest conservancy
in that country has for some time past been regarded the duty of the State.¹⁶⁷
The reason for this, Schlich argued, was that it was only the state that
could appreciate the importance of the indirect beneﬁts of forests and
which could think in the long term.¹⁶⁸
This conception had its origins in two main inﬂuences. The ﬁrst of these
was the long history of experience in India and Burma of the impact of pri-
vate enterprise on forests. As argued earlier in this chapter, a central thesis of
the colonial scientist-conservationists of the ﬁrst half of the century had
been that the onset of private commercial interests in the realm of forest
management inevitably led to a tragedy of the commons.¹⁶⁹ The thrust of
the early ‘colonial foresters’ from Captain Watson to Cleghorn and Gibson
had therefore been to exclude private operators from forests.¹⁷⁰ The early
experience of Brandis in Burma had led him similarly to look at private
ﬁrms with a great deal of suspicion. Much of his early life in Pegu and other
parts of Burma in the late 1850s and early 1860s was therefore spent ﬁght-
ing mercantile ﬁrms, a battle that was only partially successful.¹⁷¹
The second main inﬂuence behind the Indian forestry community’s
commitment to state control over forests in the second half of the
nineteenth century was, predictably, the European tradition. A number
of issues of the Indian Forester in the period 1875–90 reproduced sections
of writings by French and German foresters on the issue of the
importance of state forestry.¹⁷²The debate over the proposed forest bill in
1875, in particular, drew strongly upon both European history and the
ideas on the importance of state control implicit in continental
The Empire of Nature
92
¹⁶⁷ Schlich, A Manual of Forestry, i. 95.
¹⁶⁸ Ibid. 86–96.
¹⁶⁹ The term ‘tragedy of commons’ is used here in the sense of Hardin’s essay. See
Hardin, ‘The Tragedy of Commons’, 1243–8.
¹⁷⁰ See Chapter 2.
¹⁷¹ Ribbentrop, Forestry in British India, 73.
¹⁷² See e.g. ‘A Fragment from a German Writer on the Reasons why the State should
Manage Forests’, Indian Forester, 3/1 (1876–7), 83–5.

--- Page 110 ---

forestry.¹⁷³ It is however important to note here that there was consider-
able opposition to the adoption of continental forestry methods by some
in the colonial administration.¹⁷⁴ These opponents, like those who
protested the adoption of Prussian techniques in France in the nineteenth
century, argued that traditional practices were adequate.
Another manifestation of the commitment of the Indian forestry
community to state control over forest management was the enactment of
forest laws. The ﬁrst Indian Forest Act was passed in 1865 and, based on it,
a number of local rules were promulgated. Within a few years of its enact-
ment, however, foresters began to complain that the 1865 Act did not give
them and the state adequate control over forest lands. They argued in
particular that it did not provide for the ‘deﬁnition, regulation, commuta-
tion and extinction of customary rights’ by the state.¹⁷⁵ A protracted
debate on a new forest Act ensued. In 1878 a new forest Act was passed
which extended to all provinces except Madras, Burma, the Hazara district
in Punjab, Ajmer, Coorg, Berar, and Baluchistan.¹⁷⁶ By 1890, these
provinces passed their own forest Acts modelled on the 1878 Indian Forest
Act. These Acts divided forests into three main categories: reserved, pro-
tected, and village/communal. As in Germany and France, the Indian
Forest Act gave foresters power to determine how forests were to be man-
aged. All the forest Acts provided for control, not only of state-owned
lands but over forests and lands not belonging to the state, ‘if such control
appears necessary for the public weal, or if the treatment such forests have
received from their owners injuriously affects public welfare or safety’.¹⁷⁷
In doing so, the Indian Forest Act deﬁned and restricted rights in a manner
very similar to what had happened in Germany and France earlier.
Like their counterparts in Germany and France, the members of the
forest department in India deemed the demands of the state well founded
and those of the local people (settled agriculturalists and nomadic tribes
who were perceived as wasteful, ignorant, and blinded by short-term
interests) illegitimate. Practices such as grazing animals in forests were
therefore seen as ecologically blind:
The sheep and the goat tear up the herbage instead of biting it clean off. They
throw themselves greedily on all kinds of plants when they do not eat them, they
The Empire of Nature
93
¹⁷³ R. Guha, ‘An Early Environmental Debate: The Making of the 1878 Forest Act’,
Indian Economic and Social History Review, 27/1 (1990), 75.
¹⁷⁴ See for example the extensive correspondence including Grant Duff, Brandis,
Alexander MacKenzie, and Capt. Awdry in the Grant Duff Papers (IOR MSS Eur. F.
234/62).
¹⁷⁵ Brandis, cited in Guha, ‘An Early Environmental Debate’, 67.
¹⁷⁶ Ribbentrop, Forestry in British India, 109.
¹⁷⁷ Ibid. 114.

--- Page 111 ---

devastate the forest, ruin pasture lands, and cause damage greater and more
irremediable than all other cattle put together. When they are very numerous,
they ravage the country just as a ﬂight of locusts would. They cleave the soil with
their narrow pointed hoofs, render it thus more easily furrowed by the next
rainfall, and thus promote the formation of ravines.¹⁷⁸
The local people, they felt, needed therefore to be educated about
priorities in resource use:
as a preliminary to the real conservation and administration of the forests on the
countryside the people with their Oriental conservatism and apathy had to be
gradually weaned from their old methods of utilising—wastefully utilising—the
forests and educated to a recognition of the fact that the work being carried out
was in their true interests.¹⁷⁹
From the point of view of the Indian forestry community, while forest
resources were limited there was a great and growing demand and there-
fore competition for them. On the one hand there were the demands of
the state. India was still a net importer of timber.¹⁸⁰ On the other, there
was a great deal of pressure from the local population, in particular, for
grazing their goats and cattle. Given this overall scarcity of resources, they
felt that forest use ought to be prioritized. As scientiﬁc experts they
believed that they were the only agents competent to do this prioritiz-
ing.¹⁸¹ As one of them put it: ‘all that in India we can hope to do is from
a stand-point of necessarily superior knowledge, to ascertain facts, and
deﬁne our restrictions and the areas within which they are enforced, with
strict justice.’¹⁸²
The issue of resource allocation, principally political in nature, began
thereby to be posed as a technical problem. The Forest Act and stand-
points such as the above were seen as technical solutions. Moreover, what
was perceived as ecological ignorance also began to be seen as moral
backwardness. The technical solutions—the Forest Act and education for
the local populations—were therefore seen simultaneously as acts of
moral improvement. Nowhere is this conception better illustrated than a
poem written as a conversation between two policy standpoints during
the conference on the 1878 Forest Act, which posits a ‘solution’ to the
The Empire of Nature

example, J. Mc Kee, ‘On Grazing’, Indian Forester, 1/1 (1872), 100 which quotes Charles
Darwin on the evils of cattle grazing.

¹⁸³ I am indebted to an unpublished paper on ‘The Myth of the Environmentally
Proﬂigate Native’ by Richard Drayton for the term ‘environmentally proﬂigate’.
environmental proﬂigacy of the Bheel, a tribal community in central
India.¹⁸³
The Gentle Bheel: A Conference Poem
(H) I keenly feel that every Bheel
Should have his wood and water free,
That with his axe, and ne’er a tax
He may take every forest tree.
(W) But one may steal, tho’ not a Bheel,
(H) Yes, true! but here’s my dodge so neat,
I hang a ticket round his neck
Or brand him (gently but complete)
(W) Then with a squeal that branded Bheel
Would make the forest tribute pay,
Tree-brush and scrub, root stem and twig,
He’ll fell; then where your forest(?) say!
(H) The Hungry Bheel must make a meal,
So out of the trees I’ll pick out nine,
And say ‘my Bheel, that list is yours,
And this my gentle Bheel is mine.’
And lest that Bheel again should steal,
I’d teach him manners, give him ﬁelds;
A college, schools, both high and low,
Good roads, and all that science yields.
(W) I also feel, I love the Bheel,
But with your plan I disagree!!
I’d shut up every forest tract
And never let him see a tree,
But as the Bheel must have a meal,
On prickly pear I would have him feed;
And seat him on the cactus top,
If moral suasion he should need.
And as the Bheel the want might feel
Of clothes, I’d take a haversack
And ﬁll it up with seeds of all kinds,
And place it gently on his back.
And o’er the hills, so void of rills,
That Bheel the seed should broadcast sow;

--- Page 113 ---

thus trees would rise before our eyes
And springs would gush and fountains ﬂow.
Then looking down the stream of time
With mental eye like Dollinger,
I see the Bheel, opuntia fed,
Will bless the name of W-ii-nger.¹⁸⁴
3.2.4. Foresters and Other Colonial Ofﬁcials
Although the Forest Act gave the forest department overall legal author-
ity, their actual clout in local contexts was, however, often restricted by
members of the civil administration whose priorities were sometimes dif-
ferent from those of foresters. This restriction was in fact sanctiﬁed by
governmental ofﬁcial policy. The Rules that governed the Indian Forest
Act, for example, set out that:
‡ All Divisional Forest Ofﬁcers and their subordinates shall in all but purely
technical matters be subordinate to the Collector of the District, &c.
‡ No appointments to ofﬁces higher than that of the round guard shall be made
by the Conservator . . . without consulting . . . the collector.
‡ In all matters relating to local supply or the rights and privileges of the people
in or in respects of forests, orders shall be issued by the Collector alone and not
by any Forest Ofﬁcer . . .
‡ No forest compartment shall be closed, whether for planting or reboisement or for
punitive purposes, except under the order or with the approval of the Collector.
‡ A breach of any of these rules carries the penalty of one month’s imprisonment
and Rs. 500 ﬁne.¹⁸⁵
The members of the civil administration, and especially local bureau-
crats whose duty it was to collect revenue and maintain law and order,
were perceived by the foresters as being ambivalent and not totally com-
mitted to forestry:
in the ofﬁcial mind up to the highest, we ﬁnd various degrees of disinclination
towards vigorous conviction: and just as we ﬁnd in the people the progress of
The Empire of Nature
96
¹⁸⁴ Indian Forester, 2/2 (1874), 218. Opuntia is originally a plant of Opus (a town in
Locris, Greece) which was known to send out roots from its leaves. Later, the name was
assigned to a genus of branched, often treelike, cacti native to North and South America
with ﬂattened or cylindrical jointed stems, lateral, chieﬂy yellow, ﬂowers, and ﬂeshy or dry,
often edible, fruits. It is also the name for the prickly pear cactus and the Indian ﬁg, Opuntia
ﬁcus-indica. The following entry in the Oxford English Dictionary perhaps conveys the pur-

--- Page 114 ---

conviction barred by self-interest, so is it with their rulers. Considerations of inter-
est, such as the desire to have no complaints and have everything snug and quiet in
the district, to shew a good revenue sheet by yielding forest produce . . . affect
their capacity for the reception of a sure belief in forest economy.¹⁸⁶
The nature of the science of forestry which allowed no crucial experi-
ments and in which truths were ‘not evolvable from abstract considera-
tions of chemistry or atmospheric law’ but established only by repeated
experience made it difﬁcult for them to convince government ofﬁcials
who, they believed, even when not openly contradicting the established
conclusion about the physical importance of forests, ‘nevertheless nour-
ish a silent and half-smothered distrust, which results in their being
apathetic, coldly negative and very easily persuaded to inactivity in regard
to the subject’.¹⁸⁷
They therefore felt that forest ofﬁcers
not unfrequently ﬁnd themselves in the position of people bound hand and foot
and told to run. At one time surplus revenue is insisted on. At another the closing
of forests and a strictly conservative treatment which in general forests is incom-
patible with the realisation of revenue, is directed.¹⁸⁸
The emergence of a homogeneous professional community of foresters
thus resulted in conﬂicts over forest management within the colonial
administration. The roots of these tensions lay in the difference in back-
grounds and training between foresters and other members of the civilian
administration. Whereas most members of the elite civil service were edu-
cated in classics and the humanities in institutions like Oxford and
Cambridge, foresters were trained, as mentioned earlier, in technical
schools like Nancy in France and the Royal Indian Engineering College
at Cooper’s Hill, which did not enjoy the same status in British society
during that period.
On the ﬁeld, these differences in status were manifested, among other
things, in differences in income and status between the civil and forest
services. As of 1901, for example, a collector, the chief civilian ofﬁcial,
with a charge of approximately 10,000 square miles, earned a monthly
salary of Rs. 1,800–2,325. A conservator, the chief forestry ofﬁcial, with
a charge 6–8 times that of the collector and with 5,000–6,000 square
miles of forest, however, earned Rs. 1,200–1,600.¹⁸⁹ In addition to
The Empire of Nature
97
¹⁸⁶ Baden-Powell, ‘Forest Conservancy in its Popular Aspect’, 10.
¹⁸⁷ Ibid. 1.
¹⁸⁸ Ibid. 11.
¹⁸⁹ F. Gleadow, ‘Translator’s Conclusion’, in A. Me’lard, Insufﬁciency of the World’s
Timber Supply, trans. and applied to India by F. Gleadow (Allahabad, 1901), 39.

--- Page 115 ---

differences in income, forest ofﬁcers were also legally subordinate to their
civilian counterparts:
It will thus be seen that a senior Forest Ofﬁcer in charge of a Division sometimes
comprising more than one Collectorate, may be in any part of his charge practic-
ally subordinate to a mere boy civilian or a native deputy, and is actually at present
less esteemed and feared than any revenue subordinate. In other words, a highly-
trained and educated ofﬁcer whose charge includes a dozen or more talukas, is
practically subject to the authority as well as to the intrigues of each petty Revenue
ofﬁcer in charge of the taluka.¹⁹⁰
Another cause for conﬂicts between foresters and law-enforcing ofﬁ-
cials was that while the former were interested in efﬁciency in the forestry
realm, the latter were preoccupied with concerns about political stability.
In addition, civilian ofﬁcials were generally more interested in agricul-
ture than forests. As late as 1894, orders from the government of India
stated that
The claims of cultivation are stronger than the claims of forest preservation—
whenever an effective demand for culturable land exists, and can only be
supplied from forest areas, the land should ordinarily be relinquished without
hesitation.¹⁹¹
Both, however, were held responsible for generating revenue. In this,
there was a great deal of similarity between forestry and other systems of
environmental management in India in the second half of the nineteenth
century. In irrigation, for example, plans for agricultural improvement by
irrigation engineers
were not—and could not be—developed in the context in which the province’s
administrators found themselves. They had to balance the powerful expediencies
of revenue and commerce at every turn; they were buffeted by gusts of disap-
proval from the supreme government in Calcutta or in London; and they were
haunted by the nightmare of political upheaval should they disturb unawares the
agrarian society whose loyalty was so precariously secured.¹⁹²
By the last two decades of the nineteenth century, members of the Indian
forestry community, from the top to the bottom, began to believe that the
colonial administration needed to be educated about the importance of
The Empire of Nature
98
¹⁹⁰ F. Gleadow, ‘Translator’s Conclusion’, in A. Me’lard, Insufﬁciency of the World’s
Timber Supply, trans. and applied to India by F. Gleadow (Allahabad, 1901), 38–9.
¹⁹¹ British Library, Government of India, Resolution 22F, 19 Oct. 1894.
¹⁹² E. Whitcombe, Agrarian Conditions in Northern India, i:The United Provinces under
British Rule 1860–1900 (Berkeley and Los Angeles, 1972), 99–100.

--- Page 116 ---

forest conservation. One important manifestation of this perception was
the production of ofﬁcial histories of Indian forestry which, among other
things, emphasized the increased revenue for the state brought about by
the policies of the Indian forest department.¹⁹³ In order to get their agenda
on forest management ﬁrmly recognized by all sections of the adminis-
tration, the Indian foresters also began to organize themselves as a major
pressure group. They systematically lobbied government in India and, on
return to Britain, became leading ﬁgures in the campaign for British and
colonial forestry there.¹⁹⁴
The attitude of Indian foresters to forest management and society in
the second half of the nineteenth century mirrored those of their
European ancestors and counterparts. Their principles led to the eventual
transformation of Indian forests along the lines of the forests of Germany
and France previously. Again there was, as in the European tradition,
a strong afﬁrmation of the legitimacy of the needs of the national eco-
nomy and, in many cases, the diminution of the recognition of the rights
and requirements of local agrarian populations. Predictably, the dismissal
of the rights of local resource users was more pronounced among the gen-
eration of Indian foresters who had been trained at Nancy and the
German forestry schools. Members of an earlier generation of foresters,
especially Brandis, had held that the ‘business of the forester is to produce
not only timber, but . . . a great variety of other produce, among which
cattle fodder is most prominent’.¹⁹⁵ For their epigones, strongly imbued
with the continental tradition, however, the demands of peasants and
other rural resources were, as argued earlier, part of the problem of efﬁ-
cient resource management.
The change in attitudes to local people and their needs arose partly out
of a growing self-perception of colonial foresters as technical experts. This
can be understood better by invoking the notion of technocracy, ‘the
exercise of political authority by virtue of technical competence and
expertise in the application of knowledge’, and examining its relation
with politics.¹⁹⁶ In an article on the idea of technocracy, John Gunnell has
argued that the impact of technological systems on politics can be
analysed at three distinct levels: political power gravitating toward tech-
nological elites in circumstances in which political decisions involve
The Empire of Nature
99
¹⁹³ See e.g. Ribbentrop, Forestry in British India, 204–15. See also Stebbing, The Forests
of India, vols. i–iii.
¹⁹⁴ James, A History of English Forestry, 191–205.
¹⁹⁵ Brandis, ‘Progress of Forestry in India’, 25.
¹⁹⁶ J. G. Gunnell, ‘The Technocratic Image and the Theory of Technocracy’,
Technology and Culture, 23/3 (1982), 392.

--- Page 117 ---

specialized knowledge or technical skills; technology becoming
autonomous and politics therefore becoming a function of systematic
structural determinants over which it has little or no control; technology
(and science) constituting a new legitimizing ideology that subtly masks
certain forms of social domination. In each of these levels, argues Gunnell:
there is a concern about the depreciation of the political realm, the subversion of
traditional bases of authority, and the ascendancy of instrumental power over
political rationality. More speciﬁc concern usually centres on the problem of the
circumvention or atrophy of democratic or representative political institutions.¹⁹⁷
The emergence of a professional forestry department in late nineteenth-
century India led to increasing demands for political power in the realm of
forest management by its members, the technical elite in this context. With
this, technology, in this case scientiﬁc forestry, and the foresters’ belief in
technological and moral (ecologically moral) superiority over the local
population, began to constitute a new legitimizing ideology. An important
consequence of this was the growing demand by the forestry community to
enforce rigorously the provisions of the forest laws and their increasing
willingness to confront the civil administration on this. By the turn of the
century, the forester as technocrat had thus become a very important ﬁgure
in the politics of forest resource use in India.¹⁹⁸ It is noteworthy, however,
that the emergence of the forester as an important player in colonial envir-
onmental politics did not mean actual power for the forester.
3.2.5. Empire Forestry, 1850–1900: The Wider Scenario
The appointment of Indian foresters to set up forestry in other parts of
the empire led predictably to the transfer of ‘Indian’ ideas on forest man-
agement and on the relationship between forestry and society to these
areas. In every context where Indian forestry was transferred, forests
began to be managed according to continental European principles.¹⁹⁹
The Empire of Nature
100
¹⁹⁷ Gunnell, ‘The Technocratic Image and the Theory of Technocracy’, 397. See also
L. Winner, Autonomous Technology: Technics-out-of-Control as a Theme in Political Thought
(Cambridge, Mass., 1987), 133–72.
¹⁹⁸ Of the total land mass of 944,923 square miles under the British in India by the end of
1881–2, the forest department directly controlled 117,648 square miles and had some say in
the management of another 476,664 square miles of private and other forest lands. The area
under cultivation was 350,611 square miles (Ribbentrop, Forestry in British India, 121).
¹⁹⁹ See e.g. T. P. Ofcansky, ‘Kenya Forestry under British Colonial Administration,
1895–1963’, Journal of Forest History, 28 (July 1984); E. O. Egboh, ‘British Control of the
Forests of Lagos Colony and Protectorate, 1897–1902’, Journal of African Studies, 6/2

--- Page 118 ---

Again, in each of these contexts, lobbying by foresters resulted in the
enactment of legislation setting out the legal basis for forestry reserves and
providing for appropriate restrictions forbidding cutting, grazing, or tres-
passing without permit. More signiﬁcantly, in a number of colonial con-
texts, the forestry community increasingly confronted the state for
greater political autonomy and, often, for retaining basic policies.²⁰⁰
Signiﬁcantly, the agendas, concerns, and dilemmas of colonial foresters
were by no means unique to the British Empire. In the last three decades
of the nineteenth century, a debate on forest use ensued in the United
States of America which, as in India and the rest of the British colonial
world, faced six main questions: Are forests worth preserving? How
should the forests be managed, and who should do it? Who owns the
forest? How much forest is left? How should the forest be protected? How
should the forest be used? ²⁰¹ On each of these questions, members of the
American forestry community, some of whom had been inﬂuenced by
Indian forestry and trained on the Continent, increasingly adopted per-
spectives similar to that taken by their counterparts in Europe and in the
British Empire.²⁰² To quote Bernard Fernow, one of the founders of
forestry in the United States and Canada:
If now we take a look at the relation which private and communal interests hold
towards the forest resources of a country, we must ﬁrst of all realize the dual nature
of the forest, namely as a furnisher of useful material as which it is an important
factor in human economy and as a condition of the earth’s surface, and as such
exercising certain important functions in the climatology, hydrology and salubrity
of a country. The ﬁrst of these two qualities, namely the capacity of furnishing
material supplies is the main object of interest to the individual: it is the timber for
which the private forest owner prizes the forest, and the amount of money which
can be realized from its sale; and as long as virgin supplies last, he will exploit them
in such a manner as appears to him most proﬁtable and brings him immediate
returns for his labors. This exploitation must be of the crudest . . . In this
direction, i.e. in the matter of economic use of resources the community is almost
The Empire of Nature
101
(1979); id., Forestry Policy in Nigeria, 1897–1960 (Nsukka, 1984); Carron, A History of
Forestry in Australia; Sim, Forests and Forest Flora of the Cape Colony, 84–92.
²⁰⁰ See e.g. Ofcansky, ‘Kenya Forestry under British Colonial Administration,
1895–1963’, 139. Also Grove, ‘Chiefs, Boundaries and Sacred Woodlands’.
²⁰¹ M. Williams, Americans and their Forests: A Historical Geography (Cambridge,
1989), 395.
²⁰² See ibid. 395–465. Gifford Pinchot, a major ﬁgure in the American forestry
movement, had been greatly inﬂuenced by Dietrich Brandis and on his suggestion had
studied forestry at the Nancy forest school (G. Pinchot, Breaking New Ground (New York,
1947)). It is important to note that the American forestry tradition was not inﬂuenced by
the writings of George Perkins Marsh until early in the 20th century.

--- Page 119 ---

powerless to protect its future interests, as long as it is bound to rely upon the
competition of private enterprise for the exploitation of resources.²⁰³
In North America, as in India and the rest of the British Empire, foresters
increasingly began to grow as an important lobby within the administra-
tion. For a variety of reasons, including the close personal relationship
between Gifford Pinchot, the ‘father’ of American forestry, and President
Roosevelt, the American forestry community was more successful in
convincing its government to support its objectives.²⁰⁴ By the turn of the
century, therefore, British colonial foresters, as pointed out earlier, began
increasingly citing the American case as an exemplar. Their agenda was to
implant in the empire a regime of progressive conservationism within a
context of technocratic politics.
3.3. CONCLUSIONS
To conclude by summarizing the central argument, the European tradi-
tion of scientiﬁc work on the social, economic, and environmental
impact of forest destruction holds a vital clue to the emergence of the
campaign for forest conservancy in the British Empire. The colonial
scientists campaigning for forest conservation in the ﬁrst half of the nine-
teenth century were educated in Edinburgh and the leading centres of
learning in continental Europe, and their de facto membership of an
international network of scientists, forged by their association with
botanical gardens and especially with the Royal Botanical Gardens in
Kew, exposed them to the various debates raging among the scientiﬁc
community at that time.²⁰⁵They were particularly cognizant of the grow-
ing body of scientiﬁc research and opinion on the environmental effects
of deforestation. This awareness, combined with evidence of the environ-
mental and economic effects of deforestation in different parts of the
empire, led them to launch a systematic campaign for forest conservancy.
The campaign was pursued with explicit reference to developments in
continental Europe. Its purpose was to convince colonial governments to
adopt protective regimes of environmental management such as those
that had developed in Europe previously.
The Empire of Nature
102
²⁰³ B. E. Fernow, unpublished MS, Fernow Papers, Cornell University (20/1/561).
²⁰⁴ Williams, Americans and their Forests, 416–21.
²⁰⁵ For evidence of the correspondence between colonial scientists advocating forestry
and the Kew establishment, see W. R. Dawson (ed.), The Banks Letters: A Calendar of the
Manuscript Correspondence (London, 1958).

--- Page 120 ---

The agenda of forest management of the colonial scientists campaigning
for conservation reﬂected those of the European scientists concerned with
deforestation and the ideals of social and economic development latent in
colonial science during this period. It sought to convince sceptical colonial
governments and ofﬁcials about the economic utility of forests. It aimed
further to erect a regime of forest management that optimized resource use
while maximizing the range of products harvested from forests. It saw
forests as providing commodities for a wide group of users including the
colonial state, industries, and agrarian populations. An important aspect
of this multifaceted conception of the economic importance of forests in
the agendas of the colonial scientist-conservationists was a strong com-
mitment to curbing waste. Forest destruction, and consequently waste, it
was argued, was caused by a host of natural and human factors, including
ﬁre, insects, and disease on the one hand, and shifting cultivation,
exploitation by private contractors justiﬁed by laissez-faire economics, and
the reckless attitudes of both indigenous and European peoples, including
members of the colonial administration, on the other.
The environmentalist ideology underlying the campaign for forest
conservancy in the British Empire during the ﬁrst half of the nineteenth
century had two components. First, it had a clear conception of what
conservancy meant in terms of attitudes to nature. It is useful while
addressing the issue of ideology to invoke a distinction made by John
Passmore between ‘conservation’ and ‘preservation’. What the conserva-
tionist opposes, according to Passmore, ‘is not the harnessing of nature
for man’s economic purposes but carelessness and wastefulness in doing
so . . . Conservation (is) “careful husbandry”. ’²⁰⁶
Preservation, on the other hand, is:
the attempt to maintain in their present condition such areas of the earth’s surface as
do not yet bear the obvious marks of man’s handiwork and to protect from the risk
of extinction those species of living beings which man has not as yet destroyed.²⁰⁷
All the scientists campaigning for forest conservation in the British
Empire in the ﬁrst half of the nineteenth century were, in terms of the
above distinction, conservationists. As Hugh Cleghorn put it:
the question as between the maintenance and removal of forests appears to us to
be a question of compensations. Wherever the population requires that every
portion of the soil be made to yield its quota of human food, there the destruc-
tion of forests is to be desired, and the disadvantages to which want of wood for
The Empire of Nature
103
²⁰⁶ Passmore, Man’s Responsibility for Nature, 74.
²⁰⁷ Ibid. 101.

--- Page 121 ---

social and general purposes may lead, must be compensated for, as they doubtless
will be, by the ingenuity which is born of necessity. But there are localities in
nearly all countries to which the tide of population can never ﬂow, but where
forest can ﬂourish, and where it ought to be maintained.²⁰⁸
Another aspect of the environmentalist ideology in the campaign for
conservancy was the attitudes to human communities implicit in it.
Particularly important here was the growing perception among the colo-
nial scientiﬁc community that scientists, as opposed to others, including
various categories of local populations, settlers, and administrative ofﬁ-
cials, knew best how to manage forests. While all those who were not
members of the scientiﬁc community were deemed ignorant and held
responsible for deforestation, blame was specially placed on certain types
of forest practice, such as shifting cultivation. It must be noted here, how-
ever, that there was no explicitly detectable bias, positive or negative,
about local peoples and practices during this period. The predominant
concern was to satisfy various resource needs, of both the government and
the settled agrarian populations.
With the appointment of scientists as forest conservators in India in
the 1840s and 1850s, the history of British colonial forestry took a
decisive turn. On their recommendation, the Indian government began
to look towards Germany and France for expert help on how to manage
forests. The agendas that these experts brought with them were very sim-
ilar to those in Germany and France and, signiﬁcantly similar to that in
the United States, which also adopted the continental forestry tradition.
In each of these cases, there was a strong commitment to building a
regime of natural resource management that could cater efﬁciently to the
economic needs of the empire. As an attitude to nature, the agenda of
British colonial forestry, like its continental parent and American coun-
terpart, was unambiguously utilitarian; conservationist, as opposed to
preservationist.
In addition to adopting the forest management ethos of continental
forestry, British colonial forestry in the second half of the nineteenth cen-
tury also inherited its attitude to other forest resource users, especially
agricultural populations. Foresters in India and the rest of the empire dur-
ing this period were like their continental ancestors in that they held
a clear bias against such communities, whom they viewed as ignorant,
environmentally proﬂigate, and wasteful. In seeking objectively to deﬁne
the forest for the purposes of scientiﬁc management, they therefore
The Empire of Nature
104
²⁰⁸ Cleghorn et al., ‘BAAS Report on Tropical Forest Destruction’, 79.

--- Page 122 ---

devised a system of curtailing traditional rights, which in time developed
into a subdiscipline of forestry—forest law. The advent of the continental
system of forest management in the British Empire thus resulted, to use
Lewis Mumford’s terminology, in the introduction of an authoritarian
technics in the realm of forests.²⁰⁹
A critical element of the attitude of colonial foresters to local popula-
tions was the perception that their claims were illegitimate because of
their ostensible scientiﬁc and technological backwardness. In an import-
ant book on the culture of colonial science and technology, Michael Adas
has argued that machines (and, more broadly, science and technology)
were used by European colonizers ‘as the measure of men’.²¹⁰ It is tempt-
ing to apply such an analysis to the case of British colonial forestry.
Looked at from the perspective of the history of the development of
forestry in the European (and especially French) context, however, it
is evident that the process of judging human communities by their scient-
iﬁc and technological prowess began with the setting up of the forestry
corps in France. The origin of the process of judging human communities
on the basis of their scientiﬁc and technological prowess was thus, at least
in the realm of forestry, much earlier than the date of the ﬁrst contact
between the colonial forester and his subjects, and in a context—western
Europe—that was far away and removed from the colonies.
The inﬂuence of the continental tradition on colonial forestry has
important implications for the conception of ‘imperial’ or ‘colonial’ state
forestry. To begin with, the agendas of ‘colonial’ forestry in this period
were brought forth by a community of technocrats who attempted to
implement what they had imbibed during their professional training in
the continental forestry tradition. Indeed, it took some persuasion to
convince the colonial state to adopt policies in keeping with the recom-
mendations of the professional foresters. The modes of production and
the social relations deﬁned by forestry in the British Empire were thus not
direct offshoots of cultural or economic imperialism, as asserted by many
historians of forestry in the British Empire.²¹¹ Although colonial forestry
proved to be of strategic importance for the colonial economy, yielding
proﬁtable returns that furthered empire, the ‘environmental imperialism’
therein was a result of the transplantation into a colonial realm of the
The Empire of Nature
105
²⁰⁹ L. Mumford, Technics and Civilization (London, 1934).
²¹⁰ M. Adas, Machines as the Measure of Men: Science, Technology, and Ideologies of
Western Dominance (Ithaca, NY, 1989).
²¹¹ See especially Guha, ‘Forestry in British and Post-British India’; id., The Unquiet
Woods; Guha and Gadgil, This Fissured Land.

--- Page 123 ---

social and political contexts that had deﬁned forestry in Europe in the ﬁrst
place. The transplantation of continental forestry in India was thus very
similar to the effect of German forestry on France earlier and that of
Indian forestry on the rest of the empire subsequently. Technology was
only transitively a product of colonial expediency; it provided, as Daniel
Headrick has argued, a context for colonial politics, while at the same
time enhancing the impact of colonialism.²¹² Again, colonial foresters
were not mere ‘servants of the state’; they were carriers of culture of a
technology that had its origins in another setting. An important corollary
is that discussions about whether or not colonial forestry constituted a
watershed in the environmental history of the British Empire are trivial
in the sense that as a regime of resource management, scientiﬁc forestry
was designed to be a major watershed, even in continental Europe, its place
of birth.²¹³
A second implication of the inﬂuence of continental tradition on
forestry in the British Empire is that it seriously questions the conception
of ‘state’ forestry. The emergence of a professional forestry community
trained within a deﬁned tradition marked a signiﬁcant disjuncture in the
history of colonial forestry in that it brought with it a rigid tradition of
praxis with respect to natural resource management. This meant that
although the ethos of empire foresters in the second half of the nineteenth
century had some afﬁnities with that of the preceding regime run by
colonial scientist-conservationists, it was distinct in that it was marked by
a coherence of perspective and purpose on what to do with colonial
forests stemming from the tradition of continental forestry. This argu-
ment is particularly signiﬁcant as a qualiﬁer to attempts by certain histor-
ians to characterize the development of forestry in the nineteenth century
without recognizing the impact of the continental tradition.²¹⁴
A more important implication of the emergence of a professional
community is that although forestry was sponsored by the colonial state,
the agendas for forest management made by them were often opposed by
other governmental agencies. This led, as argued earlier, to important
conﬂicts between the forestry community and the state. The bone of
contention was political authority for the forester. In debates within the
The Empire of Nature
106
²¹² D. R. Headrick, The Tentacles of Progress: Technology Transfer in the Age of
Imperialism (Oxford, 1988), 5.
²¹³ See Rangarajan, Fencing the Forest, for a discussion of this problem.
²¹⁴ See e.g. Grove, ‘Early Themes in African Conservation: The Cape in the 19th
Century’, where he encompasses in one continuous explanation the emergence not only of
forestry regimes but of concern with species extinction and the enactment of laws on
animal conservation across the 19th century.

--- Page 124 ---

colonial administration, this was manifested in demands by foresters for
greater political autonomy, a plea for freedom to devise and implement
policy in the realm of the forest. Central to such demands by the colonial
forestry community was their belief that, as technical experts, they were
the only people within the colonial administration competent to admin-
ister the imperial forest estate. For members of the colonial administra-
tions, on the other hand, the efﬁcient management of forests was only one
of many governmental priorities, not the least of which was to maintain
social and agricultural stability.
An important consequence of this polarization was an attempt by the
empire community as a whole to lobby governments at the centre and the
periphery to recognize the importance of forestry. They therefore used
their growing status as scientists to widen the existing governmental
perspective on forests and their utility. They also used their scientiﬁc
expertise to demonstrate the utility of supporting continental forestry
and allowing foresters to take over all decision making on forest use. They
were partially successful in this in the aftermath of the First World War.
Their campaign during and after the war and the implications of it for
pan-colonial forestry in the twentieth century are the subject of the next
two chapters.
The Empire of Nature
107

--- Page 125 ---

¹ James, A History of English Forestry, 194–6.
² Oxford English Dictionary Online Second Edition, 1989. Entry for vert ‘coupled
with venison. (The common use.)’.
4
The Empire Strikes Back
4.1. INTRODUCTION
By the turn of the century, forestry had joined the bureaucracy, the
army, and the railways as a permanent ﬁxture in the British imperium.
The empire forestry community had grown both in size and in conﬁdence,
having demonstrated the ability to generate revenue for the colonial state.
It had also begun to discover a new sense of professional identity, and
promote the cause of modern forestry with an evangelical zeal.¹
In the meanwhile, forest management in mainland Britain was mired
in tradition. For the landed classes, the principal cultural signiﬁcance
of forests stemmed from the harbour they provided for game, especially
foxes and birds. The historical importance that the British elite attached
to vert and venison is evident in the venerable Oxford English Dictionary:
1455 Rolls of Parlt. V. 319/2 The oversight of verte and venyson, in all the Parkes.
1577 HOLINSHED Chron. II. 459/2 The k[ing] . . . appointed foure iustices . . .
to be as surueyers aboue all other Foresters of vert & venison. 1598 J. MANWOOD
Lawes Forest xvii. 102b, That which tendeth to the hurt and annoyance of the
Vert and the Venison. 1612 SIR J. DAVIES Why Ireland, etc. (1747) 164 The great
plenty both of Vert and Venison within this land. 1700 TYRRELL Hist. Eng. II.
820 Every Forester in Fee shall . . . Attach Pleas of the Forest, as well concerning
Vert as Venison. 1772 Junius Lett. lxviii. (1788) 347 If a man was taken with vert,
or venison, it was declared to be equivalent to indictment. 1835 J. P. KENNEDY
Horse Shoe R. xxxvii, He gave much of his time to the concerns of vert and
venison. 1840 Penny Cycl. XVI. 175/1 The master-keepers’ and groom-keepers’
duty is to preserve the vert and venison in their respective bailiwicks and walks.²
This fascinating etymological history, attributed to Junius, says a great
deal—not only about attitudes to woods and game, but also about the

--- Page 126 ---

The Empire Strikes Back
109
social relationships between the landlords and the peasants. Although
commoners, in theory, enjoyed the right to graze their animals on the
forest ﬂoor, these relations were, in practice, fraught with conﬂict and
tension. Customary rights were often denied by the upper classes seeking
to maintain their exclusive privileges for recreation and sport. Moreover,
from the eighteenth century onward, encroachments in the woodlands
were met with severe punishment.³ The consequent lack of access to
crucial forest products and grazing lands forced many to leave their
homes. Forests, therefore, represented both injustice and disinheritance.
According to a group of Canadian geographers cited by James Winter,
so angry were some poor people about the exclusive rules over access to
woodlands imposed by some landowners that they made it a point, when
they went as settlers to countries such as Canada, deliberately to fell trees
in revenge.⁴
Forests, in any event, were, for the British elite, more than merely
resources for timber. In a tradition that arguably crystallized around
the mid eighteenth century in the plantations of Thomas Brudenell,
Lord Bruce, and his chief landscape consultant, Lancelot ‘Capability’
³ Thomas, Man and the Natural World, 198–211.
⁴ James Winter, Secure from the Rash Assault: Sustaining the Victorian Environment.
(Berkeley and Los Angeles, 1999), 96–7.
Figure 4.1. Conference train, Second Empire Forestry Conference, Canada.

--- Page 127 ---

The Empire Strikes Back
110
Brown, and which continued thereafter, woodland management, for the
aristocracy, meant the marriage of the groom (utility) with the bride
(beauty).⁵ It is important to point out that the groom, in the British
context, bore little resemblance to his continental European counterpart.
As James Winter points out:
Standardization was not essential to craft work and early mechanical industry,
especially since shipbuilders, wagon makers, and furniture craftsmen required
‘bends’ and ‘knees,’ as well as other eccentrically shaped pieces.⁶
In contrast to the modern forestry systems of the Continent, the British
landed classes relied largely upon a management practice called coppice—
with standards that involved partial cutting, regrowth, and subsequent
harvest. The traditional systems of forest management in Britain thus
catered to a variety of users of wood, ranging from the domestic to the
industrial and the naval, while at the same time preserving game.
During the second half of the century, however, a new movement
began, aimed at totally reorienting British attitudes to woodland manage-
ment. Initially, the key players were a number of self-appointed advocates
who promoted the cause of forestry, while at the same time criticizing, with
great rhetorical ﬂourish, the attitudes of the traditionalists who resisted
change in forest practices. One such was the proliﬁc John Croumbie
Brown, who, having returned from South Africa, wrote in 1884:
In Britain, game was the object of preservation, arboriculture the method, the
tree the unit, and amenity the priority; whereas, across the channel, the woods are
the object of preservation, silviculture the means, the forest collective the unit,
and the national economic good, the priority. It is true to abandon tradition and
do as the others do.⁷
Similar contrasts between British and continental attitudes continued to
be made throughout the second half of the nineteenth century and even
in the opening decades of the twentieth. For example, John Simpson
wrote in 1908 that the Germans were not burdened by such ‘false senti-
ments and false aesthetics’. The ‘half-military organization of the German
forestry system’, he argued further, helps ‘considerably to promote
economy and to avoid waste’.⁸
Meanwhile, the Royal Scottish and the Royal English Arboricultural
societies, established respectively in 1854 and 1880 with patronage from
⁵ James Winter, Secure from the Rash Assault: Sustaining the Victorian Environment, 84.
⁶ Ibid. 87.
⁷ Brown, Introduction to the Story of Modern Forest Economy, 1–2.
⁸ Winter, Secure from the Rash Assault, 88–9.

--- Page 128 ---

The Empire Strikes Back
111
some landlords interested in increasing revenue, had sought to modernize
British forest management practices. The earlier date of origin of
the Scottish society is not accidental. Scotland, which had considerable
wastelands, offered landlords opportunities to experiment with new
species and methods of increasing revenue. To this end, they fervently
strove to set up, among other things, a modern forestry school. As a result
of their lobbying efforts, and those of their English counterparts later in
the century, a parliamentary special committee was appointed in 1885 to
examine the matter. Parliament was dissolved before it could complete its
deliberations, but a subsequent committee strongly advocated the setting
up of a forestry school in its ﬁnal report published in 1886.⁹
Despite the fervency of these recommendations, no action was taken
by the government to implement them. The only concrete forestry
programme established during the late nineteenth century was the one at
the Royal Indian Engineering College at Cooper’s Hill in 1885—and this
school was funded by the India Ofﬁce to service the needs of the Indian
government.¹⁰ However, the Cooper’s Hill school received numerous
requests from British landowners to send their sons to study there.
The empire had begun to strike back. Around the turn of the century,
against the backdrop of the growing movement to modernize British
woodlands, expatriate colonial foresters, on furlough or retirement,
helped guide, train, and co-ordinate the activities of enthusiasts in the
mother country. In doing so, their relationship with the ‘amateur’
foresters in Britain was very similar to that between amateurs and profes-
sionals in other scientiﬁc domains, such as plant ecology, during the late
Victorian period. In that context, amateur, but wealthy, science enthusi-
asts had provided ﬁnancial and logistical support to trained professional
savants, in exchange for formal instruction and a chance to partake in
the practice of science.¹¹ In the case of forestry at the turn of the century,
the amateurs were often landlords with active, material interests in the
scientiﬁc management of their woodlands.
Foresters from the empire also became sought after as experts by vari-
ous governmental agencies in Britain. H. C. Hill, a former conservator
of forests in India, for example, wrote the working plan for the Forest of
Dean in 1897.¹² E. P. Poppert, who like Hill had been conservator
of forests in India, became the ﬁrst consulting forester employed by
⁹ James, A History of English Forestry.
¹⁰ Ibid. 189–93.
¹¹ See e.g. P. D. Lowe, ‘Amateurs and Professionals: The Institutional Emergence of
British Plant Ecology’, Journal of the Society of Bibliography of Natural History, 7 (1976),
517–35.
¹² James, A History of English Forestry, 194.

--- Page 129 ---

The Empire Strikes Back
112
the Crown.¹³ Sir Wilhelm Schlich, the former inspector-general of forests
in India, was, as mentioned in Chapter 3, almost solely responsible for
developing the forestry curriculum at the Royal Indian Engineering
College where Indian forestry probationers were trained from 1885.
Schlich was also instrumental in establishing the inﬂuential Oxford
forestry school, and lectured widely, advising landowners on forestry
matters and preparing working plans for several large estates.¹⁴
With the increasing acceptance of expatriate professionals as experts in
the United Kingdom, forestry had metamorphosed from a provincial
‘tool of empire’ in India to a bustling profession, with an active lobby
in the metropolis.¹⁵ The growing inﬂuence of expatriate foresters also
resulted in a paradigm shift in how forest management began to be per-
ceived in Britain. At the outset, ex-colonial forestry experts convinced the
landed classes that the system of continental European forestry offered
the best method of managing woodlands. Soon, the various working
plans of British woodlands, prepared by the experts from the empire,
began to resemble their counterparts in France and Germany. Moreover,
a number of German forestry texts were translated into English, with the
aim of propagating continental forestry practices in the British Isles.¹⁶
By the end of the ﬁrst decade of the twentieth century, continental
forestry had begun to gain legitimacy among some important policy
makers in Britain. It had also begun to grow roots throughout the colonial
world, where forest departments were systematically established. These
departments were supported by a network of dedicated forestry training
and research institutions, including provincial training colleges for subor-
dinate staff, and the Imperial Forestry Institute at Oxford. Over and above
these‘brick and mortar’ institutions was a pan-colonial venue—the Empire
Forestry Conferences. For four decades following the end of the First World
War and the beginning of the process of decolonization, the Empire
Forestry Conferences literally served as the parliament for the British
colonial forestry community. As Appendix 3 indicates, the conferences
¹³ James, A History of English Forestry, 194.
¹⁴ Schlich Papers, IOR MSS. Eur. F/157. See also IOR L/PWD/8/202; L/PWD/8/279.
¹⁵ The term ‘Tool of Empire’ is used in the sense deﬁned by Daniel Headrick in Tools
of Empire.
¹⁶ Among the important translators were W. R. Fisher, an assistant professor of forestry at
Cooper’s Hill, who translated Der Forstschutz, a book on forest protection by Dr Richard Hess,
and Die Forstbenutzung, a book on forest utilization by Dr Karl Gayer. See Schlich’s Manual of
Forestry (vols. iv and v). Other signiﬁcant translators included John Nisbit, professor of
forestry at the West of Scotland Agricultural College, Fraser Story, editor of the Empire
Forestry Review, E. A. Nobbs, E. E. Fernandez, and A. Smythies. Each of these foresters was a
product of the Indian forest service. See James, A History of English Forestry, 195–6.

--- Page 130 ---

The Empire Strikes Back
113
periodically brought together the upper echelons of forest departments
from every region of the empire. They were the only institutionalized fora
in which empire foresters could systematically compare notes, take stock,
and collectively plan strategies for scientiﬁcally managing the forests of the
empire. The conferences also afforded the empire forestry community
the opportunity to lobby colonial political establishments for power and
control over the shaping of local and regional policies regarding natural
resource use. Indeed, given the presence of the leading forestry experts of
the empire, they assumed an aura ofprestige, and an invitation to host them
was seen by many colonial governments as a matter of honour.¹⁷
The conferences, thus, provide an excellent window to observe the big,
overarching debates on colonial environmental policy in the ﬁrst half of
the twentieth century. They are ideal sites for gleaning a comparative
understanding of the pan-colonial forestry agenda. They are also indicative
of the natural resource management priorities of the senior echelons of
the empire-wide forestry profession. Besides, they afford a glimpse into
the role played by professional scientiﬁc communities in the shaping of
colonial governance.
Broadly speaking, the agenda of the Empire Forestry Conferences may be
classiﬁed under two categories: forest policy, and forestry science and
management.¹⁸The objective of this chapter is to describe systematically the
debates that ensued under the policy rubric. It has two parts—the political
contexts in which the conference agendas were set, and the actual debates on
forest policy. The next chapter will carry forward the discussion by focusing
on the debates on forestry science and management at the conferences.
4.2. THE CONTEXTS OF THE EMPIRE 
FORESTRY CONFERENCES
In 1895, Chancellor Bismarck gave the visiting British Prime Minister
Gladstone an oak sapling—at ﬁrst glance, a rather odd present to one head
of state from another.¹⁹ Needless to say, the gift was full of symbolism.
¹⁷ See the correspondence between the Standing Committee and the governments of
Australia, New Zealand, South Africa, and Canada (Minutes of the Meetings of the
Standing Committee on Empire Forestry, PRO F18/87, 29 May 1919– 1 Aug. 1935 and
F18/201, 8 Aug. 1938–7 May 1945).
¹⁸ I use the term ‘forest policy’ strictly in the way the forestry community used it. The
term ‘forestry science and management’, however, is my expression to designate a broad
range of activities relating to the science of forest growth and management.
¹⁹ Thomas, Man and the Natural World, 197.

--- Page 131 ---

The Empire Strikes Back
114
Prussia prided itself on helping devise many of the practices that constitute
modern forest management. Gladstone, on the other hand, was wellknown
as a promoter of tree-felling exhibitions, a British tradition stemming from
the view ‘that to cut down trees was to strike a blow for progress.’²⁰
Against this backdrop, the events that unfolded at the Guildhall in
London barely two decades after Gladstone’s visit—on 7 July 1920 to be
exact—were quite revolutionary. Gathered under one roof, at the heart of
the capital, and with governmental sponsorship, were the leading practi-
tioners of scientiﬁc forestry in the British Empire, along with a host of
senior politicians and civil servants. Their task—to discuss a strategy for
the effective conservation of the forests of the British Empire. Two years
later, on 25 July 1922, a similar scene was re-enacted, this time in distant
Ottawa.²¹
The growing inﬂuence of expatriate colonial foresters undoubtedly
provides a part of the explanation of this transformation in British
attitudes to forestry. However, the timing was a result of a more ‘cosmic’
event—the First World War. The war forced colonial governments,
bureaucracies, and politicians seriously to rethink science policy in
Britain and the empire. At the same time, it created the perception of a
forestry crisis, with important security implications.
4.2.1. Changing Attitudes to Science Policy
The opening speeches in both London and Ottawa, delivered by leading
politicians and senior civil servants, had one resonant theme—‘the lesson
of the First World War’. Speaker after speaker claimed that the war had
demonstrated the urgent necessity for taking serious action on colonial
forestry. The Lord Mayor of London, while welcoming the ﬁrst conference,
declared that Britain would ﬁnd itself in ‘dire straits’ unless forestry was
given urgent attention.²² Lord Milner, of the Colonial Ofﬁce, similarly
²⁰ Thomas, Man and the Natural World, 197. See also Winter, Secure from the Rash
Assault, 92, where there is a photograph of Gladstone in 1878 admiring a boiler saw at work.
²¹ The ﬁrst Empire Forestry Conference was held in London from 7 to 23 July 1920.
The second Empire Forestry Conference was held in Canada from 25 July to 7 September
1922. The conferences were held subsequently in Australia and New Zealand in 1928,
South Africa in 1935, London in 1947, and Canada in 1952. The conferences continue to
be held (albeit with the term ‘commonwealth’ substituted for ‘empire’), and constitute one
of the biggest international fora for discussion on forestry-related matters.
²² The Lord Mayor of London, ‘Welcome Address to the First British Empire Forestry
Conference’, First British Empire Forestry Conference: Proceedings, Statements (London,
1920) (henceforth First Empire Forestry Conference), 1.

--- Page 132 ---

The Empire Strikes Back
115
referred to the ‘great shake-up’ of the war.²³ Again, W. L. MacKenzie
King, the Prime Minister of Canada, in his speech opening the second
conference, emphatically stated that the war had clearly exposed the
British ‘weakness and shortcoming’ with regard to forests and forestry.²⁴
To these high-proﬁle speech makers, the ‘forestry crisis’ was a barometer
of a wider problem. As early as the turn of the century, there was the
perception, in some quarters, that the resources of the empire were
languishing and not being systematically put to use to serve imperial
ends. Joseph Chamberlain, who was the Secretary of State for the
Colonies from 1895 to 1903, for example, had attempted to deploy
science and public investment to make more productive the ‘underdevel-
oped estates’ of the tropical empire.²⁵ Moreover, beginning in 1887, a
series of Colonial and Imperial Conferences were organized in a bid
toward effecting greater imperial consolidation, although the main thrust
of their agendas was defence.²⁶
Imperial consolidation was by no means a matter of consensus. On
the contrary, two radically opposed points of view cutting across
party lines—those of the laissez-faire free traders and the Imperial
Visionaries—jostled to control Britain’s imperial economic policy during
the 1920s.²⁷ This was against the backdrop of the perceived weaknesses
of Britain relative to its competitors. Indeed, the First World War was being
widely interpreted as having demonstrated that Britain was unable to meet
its manpower, ﬁnancial, and economic needs from existing local and
imperial resources. In particular, it was seen as having revealed what was
described as the ‘empire’s dangerous dependence’ on foreign manufactures
and markets.²⁸ During the conﬂict, Britain found itself critically depend-
ent on countries such as Germany for a range of vital raw materials and

The Empire Strikes Back
116
synthesized products.²⁹ Moreover, the exclusion of Germany from trade
with Britain at the onset of the war had a profound inﬂuence upon the
economic health of the empire. Prior to the war, a great deal of British
colonial exports had been to Germany. All of British West Africa’s huge
production of palm kernels, for example, had gone to that country. The
war forced the creation of new markets for British colonial products.
Agencies such as the Imperial Institute were therefore charged with
actively developing new markets in Britain for these products.³⁰
In this context, members of the war governments of the British Empire
began to argue that Germany’s dominance in many branches of industry
was the result of government-sponsored research and development. They
claimed, further, that the concentration there of certain critical sectors of
industry had placed Britain at a disadvantage in its war effort. These argu-
ments reinforced the claims of those who, prior to the war, had been
advocating state-activated development for both economic and military
security. Many specialist and popular periodicals, as well as pressure groups
and ofﬁcial inquiries, endorsed this position. In doing so, they launched a
vigorous critique of what they regarded as antiquarian anti-science attitudes
among certain dominant sections of the British ruling classes.
A case in point is an opinion editorial published in the New Statesman
on 5 June 1926, which, signiﬁcantly, found its way into the clippings
ﬁle of J.H. Oldham, a civil servant, missionary, and a leading advocate of
African affairs. The article, entitled ‘Science to the Rescue’, argued, using
coal mining as the case in point:
We are ‘up against economic facts,’ Lord Grey tells us. The coal costs so much to
produce and is worth so much less when it is sold; and to this fact we must yield.
There is no answer unless we turn to science, in which our statesmen and
economists and capitalists are not interested, having learnt about chemistry at
school, once and for all, that its proper name is ‘stinks’ and that a man of science
is a bloke, as distinguished from a gentleman.³¹
²⁹ M Wiener, English Culture and the Decline of the Industrial Spirit (London, 1985);
C. Barnett, The Audit of War: The Illusion and Reality of Britain as a Great Nation (London,
1986); S. Pollard, The Development of the British Economy, 1914–1990 (4th edn. London,
1992); D. S. L. Cardwell, The Organization of Science in England (London, 1957);

through Scientiﬁc Research, 1895–1940’ (Ph.D. thesis, University of Wisconsin, 1941),’

History of the DSIR, 1914–16’, Minerva, 8 (1970), 192–217.
³¹ Lens, ‘Science to the Rescue’, New Statesman (5 June 1926), 193. J. H. Oldham
