--- Page 134 ---

The Empire Strikes Back
117
Having gone on to say that the only solution to the ‘national crisis’ lay in
obtaining more value from Britain’s coal through research and development,
the editorial launched a barely disguised critique of the Oxbridge orthodoxy:
The chemist looks at this cheap and dirty black stuff with fascinated admiration
and awe. It is a product of life and is crammed with the inimitable achievements
of that master chemist. You may burn it as you might burn the contents of the
Bodleian Library, and you will get something out of it accordingly. In either case
your fuel is really a priceless collection of manuscripts, ignorantly thought of as
merely so much combustible carbon and hydrogen. In such terms, the Bodleian
would not fetch many shillings per ton. The scholar who would be aghast in the
one case is quite serene in the other, for when it comes to deciphering the natural
manuscripts of coal he is quite illiterate, poor fellow. Only the scientiﬁc bloke
whom we call a chemist—using the same term for those who dispense drugs to
doctor’s prescriptions, a signiﬁcant fact marvelled at by a great German chemist
many years ago—only he knows what sacrilegious waste is consummated in the
burning of coal. When we contemplate the miners to-day, we see that in such
a holocaust we waste not merely the lives of giant ferns of long ago but the life and
blood of hosts of men today.³²
Such arguments were reproduced in a variety of fora. The inﬂuential, if
maverick, botanist and city planner Patrick Geddes, for example, wrote, in
a well-publicized and reviewed book produced by the sociological society,
that Britain had produced the great pioneers of the new industrial age and:
Nor is their type exhausted; our physicists, chemists, mechanicians, and so on are
at least holding their own. So the difﬁculty is not with them, but in that robust
immunity to science, and even to its applications, which still characterises our
public and its so-called ‘Educated and Governing Classes’ alike; in a word, the
Palaeotechnic mind. Such backwardness is, of course, still all too common
everywhere; but still with us it is entrenched to a degree which makes us the very
wonder of surrounding peoples; not simply of Germans and Americans, but of
those we are pleased to think ‘small,’ or even ‘backward’ nations.³³
The weight of such cumulated and orchestrated opinion led eventually
to an increased governmental acceptance of economic strategies that con-
tained a role for science—both in Britain and within the imperial
realm.³⁴ Signiﬁcant resolutions in this regard were those of the Imperial
War Conference of 1917 and on empire migration in 1918, which

³³ Patrick Geddes, in P. Abercroumbie, V. Branford, C. Desch, P. Geddes, C. W.

³⁴ Constantine, ‘Britain and the Empire’, 263. See also Havinden and Meredith,

mooted the idea that British and imperial producers needed to be
provided with expert scientiﬁc assistance.³⁵ In the words of Viscount
Milner, the Colonial Secretary at the close of the war, the application of
science to the development of colonial resources was the ‘most urgent and
vital’ question facing those territories.³⁶
Ultimately, political establishments across the empire, from the
Colonial Ofﬁce and Whitehall to the corridors of power in places such as
New Delhi and Pretoria, began to perceive the need to organize and
extend scientiﬁc research for a more systematic exploitation of the
empire’s resources. Support for colonial scientiﬁc research was thus seen
as being crucially important for the very well-being of the empire. A number
of initiatives promoting colonial science were consequently launched and
the colonial scientiﬁc departments enlarged. Moreover, a systematic
study and survey of imperial scientiﬁc research was commissioned.³⁷
In the meanwhile, a refurbishment of the British scientiﬁc infra-
structure got underway. In 1915, an Advisory Council on Scientiﬁc and
Industrial Research was established. It was composed of eminent scient-
ists and industrialists and held accountable to a small committee of the
Privy Council.³⁸ It is important to note here that the Privy Council was
composed of present and past holders of high governmental ofﬁce,
together with other persons of distinction. Originally the king’s advisory
council, its main business was the transaction of certain formal acts
of state. Its leading ministerial member was the Lord President of
the Council, who was a senior member of the government with no
departmental duties but was available for any job the Prime Minister
assigned him.
Such an arrangement provided the Advisory Council with a constitu-
tional basis for its existence and a Cabinet minister to look after its interests,
while at the same time leaving it free to think and act largely independent
of ministerial control.³⁹ In 1916, a separate Department of Scientiﬁc and
Industrial Research (DSIR) was created. It was guided by the Advisory
Council on Scientiﬁc and Industrial Research and operated under the
loose supervision of the Lord President and the Privy Council Committee

on Scientiﬁc and Industrial Research.⁴⁰ An important mandate of the
department was to relate its work to the overseas empire, with particular
emphasis on improving the supply of tropical raw materials for British
industries.⁴¹
A year after the creation of the DSIR, the Dominions Royal
Commission, which had been established as early as 1912, ﬁled its report.
It advocated the creation of an Imperial Development Board, charged
with the task of investigating the production of the needed materials or
their substitutes from within the empire. It recommended that the func-
tions of this board be conﬁned exclusively to advising on matters of trade
and commerce, without interfering with colonial policy. The goal of the
board, it suggested further, ought to be to consider the means for placing
the empire in a position that would enable it ‘to be independent of outside
control’ with respect to raw materials and commodities essential for its
safety and well-being. To this end, it advocated a three-pronged survey to
determine the ‘matters of the world’s requirement within the empire,
matters of the empire’s requirement produced in the empire and matters
of the empire’s requirement produced outside the empire’.⁴² It recom-
mended that the Imperial Institute carry out the necessary research for
such a board.
Traditionalists in the government sceptical of any Imperial Economic
Union resisted the speciﬁc proposal to establish such a board, preferring
instead to let ad hoc bodies deal with issues. However, the question of
state support for colonial scientiﬁc research raised by the Dominions
Royal Commission was taken up by others, especially the committee
headed by Lord Balfour of Burleigh, which was appointed to advise on
post-war commercial and industrial policy.⁴³ The Balfour committee
recommended that special bodies be constituted to handle the commod-
ities important for the economic development of the empire. It argued
that these bodies could gradually grow into the sort of organization
proposed by the Dominions Royal Commission. This view was adopted
by the government, and, as a result, special organizations, such as the
Imperial Mineral Resources Bureau, the Empire Flax Growing Committee,

and the Empire Cotton Growing Corporation, were established in the
early 1920s.⁴⁴ In addition, a Department of Overseas Trade was created
and vested with the task of developing foreign as well as imperial trade,
in particular with exploring possible markets and sources of raw materials
in the colonies. Moreover, in 1926, the Empire Marketing Board was
established.⁴⁵
Other governmental initiatives towards supporting colonial scientiﬁc
research included the appointment in November 1920 of a committee
headed by Lord Chalmers to look into the possibility of more co-operation
between British universities and colonial research projects. In its report
in April 1921, the committee recommended that British universities
train more scientists to investigate colonial problems.⁴⁶ In addition to the
creation of such institutions, sums were appropriated for scientiﬁc
research throughout the empire. This was over and above the many
existing grants for particular research agencies and bureaux. Money was
also set aside at various times for organizations such as the Colonial
Research Fund, the East African Guaranteed Loan, the Empire Marketing
Grant, and the Colonial Development Fund. Committees were consti-
tuted to decide where and how these funds ought to be spent.⁴⁷ Moreover,
the dominions too saw the need for treating research in all parts of the
empire as one unit. In India, the Indian Board of Scientiﬁc Advice, a body
composed of the heads of the Indian government’s scientiﬁc departments,
established direct relations with the DSIR.⁴⁸
Once these new post-war scientiﬁc research agencies began functioning,
planners in Britain turned their attention to the organization and 
systematization of the entire research structure. The 1920s were an era
when colonial research began to be co-ordinated and organized into a
general system of imperial scientiﬁc services. Whereas imperial science
policy before the war had consisted in unconsciously creating ad hoc
bodies for scientiﬁc research, as and when the need was felt, the post-war
era saw the systematization of these institutions in a deliberate effort to
create a uniﬁed and effective imperial scientiﬁc service. The prevailing
economic conditions furthered such an effort. In the ﬁve years, 1924–9,
the revenue of each colony increased at an average 25 per cent. This meant
that more money in the treasuries was available for scientiﬁc research

and that the colonies could sponsor the creation of bigger scientiﬁc
departments.⁴⁹
Among the most important efforts of co-ordination in the 1920s was
the organization of a series of empire scientiﬁc conferences. At the outset,
imperial conferences were convened on forestry, entomology, botany, and
mycology. Subsequently, conferences began to be organized on a host of
other topics, such as cotton- and sugar-growing problems, and agricul-
tural and soil research.⁵⁰ The recommendations of these meetings were
supported by the non-scientiﬁc conferences of the empire. The Imperial
Economic Conference of 1923, for example, passed a resolution favour-
ing greater co-operation in the common scientiﬁc and technical problems
of the territories. Again, the Imperial Conference of 1926 was the ﬁrst of
the general imperial conferences to consider the organization of research
among the primary subjects for discussion.⁵¹
4.2.2. The First World War and Forestry
During the second half of the nineteenth century, Britain developed a
voracious appetite for overseas timber. Imports trebled between 1864 and
1899, when they amounted to about ten million tons. This was in addition
to the imports of softwood for making paper.⁵² By 1914, 93 per cent
of wood products on the domestic market were imported.⁵³ By the time of
the outbreak of the First World War, Britain was almost exclusively depend-
ent on imported timber, a commodity vitally necessary for the production
of weapons, ships, planes, scores of other war industries, the construction
of camps and trenches, and coal mining. But perhaps more vitally, ship-
ping space urgently needed for carrying food and military supplies had to
be used to import wood. Timber accounted for 11.6 per cent of total
imports per annum during the period 1903–13.⁵⁴ When the war broke
out, 13 per cent of British tonnage space was being devoted to importing
timber, a space equal to that occupied by grains, and larger than the
combination of all other food, cotton, and wool imports.⁵⁵
With the onset of the war, large areas of woodland began to be felled in
Britain in a desperate effort to reduce imports. Despite this, the shortage
⁴⁹ Ibid. 229.
⁵⁰ Ibid. 230.

was so acute that in 1916 Britain started buying standing forests in France
to supply the needs of its army in the ﬁeld.⁵⁶ Forests were secured in the
Jura and in northern and eastern France, and some Canadian lumberjacks
were transferred to France to exploit them. In early 1917, a Scottish
landowner and forestry advocate, Lord Lovat, was appointed by Lord
Derby, the Secretary of State for War, to work the French forests to supply
Britain’s war needs.⁵⁷ Lovat, who will ﬁgure considerably in this story, was
one of the leading landowners to begin experimenting with afforesting
the Scottish wastelands.⁵⁸
The fact that Britain faced an acute timber crisis—which was proving
a wartime liability—was not lost on many observers. A future member of
the Forestry Commission argued that the crisis in the supply of timber
almost brought the Allied war effort to a halt in the ﬁrst half of hostilities.
He wrote further:
If the French themselves had in the past shewn as little foresight as we have done
in the matter of timber, I think it is true to say that the defence of the Western
Front would have been impossible, whatever men or guns had been at our
disposal.⁵⁹
The war thus resulted in the recognition of the importance of forestry
among many in the British elite. This recognition, in turn, led the
British government toward initiating a series of policy initiatives. In July
1916, a Sub-Committee on Forestry was set up under Lloyd George’s
Reconstruction programme.⁶⁰ This committee was perhaps the most
important to be constituted after Campbell Bannerman’s liberal ministry
had, in 1909, appointed a Royal Commission on Afforestation and
Coastal Erosion and, subsequently a developmental commission to over-
haul the administration of Crown forests.⁶¹ Its task was to consider and
report ‘upon the best means of conserving and developing the woodland
and forestry resources of the UK, having regard to the experience gained
during the war’.⁶²
The sub-committee was set up under the chairmanship of F. D. Acland,
then Parliamentary Secretary to the Department of Agriculture under

123
Lord Selborne. The Acland committee, as it became known, had thirteen
other members.⁶³ The core group, however, consisted of Sir Wilhelm
Schlich, Sir Roy Robinson, Sir John Stirling-Maxwell, and Lord Lovat.
Schlich, was of course, one of the most best-known ﬁgures in scientiﬁc
forestry in the United Kingdom. R. L. Robinson had been head of the
Joint Forestry Branches of the Board of Agriculture and the Ofﬁce of
Woods, and had had a distinguished career in Adelaide and Oxford
Universities studying pure science and forestry. F. D. Acland had been
chairman of the Home Grown Timber Committee, which was set up to
reorganize the supply of timber to meet the needs of the war, and had also
been secretary to the Board of Agriculture. J. S. Maxwell had pioneered
the introduction of methods of establishing trees on land normally
considered unplantable. Last, but by no means least, Lord Lovat had for
long been interested in rural social problems and linked their solution to
afforestation.⁶⁴
The Acland Report, which became in effect the ‘working plan’ of
British state forestry until 1945, was largely written by these four gentle-
men. Robinson drafted the technical part of the report, Acland, the
historical summary and introduction, Maxwell, the land settlement
chapter, and Lovat, the general plan outlined in the report. Signiﬁcantly,
the members of the Acland committee had had long personal associations
and formed a small cabal of closely knit advocates for the modernization
of Britain’s forests. It is therefore noteworthy, but certainly not surprising,
that apart from one memorandum from each of the forestry societies, no
evidence was taken. The ofﬁcial rationale was that ‘it was felt that suf-
ﬁcient information was already available in previous government reports,
in the transactions of the forestry societies and from the practical
experience of its members’. The latter is particularly signiﬁcant, because
all four were landowners who, from about the turn of the century, had
collectively lobbied for afforestation in Britain, and particularly for a
centralized agency to undertake such a charge. For more than a decade,
their lobbying efforts had been largely fruitless, having been effectively
opposed by a number of powerful individuals. The appointment to the
committee, in the context of a national resource crisis, therefore meant an
opportunity ﬁnally to act upon long-held beliefs.
The Acland Report pointed out that less than 8 per cent of the timber
consumed in the UK was grown at home. It claimed however that the
⁶³ James, A History of English Forestry, 210.
⁶⁴ Goodstadt, ‘The Work and Inﬂuence of the Forestry Commission’, 3–4. See also
Lindley, Lovat, 252.

--- Page 141 ---

The Empire Strikes Back
124
types of wood most in demand could be obtained from species easily
grown in Britain. It stated further that it was practicable to suggest replacing
imported wood with that grown in British forests for most of the uses to
which timber was put. Moreover, it felt that with increasing consumption
abroad and diminishing forests, the UK could not rely on timber imports
for its requirements. On the contrary, it could expect to face a severe prob-
lem with the development of a crisis in world supply. The report argued
further that dependence on imported timber was a grave source of weak-
ness in war and that supplies of timber were precarious even during times
of peace, and, for large part, lay outside the empire. It concluded that
afforestation would increase the productiveness of large areas of the
British Isles, which, it claimed, were then little better than waste.
Accordingly, it went on to recommend the establishment of a Forestry
Commission in Britain to co-ordinate and oversee the development of
forestry in Britain.⁶⁵
The recommendations of the Acland Report were accepted by the
government. In addition to being largely uncontroversial, the idea of state
afforestation had the initial advantage of the prime minister’s support. Lloyd
George, who had been responsible, as the chancellor of the Exchequer, for
funds being made available to forestry under the Development and Roads
Act of 1909, was a strong public advocate of forestry.⁶⁶Ultimately therefore,
not only did the government accept the report from the Ministry of
Reconstruction, it also carried it into law. In November 1918, a Cabinet
Minute set up an Interim Forest Authority to undertake the preliminary
work which would enable the Forestry Commission swiftly to carry out the
Acland Programme once the commission had been set up by the statute. On
29 November 1919, the Forestry Commission came into existence, with
Lord Lovat at the helm. Lovat’s ﬁrst accomplishment was to steer the com-
mission through the threat posed by what later became known as the Geddes
axe, named for the Geddes committee on national expenditure, which,
among other things, had recommended the abolition of the Forestry
Commission. Lovat’s success lay in his ability to enlist the support of his net-
work of friends in the Cabinet, including Edwin Montagu, then secretary of
state for India.⁶⁷The use of similar networks of friends, including Winston
Churchill, then chancellor of the Exchequer, and the deft appointment to
the commission of members of the Labour Party, enabled Lovat to ensure the

⁶⁶ Ibid. It must be noted here that Lloyd George was an important ﬁgure in the
governmental support for science during this period. See Alter, The Reluctant Patron, 209.
⁶⁷ Clapp, An Environmental History of Britain, 109; Lindley, Lovat, 255.

ﬁnancial survival of the commission through other challenges, such as the
change of governments that ensued.⁶⁸ The emergence of the Forestry
Commission in Britain, led by a powerful landlord and supported by a
network of well-connected elites, meant that foresters, perhaps for the ﬁrst
time, had acquired a degree of political clout.⁶⁹
4.2.3. The Origins of the Empire Forestry Conferences
It was during the deliberations of the Interim Forest Authority that the
idea of the Empire Forestry Conferences was born. On 14 May 1919,
Robson Black, the secretary of the Canadian Forestry Association, wrote
to Sir John Stirling Maxwell, who, as mentioned earlier, had served as
a member of the Forestry Sub-Committee of the Reconstruction
Committee, and subsequently as a member of the Interim Forest
Authority and the Board of Trade. In the letter, Black mooted the idea of
setting up an Imperial Forestry Association and periodic Empire Forestry
Conferences, an idea which, he claimed, his association had discussed with
its counterparts in Australia, New Zealand, and South Africa. He argued
that such a forum would help the exchange of information and ideas on
colonial forestry, besides helping clear up misconceptions on matters such
as the quality and uses of various exportable woods within the empire.⁷⁰
That such a suggestion should have come from the Canadian Forestry
Association is hardly surprising, for one of its ﬁrst accomplishments, in its
early years, was the organization of the Canadian Forestry Convention,
held in the Railway Committee Room of the Canadian parliament, 10,
11, and 12 January, 1906. At that conference, the then Prime Minister of
Canada, Sir Wilfred Laurier, had remarked:
A great deal of harm has already been done, harm which I am afraid, in many
respects, cannot be recalled. But it is not yet too late, and the harm which we
know has taken place is and ought to be an incentive to us to do our best in the
endeavour to check it and to give more attention to forestry problems. . . . Our
ancestors, when they came to this continent, found it an unbroken forest from
the shores of the Atlantic to the Mississippi valley . . . They had to clear their
homes from the forest, but instead of attacking the forest with care and tender-
ness, they looked upon it as an enemy to be got rid of with the axe, with ﬁre, and
with every mode of destruction . . . The forest had no friends whatever, because
⁶⁸ Lindley, Lovat, 257–8.
⁶⁹ See also G. B. Ryle, ‘History of the Forestry Commission’, RHO MSS Brit. Emp. S. 459.
⁷⁰ Mr Robson Black, Secretary, Canadian Forestry Association, to Sir John Stirling
Maxwell, Board of Trade, Edinburgh, Scotland, 14 May 1919, PRO F18/87, 29 May
1919–1 Aug. 1935.

--- Page 143 ---

The Empire Strikes Back
126
to clear off a few acres of land they would set ﬁre to miles upon miles of the
noblest trees that ever lifted their lofty heads to the heavens.⁷¹
Laurier’s remarks were, in part, a reﬂection of the growing interest in
forest management among the British expatriate elite. They were also in
keeping with the spirit of the progressivist conservation ideal that had by
then gained ascendancy in the United States, and North America more
generally. Indeed, present at that conference was none other than Gifford
Pinchot, who minced no words in setting out the broad thrust of such
an approach to forests and forestry, an unabashedly conservationist, as
opposed to preservationist, attitude to nature:
Finally, the end and aim of all this work is a very deﬁnite one. I have said a hundred
times that I have no interest in a forest that is not of use. If our forests are to stand
unused there, if all we get out of them is the knowledge that we have them, then, so
far as I am concerned, they disappear from my ﬁeld of interest. I care nothing about
them whatever. But use is the end of forest preservation, and the highest use.
Thegreat object of this whole movement, as the President has repeatedly said, is the
making and maintenance of prosperous homes. Our forest reserves are part of
the great equipment of our country for the good of its citizens, and just so far as we
use these forests to promote family life, to produce prosperity for the nation, to make
and maintain prosperous homes, just so far shall we think ourselves successful.
Such a perspective was attractive to colonial policy makers, some of
whom, like the then Canadian governor-general, Earl Grey, had begun to
argue, based on personal experience, for more sustainable approaches
to the exploitation of forest resources:
I have seen myself in India, in Asia Minor, in Greece and in Italy, extensive
tracts of territory once inhabited by a strenuous, prosperous, numerous popula-
tion, and now reduced to the misery of a barren desolation through the unregu-
lated deforestation of their lands by a blind and selﬁsh generation who had
no regard for posterity, and no eyes for anything but their own immediate
requirements. . . . Gentlemen, there are no more melancholy reﬂections than
those suggested by the sight of a country once rich and equipped with all the
majesty and panoply of power, which had become a waste and a stony desert
through the reckless improvidence of its own people.⁷²
The proposal to convene an Empire Forestry Conference, then, was an
idea whose time had come. But the speed with which the members of the
Interim Forest Authority, and the British government, more generally,
⁷¹ Website of the Canadian Forestry Association (www.canadianforestry.com/eng/
history/html/0main.htm).
⁷² Ibid.

--- Page 144 ---

seized upon the proposal was remarkable. Barely a fortnight after Black
wrote his letter, the authority accepted his proposal and recommended that
it be taken further.⁷³ Needless to say, the enthusiasm of the authority was
not surprising, for many of its members had formerly served in the overseas
empire, and had evinced strong interest in the state of forests and forestry.
On 6 June, replying to a letter from Acland on this issue, the Department
of Overseas Trade accepted the idea of convening an Empire Forestry
Conference, and deputed W. J. Glenny, the director of the UK Division, to
represent it in the proposed organizing committee.⁷⁴ An informal com-
mittee was subsequently constituted to discuss the organization of the
conference. It consisted initially of the usual suspects—Sir John Stirling
Maxwell, Lord Lovat, and Roy Robinson of the Interim Forest Authority,
and other forestry enthusiasts: Major Courthope and the Duke of
Buccleuch of the Royal Scottish Arboricultural Society, and Glenny.⁷⁵The
committee was subsequently expanded to include representatives from the
Timber Supply Department of the Royal English Arboricultural Society.⁷⁶
On 4 July, less than eight weeks after the initial Canadian proposal,
a preliminary meeting was held to discuss the idea of the Empire Forestry
Conference. In this meeting, the delegates recalled that the Forestry
Sub-Committee Report had emphasized the importance of the imperial
aspect of forestry. They argued that the idea of the Canadian Forestry
Association was seriously worth pursuing and felt that there would be no
treasury objection to such an enterprise.⁷⁷ Within less than a fortnight,
another meeting was held, this time to plan the proposed conference. The
majority of those who attended were the very same people who had
actively lobbied for the forestry commission in Britain—Acland who
acted as the chairman, Stirling Maxwell, who represented the Timber
Supply Department, and Lovat and Robinson who represented the
Interim Forest Authority.⁷⁸ In addition to these were Major Courthope
representing the Royal English Arboricultural Society, Mr Hart represent-
ing the India Ofﬁce, and Mr Scott the Department of Overseas Trade.
The Empire Strikes Back
127
⁷³ Minutes of the 26th Meeting of the Interim Forest Authority, 29 May 1919, PRO
F18/87, 29 May 1919–1 Aug. 1935.
⁷⁴ Department of Overseas Trade to Miss Baker King, 6 June 1919, PRO F18/87,

--- Page 145 ---

The planning meeting began by discussing logistics. It was decided
that the Colonial Ofﬁce would issue invitations, the Forestry
Commission organize the conference, and the treasury fund it. It was
resolved, next, to invite all the forest services and societies ‘whose chief job
was to promote the welfare of forestry’. These agencies included associ-
ations of ﬁrms trading in forest products, representatives of forest depart-
ments from the different parts of the empire, and eminent individuals
who had contributed to empire forestry. The agencies were the Royal
English Arboricultural Society, the Royal Scottish Arboricultural Society,
the Land Owners’ Association, the Surveyors’ Institution, the Land
Agents’ Society, and the Scottish Factors’ Society; the English and
Scottish Timber Federations, the Institute of Scottish Architects, and the
Imperial Technical College; the Department of Overseas Trade; the
Timber Supply Department; the Board of Trade; the India, Colonial, and
War Ofﬁces; and the Interim Forest Authority. The individuals invited
included representatives from Newfoundland, the UK, Canada,
Australia, New Zealand, South Africa, India, African Crown colonies,
other Crown colonies, and certain important individuals, e.g. Sir James
Ball and Sir Wilhelm Schlich. In choosing these individuals, care was
taken to ensure that members of the conference were in general ‘the
ofﬁcers actually in charge of forest administration in various parts of the
Empire’.⁷⁹
The thrust of the planning meeting was, however, on building an
agenda for the conference that would enable foresters and other ofﬁcials
to determine what the central management priorities for the imperial
forestry estate ought to be. Given the importance of statistical knowledge
about the state of forestry resources, it was decided to use the conferences to
compile data on the area and types of forest, the variety of species, and their
uses. The next agenda item to be ﬁnalized concerned patterns in the own-
ership of forests, and the balance between production and utilization in
different parts of the empire. A related set of questions on the agenda were
about the nature of the utilization of forest resources—such as the features
of trade, imports and exports of timber and wood pulp, and other forest
industries. Last, but by no means least, was the question of the logistics of
forest management. Important here were the state of forest ﬁnance, admin-
istration, management, publications, research, education, institutionaliza-
tion, and standardization. The latter included empire forest legislation,
education, the co-ordination and publication of empire forestry literature,
The Empire Strikes Back
128
⁷⁹ Minutes of meeting held on 15 July 1919, PRO F18/87, 29 May 1919–1 Aug. 1935.

--- Page 146 ---

the compilation of annual reports of various forestry departments, and the
standardization of methods of experimentation and research.
Having set the above agenda, the committee decided to adopt a
conference format that included discussions and excursions, and four
committees deliberating respectively on resources, utilization, policy, and
proposals for action.⁸⁰ In adopting these resolutions, the committee
basically endorsed the proposals made by R. L. Robinson in his circular to
the committee on 11 July.⁸¹ By 24 February 1920, invitations to the con-
ference had been dispatched.⁸² Four months later, the conference met in
London, and after what was seen as a major success, reconvened two years
later in Canada. The second conference, which was convened to complete
the task the ﬁrst conference had set out to do, had an agenda similar to the
ﬁrst. Both conferences were chaired by Lord Lovat.
4.2.4. Science, Colonial Development, and 
Conferences, 1928–1952
If the agendas of the early Empire Forestry Conferences were shaped
by the shake-up of the First World War, the context for the subsequent
meetings during the inter-war years was provided by the debate
over Britain’s ‘mandate’ in the colonies. This debate was, in turn,
inﬂuenced by the need felt by policy makers to tackle the growing
unemployment problem in the United Kingdom in the 1920s. Vocal in
the debate was an inﬂuential section of the colonial bureaucracy in
Britain, including Lord Milner, Leopold Amery, William Ormsby-
Gore, and J. H. Thomas.⁸³
The Imperial Visionaries, as they became known, believed that an
increase in primary production in the tropical dependent empire would
result in the growth of the British economy.⁸⁴ Underlying their vision was
the idea that the colonial and colonized worlds were complementary to
each other. At the Imperial Economic Conference of 1926, for example,
Leo Amery, the Secretary of State for the Colonies, stated that ‘one of the
most striking features of modern industrial development is the marriage
of tropical production to the industrial production of the temperate zone.
The Empire Strikes Back
129
⁸⁰ Ibid.
⁸¹ Ibid.
⁸² F. D. Acland, ‘Statement to the Forestry Commission’, Minutes of the Fifth Meeting
of the Forestry Commission, 24 Feb. 1920, PRO F18/87, 29 May 1919–1 Aug. 1935.
⁸³ Havinden and Meredith, Colonialism and Development, 140.
⁸⁴ Drummond, British Economic Policy and the Empire, 1919–1939, 40. The key ofﬁcials
involved were ‘the Imperial Visionaries’, led initially by Lord Milner and L. S. Amery.

--- Page 147 ---

They are essentially complementary regions, and owing to their character
and the character of their inhabitants they are likely to remain so.’⁸⁵ The
Imperial Visionaries argued that this complementarity would ensure that
colonial development provided a long-term cure for unemployment in
Britain.⁸⁶ Similar views began to be expressed in a variety of contexts. To
take one example, the Report of the Committee on the Stafﬁng of the
Agricultural Departments in the Colonies rationalized its recommenda-
tion for an expansion in stafﬁng by arguing that there would be a ‘rich
return to the individual colonies in shape of increased revenue and the
consequent increase in the supply of raw materials will help restore
the ﬁnancial position of the empire as a whole’.⁸⁷
Such views were contested throughout the 1920s by the treasury, and
by several individuals, within and outside the government.⁸⁸ However, in
a climate of political despair, with rampant unemployment and societal
anxiety, supplemented by labour strikes, the Imperial Visionaries kept
hammering home their contention that colonial development was indeed
the long-term solution to the woes in Britain. Accordingly, they intro-
duced a bill, the Colonial Development Act, which they defended ‘as a
practical step to help both operatives and industry in this country, and at
the same time to start a ﬂow of permanent trade by opening up undevel-
oped countries’.⁸⁹ The amount of money was not large, and the entire
project was mired in ad-hoc-ism.⁹⁰ However, the broad outlook persisted
through the 1930s and the early 1940s, in the context of the growing
shadow of the Great Depression.⁹¹ The Colonial Development Advisory
Committee, for example, argued that virtually any development in the
colonies—from railways to public health—would help both Britain and
the colony itself.⁹²
By the 1940s, the concept of colonial development began to be
expanded to include the welfare of colonial subjects, in addition to the
health of the British economy. In part, this expansion had to do with
thwarting the negative propaganda stemming from criticisms abroad,
The Empire Strikes Back
130
⁸⁵ Havinden and Meredith, Colonialism and Development, 169.
⁸⁶ Constantine, The Making of British Colonial Development Policy, 189.

(London, 1920), 8–10.
⁸⁸ Havinden and Meredith, Colonialism and Development, chapter 7.
⁸⁹ Constantine, The Making of British Colonial Development Policy, 189–90.

tively little attention to the proportion of their grants to be spent in the UK. Havinden and

--- Page 148 ---

especially in Europe and America, of the British ‘slum empire’.⁹³ There
were other reasons too, including worries, within Britain, about the
morality of colonialism.⁹⁴ These concerns were compounded by an
increasing fear, following the rise of nationalist movements, and the onset
of the resource-sapping Second World War, that the survival of the
colonial economy and the empire was at stake. An illustration of such
fears is the following statement made by the secretary of state in 1940:
if we are not now going to do something fairly good for the colonial empire,
and something which helps them to get proper social services, we shall deserve
to lose the colonies and it will only be a matter of time before we get what we
deserve.⁹⁵
Against this backdrop, the Colonial Development and Welfare Act,
described by one observer as ‘perhaps the ﬁrst unselﬁsh act in British
Imperial history’, was passed in 1940, and, in the aftermath of the Second
World War, new deﬁnitions of colonial developmental responsibility
began to be articulated in Britain. Among these was the idea of the dual
mandate, preserving British imperial interests while promoting a com-
prehensive policy of ‘progress’, linking economic development with
political stability.⁹⁶
The growing governmental commitment to colonial development
during the 1930s and the 1940s also translated into the adoption of a
deliberate policy of soliciting scientiﬁc advice. During this period, the
Colonial Ofﬁce sought to ensure that administrators in the colonial
periphery got sound technical advice on various issues, such as resource
management, organization, and communication.⁹⁷ Commissions such
as Ormsby-Gore (1924), Hilton Young (1928), and the Hailey Survey of
1934–7 emphasized the need for closer co-operation in research between
the several territories comprising the regions of Africa, and between the
European powers responsible for these regions.⁹⁸

⁹⁴ References to the Fabian Colonial Bureau documents, IMP.
⁹⁵ J. M. Lee and M. Petter, The Colonial Ofﬁce, War and Developmental Policy:
Organisation and Planning of a Metropolitan Initiative, 1939–1945 (London, 1982), 40.

African Research Survey and the British Colonial Empire: Consolidating Environmental,

At the same time, British expatriate scientists began systematically to
lobby for the recognition of their expertise and for more money to be
spent on building cadres of professionals in their respective ﬁelds. For
some of them, such recognition was crucial in light of what they saw as a
strategic competition with the United States, which, they feared, was
extending its inﬂuence in the tropics by the mid 1940s.⁹⁹ The majority
were, however, frustrated by the government’s lack of proactive support
for science, as the following comment by E. B. Worthington on science in
East Africa illustrates:
Everywhere in East Africa, except perhaps in the Groundnut Scheme, there is a
feeling of frustration among scientists in more or less degree. This was touched
upon in the Heilbron–Simonsen report and is largely caused by uncertainty
about the future. Some of the dissatisfaction is concerned with terms of service,
but more important is the recognition by many scientists of the magnitude of
the problems facing East Africa and the apparent inability of Governments to
solve them. The frustration is much less marked among administrative ofﬁcers
and those technical men who are concerned primarily with administrative
aspects of their subjects. The prevalent scientiﬁc feeling is that, in spite of large
talk, science and the scientiﬁc viewpoint are not adequately represented in
the deliberations of Governments, and there are practically no ofﬁcers in high
places who understand adequately the research workers point of view.¹⁰⁰
An important consequence of the public expression of such views and
the intense lobbying within the halls of government was the reorganization
of the Colonial Ofﬁce. Permanent subject advisers to the secretary of state
were appointed to provide an effective link between the specialist
departments in the colonial governments and professional bodies in the
United Kingdom. The personnel appointed as advisers were usually those
with a great deal of experience in the ﬁeld. By 1938, advisers had been
appointed in the ﬁelds of medicine, agriculture, labour, and law.¹⁰¹Inaddi-
tion to the appointment of subject advisers, the reorganization of the
Colonial Ofﬁce also involved the creation of departments that specialized
in particular subjects and geographical territories.¹⁰²During the 1930s and
much of the inter-war years, colonial developmental policy at the centre

sought to bring together the three elements of the colonial bureaucracy that
had hitherto little experience at working together—the geographical
departments, the subject departments, and the expert advisers.¹⁰³
The passage of the Colonial Development and Welfare Acts of 1940 and
1945 provided a further ﬁllip to the process of the consolidation of colonial
research. To take one example, in East Africa, the Colonial Research
Committee in March 1944 proposed a scheme that mooted the creation of
an East African Research Council with executive powers and authority to
spend money voted for research by the African territories, and from the
Colonial Development Fund. Although this particular plan did not meet
with a favourable reception at the Colonial Ofﬁce—even when modiﬁed
by reducing the executive functions of the proposed council—a number of
subsequent visits and conferences cleared the air. In March 1946, a team
concerned with agriculture consisting of Sir Frank Engledow, Sir Harold
Tempany, and Professor Munro visited East Africa. Subsequent initiatives
resulted in deﬁnite proposals, some of which were approved. They included
those by Professor P. A. Buxton in 1945 on the reorganization of the tsetse
ﬂy and trypnosomiasis research; by Professor McSwiney in September
1946 on medical research; by Sir Ian Heilbron and Professor Simonsen in
September 1946, on industrial and related research; by Dr Worthington
in April 1944 on inland ﬁsheries; by Mr Hickling in July to August 1946
on ﬁsheries generally; by Mr Robertson, December 1946 to February
1947, on forestry; and by Dr Keen, March to July 1947, on agriculture.¹⁰⁴
Meanwhile, the process of science policy planning continued in the
territories with the preparation of ten-year development plans, which
envisaged speciﬁc roles for scientiﬁc research to meet colonial develop-
ment goals. Implicit in these plans was the recognition of the need to
nurture scientiﬁc agricultural expertise and provide for the envisaged
expansion in stafﬁng. The expenditure involved in the provision of such
expertise was rationalized by arguing that a ‘rich return to the individual
colonies in the shape of increased revenue plus consistent increase in
the supply of raw materials will help restore the ﬁnancial position of the
empire as a whole’. For their part, colonial scientists began to press home
the argument that science policy could speciﬁcally address colonial devel-
opment, and lobbied for ﬁfty-year plans.¹⁰⁵ In doing so, they contended
The Empire Strikes Back
133
¹⁰³ Ibid.
¹⁰⁴ Worthington, ‘Regional Research in East Africa: A Draft Development Plan’,
Aug. 1947, RHO MSS Afr. S. 1425, 4/82, p. 2.

that ‘a wealth of material awaits research and upon the knowledge gained
depends the future of East Africa’.¹⁰⁶ This future, they argued, depended
on ‘achieving a satisfactory relation between the rates of population
increase and production increase, in order to provide an economic basis
on which the standard of living in the region as a whole can rise rather
than fall’.¹⁰⁷Such perceptions of the role of science in colonial development
were not restricted to East Africa, but fairly widespread across the
continent, and the empire at large. It is important to note here that ‘devel-
opment’ was understood, by most colonial scientists, to connote the
eradication of pests and diseases, the provision of adequate nutrition, and
the creation of efﬁcient regimes of natural resource management.¹⁰⁸
Colonial foresters did not lag behind their counterparts in the other
scientiﬁc services when it came to lobbying. As far back as January 1920,
E. B. Stebbing, formerly of the Indian forest service, and then head of the
Forest School at Edinburgh University, had met with Ralph Furse, the
recruiting ofﬁcer at the Colonial Ofﬁce.¹⁰⁹ During that meeting,
Stebbing pressed hard for the appointment of a scientiﬁc adviser to the
Colonial Ofﬁce in forestry. He argued that there was a real need for a
central forest adviser based in England who would be able to advise
government on the latest in forestry. Stebbing argued further that there was
need for better salaries and guaranteed jobs to attract the best students.¹¹⁰
There was a speciﬁc reason why Stebbing chose Furse to make such a
pitch to the Colonial Ofﬁce. During the autumn of 1920, in the imme-
diate aftermath of the ﬁrst Empire Forestry Conference, Furse had
been appointed to serve, as the Colonial Ofﬁce Representative, on the
Interdepartmental Committee that had been set up to establish the Imperial
Forestry Institute—an appointment that changed his attitude toward
forests and forestry. In his own words:
To me this episode was a liberal education . . . I could watch the methods of men
like Clinton and Lovat, trained in public affairs, politics and business; accus-
tomed to take decisions, to be concise, businesslike, and commonsensical . . .
I began to see the importance of forestry, hitherto a closed book to me . . . I dimly

began to realize, too, the immense possibilities of scientiﬁc forestry in the life and
economy of many colonies with their huge areas of natural forest, and how little
had been done for their conservation and management . . . for the next twenty
years . . . I devoted much of my time and energies to nursing and helping the
sorely neglected Colonial Forest Departments in ways which often went far
outside the scope of my proper duties.¹¹¹
Given this background, Furse was quick to agree with Stebbing. In a
memorandum to the Colonial Ofﬁce, he pointed out that there had in the
past been a number of precedents for the appointment of such expert
advisers. He referred especially to Wilhelm Schlich, who was appointed
in such a capacity by the India Ofﬁce, Sir David Drain, director of Kew,
who had been appointed to the Colonial Agricultural Service, and Sir
Francis Watts, to the governments of the West Indies. Last, but by no
means least, he concurred with Stebbing’s observation that better salaries
and guaranteed jobs were needed in order to attract the best students.¹¹²
Furse was not alone in making such a recommendation. In 1920, the
Colonial Ofﬁce appointed an interdepartmental committee on imperial
forestry education, which concluded, among other things, that training
foresters was a matter of priority.¹¹³ Not long afterward, Furse noted that
treasury ofﬁcers were up to date about the importance of training men
and organizing tropical forest products along scientiﬁc lines.¹¹⁴ Furse
himself held discussions with Col. H. S. Graves of the US forest service,
then dean of Yale Forest School, and Professor S. J. Secord, about
potential US demands for tropical wood—in a bid to explore the export
potential of British colonial forest products.¹¹⁵ Moreover, en route to the
third Empire Forestry Conference, Furse stopped in the colonies that lay
in the route to Australia and New Zealand in a bid to inventory the state
of forests and forestry therein.
As a result of lobbying by foresters and their supporters in the Colonial
Ofﬁce, the government began to explore the costs and beneﬁts of extend-
ing ﬁnancial support to the forestry profession. On 12 May 1928,
Leopold Amery, then Secretary of State, sent a circular dispatch on forest
recruitment from Downing Street to the governments of the Federated

Honduras, British Guiana, Trinidad, Cyprus, Ceylon, Mauritius, Fiji,
Northern Rhodesia, and Palestine. The circular asked whether they were
willing to pay for new forest ofﬁcers and their training.¹¹⁶ Amery’s memo
was in response to one of the recommendations of the Colonial Ofﬁce
Conference of 1927.¹¹⁷The Memorandum on Forestry therein, as well as
its Summary of Proceedings, had recommended as follows:
It was held very desirable that, with a view to securing ofﬁcers of suitable qualiﬁ-
cations, Colonial Governments should endeavour to supply the Colonial Ofﬁce
annually with a minimum estimate of their requirements for ﬁve years ahead.
Moreover . . . it was urged that it was extremely desirable that Colonies with
larger Forest Departments should be willing to absorb between them a few super-
numerary probationers from time to time, if and when the estimates of smaller
Forest Departments proved in excess of actual requirements. The Conference
expressed general agreement in these two recommendations.¹¹⁸
In essence, the argument built on those made by Stebbing in his inter-
locution with Furse—that the absence of clearly available jobs deterred
good candidates from taking up forestry.
In addition to recruitment, another major initiative on forestry under-
taken by the Colonial Ofﬁce was the establishment, after much discussion
of a recommendation of the second Empire Forestry Conference, of
a Standing Committee on Empire Forestry. The ﬁrst meeting of the
Standing Committee was held on 23 February 1926.¹¹⁹ The committee
served as an advisory body in the centre by communicating the recom-
mendations made at the periodic Empire Forestry Conferences to the
Colonial Ofﬁce and to governments in the colonial periphery. Its main
function was to organize and co-ordinate the conferences. During the
period between the First World War and the beginning of the process of
decolonization in the late 1940s and the 1950s, the Empire Forestry
Conferences, and their mouthpiece at the centre, the Standing Committee,
were the single most important source of advice to the Colonial Ofﬁce,
and indeed colonial governments, on forestry matters.
The Standing Committee saw the Empire Forestry Conferences,
which it helped organize, as proceeding in stages. The agenda of each
The Empire Strikes Back
136
¹¹⁶ Circular dispatch from L. S. Amery, Downing Street, to the aforementioned colo-
nial governments, 12 May 1928, Furse Papers, RHO MSS Brit. Emp. S. 415.
¹¹⁷ Proceedings of the Colonial Ofﬁce Conference, 1927, Cmd. 2883.
¹¹⁸ Appendix XV of Cmd. 2884, especially the remarks on p. 172, and the summary of
proceedings, p. 62.
¹¹⁹ PRO F18/87, 29 May 1919–1 Aug. 1935.

--- Page 154 ---

conference was drawn up consciously to build on that of the one preceding
it. By staging the conferences sequentially in different parts of the empire,
it hoped to review cyclically the main issues of colonial forestry in speciﬁc
regions and the empire as a whole.¹²⁰ It was concerned in particular with
thoroughly discussing the issues that the empire forestry community held
important. The committee therefore collated the various ideas about
topics for discussion in the conferences that emerged in other fora in which
colonial foresters deliberated on matters concerning empire forestry. Inthe
run-up to the third conference, for example, it drew up a list of issues to be
discussed therein on the basis of suggestions received from the Forestry
Special Sub-Committee of the Imperial Conference of 1926, the Inter-
State Forestry Conference held in Melbourne in 1927, and the Imperial
Institute in London.¹²¹The committee also attempted to facilitate discus-
sion in the Empire Forestry Conferences by standardizing the manner in
which participating foresters were to report on speciﬁc issues.¹²²
It is important to note that although there was representation from
certain governmental agencies—especially the India and the Colonial
Ofﬁces—the Standing Committee, which formally prepared the confer-
ence agendas, consisted largely of professional foresters or established
forestry advocates. The ﬁrst committee, for example, was composed of
the usual suspects—Lord Lovat (chairman), Lord Novar, representing the
Council of the Empire Forestry Association, R. L. Robinson of the
Forestry Commission, Professor R. S. Troup, the director of the Imperial
Forestry Institute, Oxford, Major Ralph Furse, the recruitment ofﬁcer
of the Colonial Ofﬁce and a major supporter of colonial forestry,
Mr E. J. Turner of the India Ofﬁce, Fraser Story, formerly of the Indian
forestry service and the editor of the Empire Forestry Journal, who served
as secretary to the committee, and E. H. Finlayson, the director of the
Canadian forest service.¹²³The composition of the Standing Committee—
in the sense of the agencies represented in it—did not change much
during the entire period of its existence.¹²⁴
The Empire Strikes Back
137
¹²⁰ Standing Committee ﬁles, PRO F18/201, 8 Aug. 1938–7 May 1945.
¹²¹ Minutes of the Second Meeting of the Standing Committee on Empire Forestry,

--- Page 155 ---

Given the central role played by the Standing Committee in the
organization of the Empire Forestry Conferences, it is reasonable to argue
that the choice of the agendas therein was made principally by the colo-
nial forestry community and its supporters. Moreover, the manner in
which the agendas of the Empire Forestry Conferences were formulated
by the Standing Committee reveals a commitment on its part to seek and
include the views of the colonial forestry community as a whole. For
empire foresters, the high proﬁle of the conferences, and the fact that they
were the principal window of colonial governments on matters related to
forestry, meant that they were an opportunity to put across their views
systematically to colonial governments and the public at large.
4.3. FORESTERS AND COLONIAL 
FOREST POLICY
To return to the opening speeches of the ﬁrst two conferences, the utter-
ances of foresters and public servants in those fora reﬂected two distinct
attitudes to imperial forestry. For its political patrons, the signiﬁcance of
the ﬁrst two conferences lay in their potential to address what was now an
issue of strategic importance. For them, the conferences had two imme-
diate objectives, to prepare a ‘doomsday book’ of the forest resources of
the British Empire as a whole and to begin a process of discussion and
debate that would lead to the emergence of strategies adequately to
develop and conserve empire forest resources in the long run.¹²⁵
It is important to note here that the Empire Forestry Conferences were
afforded the Royal Charter. They were also recognized by the Imperial
Conference, 1926, wherein it was also decided, on 19 October 1926, to set
up a Forestry Sub-Committee to consider Empire forestry questions and to
report to the conference thereon. Signiﬁcantly, that committee consisted of
the usual suspects—Lovat (chairman), J. Stirling Maxwell, R. L. Robinson,
and R. S. Troup—in addition to ofﬁcers representing the treasury, the
Dominions Ofﬁce, the DSIR, the Imperial Institute, the Scottish Board of
Agriculture, Australia, South Africa, Newfoundland, Canada, New
Zealand, India, and the colonies and protectorates.¹²⁶ Not surprisingly,
The Empire Strikes Back
138
¹²⁵ Lord Lovat, ‘Opening Address to the First Empire Forestry Conference’, First
Empire Forestry Conference, 4.
¹²⁶ Forestry Sub-Committee Report, Furse Papers, RHO MSS Brit. Emp. S. 415,
Box 2/8 Item 1, p. 1.

--- Page 156 ---

it noted that there was a consensus that the ‘outlook with regard to
the world’s supply of softwoods’, as well as hardwoods, was ‘distinctly
unsatisfactory’.¹²⁷ It argued, further, that ‘there are few subjects concerning
the general well-being of individual parts of the Empire, and of the Empire
as a whole, which require more earnest consideration by Statesmen than
this subject of the rational use and development of forests’.¹²⁸
The committee recommended that Australia and New Zealand be the
site for the third Empire Forestry Conference in 1928. It drew up a
desired agenda and a pro forma of the kinds of personnel it felt should
attend.¹²⁹ Next, it passed a resolution on empire settlement, urging the
development of ‘schemes of settlement in connection with state forestry
and afforestation, or of developing adequate afforestation schemes in
existing agricultural areas’.¹³⁰ Last, but by no means least, the committee
argued for the creation of an Imperial Forestry Bureau and the consolida-
tion of Empire forest products research.¹³¹
The ready acceptance of the proposals made by the Interim Forestry
Authority by the government is thus indicative of its increasing willing-
ness to solicit and heed expert scientiﬁc opinion. Indeed, at the very time
it agreed to convene the conferences, the government reacted favourably
to Stebbing’s aforementioned proposal to serve as a central forest adviser
based in England.¹³²
For the members of the British and imperial forestry community, this
new governmental attitude to scientiﬁc advice meant a chance to
promote and legitimize their perspective on how colonial forests
were to be best managed. To this end they adopted a pragmatic and
forward-sounding slogan—Prospice, Perspice—for the Empire Forestry
Association.¹³³ At the same time, they sought to discredit the attitudes
toward forests that they perceived as being held by civil servants and
administrators in Britain and the empire.
The Opening Address to the ﬁrst conference by Lord Lovat, then
chairman of the Forestry Commission of Britain, is a good example of
the latter motive. Lovat stated bluntly that Britain was ‘the least inter-
ested of all nations in forestry science’ but, ‘of all nations, the most
effective in the destruction of forest resources’. It was the last European
The Empire Strikes Back
139
¹²⁷ Ibid. 2
¹²⁸ Ibid.
¹²⁹ Ibid.
¹³⁰ Ibid.
¹³¹ Ibid., Item 1. See appendix IV, which details the work being done in this regard
across the empire during the period 1923–6.
¹³² Memorandum on the Colonial Forest Service, recording an interview with
Professor. E. B. Stebbing, Furse Papers, RHO MSS Brit. Emp. S. 415, Box 1/2, fo. 26.
¹³³ ‘Prospice’ is the Latin imperative of prospicio, which means ‘to look forward, look
ahead’. ‘Perspice’, similarly, means ‘look to the future’.

--- Page 157 ---

state to adopt a forest policy. It had the smallest area of state forest in
Europe, the least area of wood per head of population, and, with one
exception, the most poorly wooded country in proportion to area of
land. Lovat argued that this ‘attitude of indifference’ with regard to
forestry was peculiarly Anglo-Saxon and that it was ‘not local or period-
ical’ but a ‘permanent and racial’ characteristic. He argued further that
whereas in the Middle Ages forestry laws on the Continent were being
built up on national and constructive lines, forest laws in Britain had
been based on ‘injustice, cruelty and repression’. It is important to point
out here that Lovat, as a landlord, was unusual in that he apparently took
considerable interest in the welfare of the crofters on his estate.¹³⁴ Lovat
went on to claim that the British were ‘at heart forest vandals who look
on all woodlands as their prey’, and that it was such a mindset that
resulted in the ‘careless, indifferent and wasteful’ attitudes to forests
adopted by British ofﬁcials in Britain and the empire and consequently,
in forestry in parts of the world occupied by Britain being ‘haphazard
and unsatisfactory’.¹³⁵ It is plausible to argue that in making such a bold
statement, Lovat was perhaps venting his frustration at attitudes he had
generally encountered among the British elite throughout his long career
as a forestry advocate.
At the core of the alternative framework being articulated by Lovat and
his colleagues was a commitment to a particular paradigm of forest
resource development and conservation—whose origins lay in continental
Europe. Forests, in this view, were ‘grown for use and not for mere orna-
ment . . . Forests are national assets only so far as they supply the raw
material for industrial development.’¹³⁶
This paradigm, reminiscent of that advanced by Gifford Pinchot in
the United States, was articulated explicitly in the ﬁrst conference by
R. S. Troup, a former Indian forest service ofﬁcer and then the professor
of forestry at Oxford. Troup began by sketching a linear model of
the development of human relationship with forests, arguing that the
human–forest interaction in civilized societies usually went through
three distinct phases—destruction, conservation, and economic man-
agement.¹³⁷ Conservation was a ‘wise and necessary measure’ but it was
‘only a stage towards the problem of how best to utilise the forest
resources of the empire’. The ultimate ideal was economic management,
The Empire Strikes Back
140
¹³⁴ Lindley, Lovat, 153–65.
¹³⁵ Lord Lovat, ‘Opening Address to the First Empire Forestry Conference’, First
Empire Forestry Conference, 3–5.
¹³⁶ Sir Mayson H. Beeton, ibid. 185.
¹³⁷ Prof. R. S. Troup, ibid. 136.

--- Page 158 ---

epitomized by working plans, which regarded forests as capital assets,
ﬁxed annual yields in such a manner as to exploit ‘to the full the interest
on this capital without encroaching on the capital itself’, and aimed for
equal annual yields so as to stabilize the market and provide regular
supplies of timber to industry. Anything removed was to be made good
by regeneration.¹³⁸
Such attitudes, as argued in Chapter 2, had their roots in continental
Europe in the eighteenth century. They were also part of a wider world view
of forestry professionals internationally during the period of the ﬁrst two
conferences.¹³⁹ It was a mindset that regarded forestry as a tool to provide
resources for global industrialization. These new attitudes dovetailed well
with the objectives of the political patrons of imperial forestry. For the par-
ticipating foresters, the deliberations of the ﬁrst two conferences thus
assumed an important strategic signiﬁcance. They afforded a chance to
drive home the imperial importance of forestry to their political bosses, in a
context in which the latter were receptive to hearing about ideas to boost the
British economy via colonial development and trade. They also afforded an
opportunity to take the ﬁrst step towards producing a master plan for the
development and optimal use of empire forest resources.
Although the colonial forestry community had several practical issues
to discuss relating to the physical management of forests and their ecolo-
gies, a great deal of time in the conferences was spent on the question of
how to gain effective control over the imperial forest estate. The concern
over the domain of jurisdiction stemmed from the perception that
foresters on the ground had limited authority, especially when their
policies had the potential to stir anti-government sentiments among local
populations.¹⁴⁰This limited authority was further exacerbated by the fact
that foresters were subordinate to the more prestigious civil services, who,
in addition to yielding power and making the big decisions over colonial
land use, also enjoyed better salaries and beneﬁts. Both these grievances,
to recall the previous chapter, had a long history, going back to the early
days of institutionalized forestry in British India. The Empire Forestry
Conferences thus offered an opportunity to vent these frustrations and to
lobby for better conditions for work.
The Empire Strikes Back
141
¹³⁸ Ibid. 137–8. It is interesting to note that similar views were held on the ground. See
e.g. the diaries of A. J. Argyle, assistant conservator of forests in Malaya (RHO MSS. Ind.
Ocn. S. 168 1–3). See also Chipp’s notes and diaries—which show how strongly conserva-
tion was related to economic considerations (RHO MSS Brit. Emp. S. 311).
¹³⁹ See e.g. Fernow, A Brief History of Forestry.
¹⁴⁰ Such perceptions had a long genealogy. See Chapter 3 for a description of similar
views in the context of British India during the Victorian era.
