4.3.1. Forest Policy at the Empire Forestry Conferences
The conviction that forest use must be controlled and regulated by the
state was a core belief held by the British colonial forestry community
virtually from the onset of scientiﬁc forestry in India. As forest depart-
ments were set up in different parts of the empire, the question of
authority—the issue of the agency most suited to manage forests—was
among the ﬁrst issues raised by foresters. Yet, although non-foresters in
colonial establishments, especially civil administrators, increasingly
accepted the need for scientiﬁc forestry, they did not delegate the kind of
comprehensive authority sought by the foresters. For them, forest
management was only one element of the wider problem of colonial land
use, and the demands of the empire forestry community had to be
balanced against those of others, such as agriculturists and agricultural
ofﬁcers.¹⁴¹
The discussion on the question of control in the ﬁrst and second con-
ferences arose against this backdrop. The context was identiﬁed by a
number of speakers. The deposition of Edward Battiscombe, the East
African representative, is an example. Battiscombe argued that the apathy
of the government, the antagonism of white settlers, and lack of propa-
ganda meant that there was virtually no continuous forest policy in
British East Africa. The government, with its concern to ‘develop the
country’, was committed to giving out as much land as possible to poten-
tial developers. The settlers, in turn, could not see why the forests should
not be felled. Indeed, they often demanded commercial reasons to refrain
from cutting down the forests under their control:
We set out to know all about forests and we frequently come up against the
settlers. We boast as authorities, naturally, and they frequently argue, ‘How can
you be authorities when you can not tell me how much money I can get out of
a certain forest?’¹⁴²
To compound such conﬂicts, there was no provision in the Forest Act for
any policy at all. A change in a governor therefore meant a change in policy.
The Malayan representative gave a somewhat similar story. He claimed
that, as with East Africa, there was a lack of deﬁnite policy. On the con-
trary, there was a ‘casual parochialism’, each resident wishing to develop
the state to generate the biggest revenue. Again, because the people did
not know why there was a forest department and what its policy was, they
The Empire Strikes Back
142
¹⁴¹ See the discussion in Chapter 3.
¹⁴² E. Battiscombe, First Empire Forestry Conference, 9, 70.

--- Page 160 ---

resisted interference, which ‘naturally’ arose when land was reserved.
Moreover, the civil ofﬁcers of the government did not have a clear
understanding of why there was a forest department. There was in fact a
feeling among the civil service that the forest department were land grab-
bers. Besides, forest ofﬁcers were themselves unaware what the priorities
were. There was, for example, confusion on questions such as whether the
primary productive species to be planted ought to be rubber or timber,
and what the target for exploitation should be.¹⁴³
The discussion on the issue of the need for state-controlled forestry
was driven by one basic ideal—of a co-ordinated and continuous forest
policy, a policy that thought in the long term, and which was willing to
take risks and make investments for the future. According to this belief,
only the state, and in particular state foresters, had the responsibility and
the commitment to implement this vision. The debate on the question
of control in the ﬁrst and second Empire Forestry Conferences enabled
the participating colonial foresters to use their credibility as experts to
reiterate this vision, and forcefully argue for it. During the course of the
debate, three issues came up: the question of why the state must take
responsibility for forest policy; the question of the material conditions
needed to realize the ideal of state forestry; and the issue of the mode of
state intervention.
Why State Forestry?
The argument for state forestry was made clearly by P. H. Clutterbuck,
a forester from India, who initiated the debate on the issue of state and
forests. Clutterbuck began by arguing that the state must take respons-
ibility for forest policy. State forestry would ensure that, once reserved, no
forest could be cut down without the most careful scrutiny and the most
valid reasons. State involvement in the making of forest policy would also
mean that forest products would be better exploited. This would be so
because the state was the only agency that could take the risks involved in
introducing new forest products. The Indian experience was testimony to
the utility and importance of a forest policy directed by the state. Had it
not been for state action in India, he said:
extensive tracts of valuable forest land would have been converted into worthless
waste land, and in place of a forest property of almost unlimited potential value
we should now possess nothing but the degraded remnants of what was once a
magniﬁcent heritage.¹⁴⁴
The Empire Strikes Back
143
¹⁴³ G. E. Cubitt, ibid. 72.
¹⁴⁴ P. H. Clutterbuck, ibid. 65.

--- Page 161 ---

Moreover, whereas in the past the Indian state did not give funds to the
forest department for the barest necessities, it now looked upon forest
development as a highly proﬁtable investment. Indeed, rational forest
policy in India had produced a huge ﬁnancial surplus.¹⁴⁵
The broad perspective articulated by Clutterbuck was given greater
shape by Wilhelm Schlich, who argued that state control on forests was
also needed to secure against timber scarcity and famine. Drawing upon
history, he argued, further, that other attempts to do this, such as private
agency, had failed. The state was needed also to determine how much was
to be produced and to ensure that the projected goals were translated into
reality. Moreover, the state was needed to limit the action of the private
individual and further the interests of the proprietors of the forests.
The Conditions for State Forestry
All the foresters who took part in the discussion reiterated the views
expressed by Clutterbuck and Schlich. Their overarching concern,
however, was the question of how to realize the ideal of a co-ordinated
state forest policy. First, there was the question of how to induce the
governments of various states to constitute forest authorities and, more
importantly, to heed their advice on forest policy. Indeed, as late as 1920,
many parts of the empire did not have a state forestry agency.¹⁴⁶ Besides,
there was the issue of how to change the perspectives of ofﬁcials from the
political administrations, including those who were otherwise broadly
sympathetic with forestry, but who had some reservations on this issue. It
was argued that such ofﬁcials were worried about potential conﬂicts for
colonial administrations consequent to jurisdictional disputes between
foresters and civil servants. One such potential conﬂict concerned the
question of how to arbitrate between differing expert scientiﬁc advice that
would stem from such authority. As W. D. Ellis, the Colonial Ofﬁce
representative to the ﬁrst Empire Forestry Conference, put it:
Twenty-ﬁve years ago you sent out a sensible man, and he worked by rule of
thumb . . . but various branches of science now have a great deal to say to
government, and you are confronted on all sides with experts in medicine, in
agriculture, in forestry, and a number of other sciences. But then unfortunately
he ﬁnds that the experts on different subjects do not agree . . . so the Governor
ﬁnds himself in danger of being torn into pieces by wild experts, and he sends an
appeal to the Colonial Ofﬁce, and then the Colonial Ofﬁce has to scratch its head
and think what is to be done.¹⁴⁷
The Empire Strikes Back
144
¹⁴⁵ Clutterbuck, First Empire Forestry Conference, 64–5.
¹⁴⁶ C. E. Lane-Poole, ibid. 75.
¹⁴⁷ W. D. Ellis, ibid. 73.

--- Page 162 ---

Ellis stated, however, that there was a need to set up an expert committee
of foresters at the Colonial Ofﬁce who could arbitrate and maintain
continuity of policy. While falling short of an afﬁrmation of the stand
taken by the foresters in this debate, Ellis’s statement did reinforce
demands that had been made previously by foresters such as Stebbing,
who, as mentioned earlier, had lobbied for exactly this in his correspond-
ence with Ralph Furse.¹⁴⁸
A second aspect of the issue of realizing a co-ordinated state forest policy
was the question of the status of foresters. In a lighter vein, the problem
was reminiscent of the plight of the character James Florey—a timber mer-
chant who found himself at the bottom of the social hierarchy in an
English club in an outpost in Burma in George Orwell’s Burmese Days:¹⁴⁹
generally speaking, in an oriental country, where everybody’s cook knows his
master’s salary, salary and status are the same and the small and salaried ofﬁcial is
not in the same position as those with the larger salaries. Incidentally, the small
salaried ofﬁcial does not get a good cook.¹⁵⁰
In reality the problem was not just of salaries, but of the status of foresters
within the civil service hierarchy. Other issues included the question of
funds to implement forestry agendas and of political power to ensure the
adoption of these agendas.
The Mode of State Forestry
Schlich articulated the dominant perspective on the question of the mode
of state involvement and intervention. The state, according to this view,
ought to
select areas and to declare them a reserve forest and not allow the people to go
inside. Unless this is done the reservation has no meaning. To proceed to select
the permanent state forest is the ﬁrst and foremost duty of a Government with
regard to forestry.¹⁵¹
Schlich argued that such a coercive approach to forest management
was not only necessary, but imperative, and that it was a responsibility of
the state. He argued further that since the state alone could determine
what areas were required, it ought to enact forest laws to lay down certain
guidelines which ought to govern forest use. The Indian Forest Act of 1878
was an example: it determined land use and regulations for utilization,
The Empire Strikes Back
145
¹⁴⁸ Ibid.
¹⁴⁹ See e.g. George Orwell, Burmese Days: A Novel (London: Penguin, 2001).
¹⁵⁰ Cubitt, First Empire Forestry Conference, 188.
¹⁵¹ Ibid. 69.

--- Page 163 ---

gave the state power to acquire land for forestry, and provided for the
formation of a ﬁxed and sufﬁcient staff to manage the areas which had
been declared state forests. Schlich stated that every part of the empire
‘must do the same thing’.¹⁵²
Such an outlook had many supporters, particularly foresters represent-
ing Asian and African parts of the empire, who, spurred on by attitudes
about race stemming from the Victorian era or earlier, claimed repeatedly
that prudent policy involved the imposition of state control, as opposed to
democracy, consultation, or propaganda. The statement of Mr Thompson,
the representative from West Africa, is a case in point.
Propaganda is a thing that takes a long time to establish . . . in places like that
where the people are not civilised enough to understand the situation, the
Government should take it under their control more than they do at present.¹⁵³
The Malayan representative, Mr Cubitt, made a similar comment:
‘the people are inarticulate; there is no question of co-operation with the
people.’¹⁵⁴
Nor surprisingly, foresters representing white settler regions like
Canada took a slightly different position on the issue of the mode of state
forestry. Unlike parts of Asia or Africa, settler societies such as Canada had
a political context that did not allow a coercive approach to forest man-
agement. If anything, conditions in Canada were closer to that in the USA,
where foresters had since the 1880s adopted a complex strategy including
education, propaganda, and careful lobbying. C. Leavitt, who represented
the Canadian forest department, thus argued that in such a context, educa-
tion and propaganda were the best way of realizing statist objectives,¹⁵⁵ a
point that his compatriot Ellwood Wilson made eloquently: ‘co-operation,
rather than coercion, is the best way for a state controlled forest depart-
ment to deal with the people . . . Education of people in all walks of life is
important, and is the basis for reform and progress.’¹⁵⁶
The concluding statement by the chairman of the session,
R. L. Robinson, effectively summarized the discussion and set the tone for
the resolutions that were to follow. He argued that forests were national
assets and a state held its forests ‘not as an absolute property which it can
dispose of at will, but as a trust, and it is only when the conditions of the
trust have been formulated that rational forest management becomes
possible’.¹⁵⁷ The conditions of this trust were laid down explicitly in the
The Empire Strikes Back
146
¹⁵² Sir William Schlich, ibid. 68.
¹⁵³ H. M. Thompson, ibid. 81.
¹⁵⁴ Cubitt, ibid. 72.
¹⁵⁵ C. Leavitt, ibid. 7
¹⁵⁶ Ellwood Wilson, ibid. 66.
¹⁵⁷ R. L. Robinson, ibid. 82.

--- Page 164 ---

resolutions adopted by the conferences. The ﬁrst, on forest policy, stated
that the empire as a whole and individual governments had both a stake
and a responsibility to lay down a deﬁnite forest policy. The stake was sus-
tained yield of all classes of timber, the economic utilization of timber and
other forest products, and maintaining and improving climatic condi-
tions. The responsibility was to establish a ‘properly constituted and ade-
quate forest service’.¹⁵⁸
The second resolution, on constitution and status, went into more
detail. It began by emphasizing the need to secure elements of stability in
the constitution of forest policy. It argued that where it had not been done
already, a forest policy ought to be spelled out clearly in a forestry Act or
ordinance. This Act, it stated, ought to provide for the reservation of land
primarily suited for forestry for the purpose of economic management
and development and provide an assurance of funds to the forest author-
ity sufﬁcient to carry out the accepted policy for a number of years.
Further, it ought to grant to members of the forest service the status of
civil servants with provision for pension; provide for the appointment as
the chief ofﬁcers of the forest service persons having a high standard of
training in forestry; appoint senior ofﬁcers to advise governments on
forest policy; and ensure that the advice they provided was adopted.¹⁵⁹
The third resolution was about publicity. It stated that it was the duty of
the forest authority in every part of the empire to adopt methods of educa-
tion and publicity in order to inform people about the aims of forest policy
and to ‘induce’ them to ‘co-operate towards its successful fulﬁlment’.¹⁶⁰
The Forest Policy Debate in the Subsequent Conferences
The broad tenor of the forest policy debate of the ﬁrst two conferences
was continued in the subsequent meetings. However, although the issue
of forest policy was discussed at every Empire Forestry Conference, it was
the third and fourth conferences (1928 and 1935) that saw the greatest
degree of engaged debate.
The premiss of this debate was the ‘forestry creed’, adopted by consen-
sus. The creed, ﬁrst proposed by Lord Robinson, one of the chief
advocates of forestry in the United Kingdom, had four essential elements.
First, it deﬁned the object of forest policy as being sustained yield and
economic utilization. Secondly, it emphasized systematic data collection,
on the assumption that stable forest policies could be founded only on
The Empire Strikes Back
147
¹⁵⁸ Resolutions, ibid. 215; Second Empire Forestry Conference, 369.
¹⁵⁹ Resolutions, First Empire Forestry Conference, 217; Second Empire Forestry
Conference, 369.
¹⁶⁰ Resolutions, Second Empire Forestry Conference, 369.

--- Page 165 ---

sound information about the nature and extent of available resources.
Thirdly, it subsumed under the rubric ‘Constitution and Status’ three
issues: the deﬁnition of a forest policy in an act of legislation; the reserva-
tion of land for forestry purposes (including the classiﬁcation, demarca-
tion, and deﬁnite dedication of land); and an assurance of ﬁnance for
forestry by the state. Fourthly, the forest creed highlighted the
importance of professionalization: members of the forestry profession
had to be recruited on the basis of technical merit, trained in professional
forestry institutions, and provided with security of tenure and status
equivalent to civil servants.¹⁶¹
Empire foresters unanimously ratiﬁed their commitment to the
forestry creed at every conference.¹⁶² However, the inter-war years posed
a challenge. The changing political contexts in the empire had begun to
thwart the implementation of their ‘creed’. One of these contexts was the
increasing tendency towards provincial devolution—the transfer of
administrative control to individual states or provinces. The impact of
such measures on forestry was the creation of provincial forest depart-
ments with independent forest policies. This meant that there was
increasingly less co-ordination between different provinces on forest pol-
icy matters. The fragmentation of forest departments also meant that,
being smaller, they were much more at the mercy of the whims of indi-
vidual provincial governments. The foresters at the Empire Forestry
Conferences testiﬁed that this state of affairs prevailed in nearly all parts
of the empire, with South Africa being almost the sole important excep-
tion.¹⁶³ For empire foresters, the fragmentation of their departments
meant that the basic principle of their ‘creed’—total control over decision
making on forestry matters—was therefore becoming unrealizable on the
ﬁeld. As one forester speaking at the fourth conference put it:
However ardently foresters may desire the powers of a dictator for putting their
world to rights they are not going to get and hold those powers. In other words
they are not going to get what they want. In any event, Parliament with its
machinery and safeguards is or tends to become even in the most backward parts
of the Empire the ultimate authority in all matters. It can make and unmake the
forester at will.¹⁶⁴
The Empire Strikes Back
148
¹⁶¹ Lord Robinson, The Third Empire Forestry Conference: Proceedings, Resolutions and
Statements (n.p, 1928), 84. 
¹⁶² ‘Resolutions’, ibid. 236–8; The Fourth Empire Forestry Conference: Proceedings,
Resolutions, Statements (n.p., 1935), 356; The Fifth Empire Forestry Conference: Proceedings,
Resolutions, Statements (London, 1947), 264–5; The Sixth Commonwealth Forestry
Conference Proceedings, Resolutions, Statements (n.p., 1952), 354–61.
¹⁶³ Roy Robinson, Fourth Empire Forestry Conference, 86.
¹⁶⁴ Ibid.

--- Page 166 ---

The process of subordination and, often, loss of authority over forest
reserves resulted in a great deal of frustration among the empire forestry
community.¹⁶⁵ In some cases, foresters felt so defeated that they resorted
to suicide.¹⁶⁶
Another issue that began to loom large in forest policy discussions
in the conferences, especially in the fourth (1935), was the Great
Depression. Signiﬁcantly, the conferences themselves were hit hard by the
depression. The South African meeting, for example, had been post-
poned from 1933 to 1935, and after considerable correspondence
between October 1938 and July 1939, India agreed to host the confer-
ences on the condition that they were restricted to Dehra Dun and held
for only thirty days.¹⁶⁷ It is important to note here that although the
impact of the Depression on colonial conservation policies has become
a topic of historical research recently, this body of literature has tended to
concentrate on the policies of environmental management that emerged
in response, as opposed to the consequences of the Depression for the
colonial forestry community and its institutions.¹⁶⁸ That this historio-
graphical gap is signiﬁcant is indicated by the plethora of editorial
comments and articles by foresters during the period bemoaning an
increasing governmental lack of interest in forestry and proposing ways of
countering it.¹⁶⁹ The debate in the fourth Empire Forestry Conference
reﬂected precisely such a concern amongst the colonial forestry commun-
ity. The premiss underlying the debate was that forestry had been among
the colonial agencies to be hit the hardest in the wake of governmental
cutbacks. As Roy Robinson, who gave the keynote address on forest
policy at the fourth conference, put it:
the great economic depression hit forestry very hard. The ﬁrst reaction of almost
all Governments was to cut appropriations all around. In the case of Forest

--- Page 167 ---

Services trained foresters were dismissed, the scale of operations was reduced,
carefully thought out projects were arrested in mid-career and so on.¹⁷⁰
Another important issue of contention in the debates over the question
of control during the forest policy deliberations of the third and fourth
conferences was the subsumption of forest departments under agriculture
departments for administrative purposes in some parts of the empire.
Foresters argued that such a step was a major mistake. In the Cape Colony,
for example, they pointed out, although the forest department was to have
been run by senior ofﬁcials of the agricultural department, senior agricul-
tural ofﬁcers often subdelegated forestry issues to subordinates. As a result,
senior men in the forest department were given instructions by junior
members of the agricultural department. Moreover, when agricultural
departments faced budgetary problems, the ﬁrst sections to suffer ﬁnan-
cially were those that dealt with forestry.¹⁷¹ In emphasizing the need to
separate administratively the forestry and agricultural departments, foresters
pointed out that the chief reason for the success of Indian forestry was that
it had had an independent forest department from the outset.¹⁷²
Colonial foresters began to see in tendencies such as provincial devolu-
tion, the subordination of forestry under agriculture, and the response of
governments to the Depression a drastic erosion of the cardinal princi-
ple of their ‘forestry creed’, which demanded a strong governmental
commitment to give them control over the management of forests. The
colonial forestry community therefore used the Empire Forestry
Conferences to mount a fervent campaign to retain control over forested
lands.¹⁷³ The starting point of their campaign was a strong and collective
reafﬁrmation of their creed. In the third conference, for example, foresters
urged that central governments, where they existed, ought to ‘exercise a
considerable measure of control’ over the local governments on certain
matters, especially the ‘matters of vital signiﬁcance’, such as broad policy,
legislation, and working plans. They argued further that the question of
forests must lie outside the range of party politics, as it was ‘a national, as

opposed to a provincial or parochial concern’.¹⁷⁴ Moreover, they resolved
that it was of paramount importance for central governments to lay down
and adhere to a deﬁnite and permanent general policy and that it was the
task of central governments to exercise control over legislation, manage-
ment, and the alienation of land dedicated to forestry, in addition to
providing for research and the training and recruitment of superior
staff.¹⁷⁵The fourth conference saw similar sentiments being expressed.¹⁷⁶
In addition to making such demands, the colonial forestry community
sought to develop greater cohesion and, thereby, bargaining power. One
leading forester thus stated:
I do not wish to appear to stress unduly the suggestion that forest policy is a
battleﬁeld between politicians and foresters. To do so would be contrary to my
whole theme. But I do make the point that unless there is agreement between
foresters as to what they want, that unless they present a united front the ﬁght is
inevitably lost before it is even joined. There cannot be complete agreement
among foresters on technical points any more than there can be complete agree-
ment among experts in other walks of life, but there can be complete loyalty to
the cause of forestry, there can be broadmindedness and there can be determina-
tion to work together at all hazards.¹⁷⁷
Empire foresters also sought to gain greater visibility as a way of gaining
a political base for their policies and priorities. The keyword used was
‘propaganda’. Forest departments across the empire were directed to reach
out to the public using a multi-media campaign, on the model of the
Canadian forest department’s campaign of the 1920s.¹⁷⁸ At the Empire
Forestry Conferences, foresters themselves were encouraged to ‘make
friends’—‘not just write memoranda but follow up through personal
contacts’.¹⁷⁹ Underlying such advice and directives was a belief in the
politics of lobbying. Engaging in ‘consistent and honest’ propaganda,
advocates of this approach argued, would be the best tactic to get their
policies adopted:
The great depression has shown that above all things there can, under democratic
government, be no absolute security in the ﬁnance of forestry undertakings. If the

151

Clutterbuck (pp. 74–84).
¹⁷⁵ ‘Resolutions’, ibid. 236–8.
¹⁷⁶ Fourth Empire Forestry Conference, 84–9, 333–8.

pressure is great enough a minister of forests will raid any convenient ‘henroost’.
We must be prepared to help him while insisting to the limit that our breeding
birds are preserved to us. That is the line that the Forestry Commissioners adopted
in England and I think we are now beginning to reap the reward.¹⁸⁰
The idea of engaging in deliberate propaganda constituted an important
thrust in forest policy through to the 1940s. In the immediate aftermath of
the Second World War, for example, the Standing Committee for Empire
Forestry sought to compile information on the contribution of colonial for-
est departments to the war effort.¹⁸¹ In a letter to Roy Cameron of the
Department of Mines and Resources, Land, Parks, and Forests in Ottawa,
for example, Robinson wrote that ‘we feel that has a very interesting and
important story to tell and one which if properly told will bring about a bet-
ter appreciation of the value of forests and incidentally of forester’s work.’¹⁸²
Following up, H. R. Blandford, the secretary of the Empire Forestry
Association, wrote to Roy Robinson a couple of months later that ‘I have
been in communication with Messrs. Faber and Faber about the popular
publication running to about 200 pages with about 50–60 illustra-
tions.’¹⁸³ The response of colonial forest departments to the idea of such
a propagandistic document was euphoric. ‘When the history of the forest
departments’ war effort comes to be written,’ wrote S. Howard, inspector-
general of forests, India, ‘it will be a romance.’¹⁸⁴
In addition to the issue of control, the debates on forest policy at the
third and fourth Empire Forestry Conferences concentrated on aspects of
land utilization. Of particular importance was the issue of the regulation
of forests in privately owned lands. It was claimed during the third
conference that private forests in many parts of the empire were being
destroyed and that steps to acquire them were being thwarted by legislat-
ive assemblies.¹⁸⁵ Foresters argued that such events were seriously detri-
mental to the countries concerned because forests situated in mountains
and catchment areas were hugely important for the maintenance of water
supply, especially in countries with warm climates. They thus urged
governments to include provisions in their policies to prevent the
The Empire Strikes Back
152
¹⁸⁰ Roy Robinson, Fourth Empire Forestry Conference, 88.
¹⁸¹ Proceedings of the Meeting of the Standing Committee on Empire Forestry, 2 Feb.
1944, PRO F18/201, 8 Aug. 1938–7 May1945.

Forests, Ottawa, 9 Feb. 1944, PRO F18/201, 8 Aug. 1938–7 May 1945.
¹⁸³ H. R. Blandford to Roy Robinson, 4 Apr. 1944, PRO F18/201, 8 Aug. 1938–7 May

1945.
¹⁸⁵ S. H. Howard, Third Empire Forestry Conference, 82.

--- Page 170 ---

destruction of forests in certain designated areas.¹⁸⁶Tanzania was cited as
a model example of a country that had already done so. Legislation in that
country, it was pointed out, prevented the destruction of more than a
quarter of any forest over 250 acres unless an intention existed immedi-
ately to afforest. It also provided forest ofﬁcers with access, for purposes of
inspection, to private properties containing forests. Further, it gave the
conservator of forests control over the felling of forests in catchment areas
in cases where in his opinion, public water supplies were being jeopard-
ized by fellings.¹⁸⁷ In making such suggestions, foresters were however
cognizant of the general attitude to private property prevalent among
British administrative ofﬁcials, for whom, they claimed, the idea of inter-
fering with the control of private property was against their ‘inherent
ideas and traditions’.¹⁸⁸They therefore sought to word their resolution in
a manner that would be palatable to such ofﬁcials.
The resolutions on forest policy at the third, fourth, ﬁfth, and sixth
Empire Forestry Conferences unanimously ratiﬁed the various arguments
made during the debate on forest policy at the third and fourth conferences.
They thus urged that forest legislation should be enacted by governments
and ought to provide for the protection of water supplies for human
consumption and agricultural and other purposes; the prevention of
erosion; the control of shifting cultivation, whether on private or public
lands; and, most importantly, the control of forested lands, including
communal and private, by forest departments. The resolutions urged further
that provisions ought to be made for the dedication and afforestation of
areas near centres of consumption and to meet agricultural requirements—
and that forestry departments should not be amalgamated with agricultural
departments for administrative purposes. Finally, they emphasized the
importance of educating politicians, ofﬁcials, and the general public on
the importance of forests and forestry and urged that elementary instruc-
tion on the subject be given in primary and secondary schools.¹⁸⁹
4.4. CONCLUSION
The idea for staging the Empire Forestry Conferences emerged against
the backdrop of the First World War. In a context in which the colonial
governments had begun to show interest in sponsoring scientiﬁc research
The Empire Strikes Back
153
¹⁸⁶ Ibid.
¹⁸⁷ D. K. S. Grant, ibid. 81.
¹⁸⁸ Lord Robinson, ibid. 85.
¹⁸⁹ See e.g. ‘Resolutions’, ibid. 236–8, and ‘Resolutions’, Fourth Empire Forestry
Conference, 356.

--- Page 171 ---

on the efﬁcient use of empire resources, colonial foresters, supported by
a small but inﬂuential cohort in Britain, proposed the establishment of
fora that would enable them to audit and co-ordinate the making of
policy. Their proposal was swiftly accepted by the government and led to
the convening of the Empire Forestry Conferences. The agendas for these
conferences were made by the foresters and reﬂected their desire to inﬂu-
ence the making of colonial forest policy.
The process leading up to the establishment of the Empire Forestry
Conferences is reﬂective of the growing inﬂuence of professional scient-
iﬁc communities on governmental policy within Britain and in the
empire. Here, the role played by British and expatriate foresters is remin-
iscent of that played by other scientiﬁc communities—such as geology,
botany, physics, and medicine—during the same period.¹⁹⁰ In each case,
scientists lobbied the government by promising to help contribute to
the growth of the economy and improve efﬁciency in production. At the
same time, they sought ﬁnancial investment, prestige, and status. If there
is one area where the story of forestry is different, it is in the manner in
which the discipline and profession diffused into Britain via the empire.
Unlike the other scientiﬁc ﬁelds mentioned, where the expertise involved
had been grown and nurtured from within British institutions, forestry
was a reluctant import—a German seed, acclimatized, so to speak, in
British India, and then transplanted into the United Kingdom.
The agents of change were a very small group of inﬂuential landlords,
who adeptly mobilized their political connections, on the one hand, and
the expertise supplied by foresters returned from service in India, on the
other. In convincing the former, they argued, initially, in the aftermath of
the First World War, that winning large-scale modern wars must involve
modernizing the natural world. Subsequently, during the inter-war years
and after, their rhetoric shifted to one that argued that scientiﬁcally
managed forests would result in helping national and regional economies
grow, and, thereby, cope with the crisis of employment and well-being.
Both these arguments were part of a wider rhetoric about modernization
and efﬁciency that had got under way in Britain with the onset of the
movement for national efﬁciency around the turn of the century.¹⁹¹
The Empire Strikes Back
154
¹⁹⁰ See Chapter 1 for a description of this role.
¹⁹¹ Alter, The Reluctant Patron. See also G. R. Searle, The Quest for National Efﬁciency:
A Study in British Politics and Political Thought, 1899–1914 (Berkeley and Los Angeles,
1971). For a comparative perspective, see John Peter Rasmussen, ‘The American
Imperialist Elite: A Study in the Concept of National Efﬁciency (Ph.D. thesis, Stanford
University, 1962).

--- Page 172 ---

In working with the latter—the colonial forestry community—the
small group of usual suspects enabled a well-trained but diffuse, though
eager, colonial scientiﬁc corps to organize, build a centre of calculation—
the Empire Forestry Conferences—and take the next step toward attain-
ing a wider, pan-colonial professional identity.¹⁹² In turn, the forestry
community gladly embraced such patronage, and used the forum of the
conferences to campaign for the central demands, which stemmed from
the view that they were a body of specialists best capable of economically
managing forests—colonial and British. As argued in Chapter 3, this view
had begun to emerge in British India during the late Victorian period. By
the inter-war years, the outlook had metamorphosed to become the
bedrock of a technocracy, whose foundational myths included a strong
critique of existing British attitudes to ecology. Pax Britannica, foresters
thus argued with strong moral overtones, had, prior to the work of their
professional ancestors, been an ecological disaster with explicit economic
ramiﬁcations for the British Empire. Implicit in this critique was the
contention that had it not been for the efforts of the empire forestry
community, the scale of the problem would have been greater.
In pursuance of their almost envangelical goals, the empire forestry
community sought to use the conferences to lobby for control over
empire forests and for power over the management of forest lands. An
important aspect of their agenda was the felt need for state-controlled
forestry. Empire foresters unanimously argued for forest policy to be run
by the scientiﬁc foresters in agencies sponsored by the state. To propagate
this ideal, the foresters strongly used their growing credibility as
experts—in a context in which scientiﬁc expertise had begun to emerge
as an important factor in British economic and colonial developmental
policy. Colonial forest ofﬁcers speciﬁcally sought to use the increasing
clout of the conferences, stemming from their proﬁle as scientiﬁc experts
of the empire, to get politicians and civil administrations across the
empire to accept their perspective on how colonial forests were to be best
managed. The thrust of the deliberations on policy indicate an increasing
tendency on the part of empire foresters to organize and become a pan-
colonial lobby group. They also show a growing commitment by empire
foresters to pressure-group politics.
Signiﬁcantly, the rise of forestry experts coincided with a wider attitude
on colonial governance and, especially, a growing ofﬁcial discourse on
The Empire Strikes Back
155
¹⁹² The term ‘Center of Calculation’ is used here in the sense deﬁned by Bruno Latour
in Science in Action.

--- Page 173 ---

colonial development which reluctantly provided legitimization to experts.
The negotiated imperialism of the Victorian era soon began to give way
to a patronizing science-based imperialism. Owing to its ostensibly ‘pro-
gressive’ ideology—enshrined and encoded within the Colonial Welfare
and Development Acts—this new developmentalism helped redeem
what were once seen, especially by the burgeoning nationalist move-
ments, as instruments of coercion and exploitation.¹⁹³ In the process,
ideas promoted by technocracies, such as forestry, became emmeshed
within and inseparable from the discourse on modernization and state
building.
At the centre of the emergent technocratic politicking, however, was a
fundamental and deep-rooted commitment to certain basic principles of
forest management held by colonial foresters, which, as argued earlier,
stemmed from the continental roots of British colonial forestry. Implicit
in this paradigm was a strong commitment to economic development.
The paradigm also had a strong cultural aspect. Part of its normative
appeal for its protagonists, the empire forestry community, lay in its advo-
cacy of material progress through an ostensibly universal technology
which enabled, to use the words of Alexander Spoehr, not just the
exploitation of ‘single landscapes, separately’, but the ‘pooling and redis-
tributing the products of all types of environment’.¹⁹⁴ In order to realize
this vision on an empire-wide scale, colonial foresters explicitly argued for
state control of forest management on grounds that the state alone was
capable of taking the initiatives and risks involved. Their particular target
was private forestry of any kind, regardless of whether such forestry was
managed by white settlers or other local peoples. Such an attitude to
privately run forestry was in turn part of a wider distrust of any attempt
to run forestry along either ad hoc or laissez-faire lines, a distrust that had
its roots partially in the cameralist origins of forestry in Prussia and
subsequently in the rampant destruction of forests in various parts of the
empire prior to the emergence of forest departments.
Signiﬁcantly, there was no fundamental change in the perceptions of
colonial foresters on what constituted the principles of forest policy dur-
ing the ﬁrst half of the twentieth century. The debates on forest policy at
The Empire Strikes Back
156
¹⁹³ A fascinating account of this process is provided in Patrick Geddes’s letters to Lewis
Mumford on Mahatma Gandhi’s attitudes to forestry. See Frank G. Norak (ed.), Lewis
Mumford and Patrick Geddes: The Correspondence (London, 1995). The issue of the transi-
tion of colonial agendas to the post-colonial context is discussed in Chapter 6.
¹⁹⁴ A. Spoehr, ‘Cultural Differences in the Interpretation of Natural Resources’, in
Thomas (ed.), Man’s Role in Changing the Face of the Earth, i. 95.

--- Page 174 ---

the Empire Forestry Conferences during this period were largely an
attempt at articulating and elaborating on the core ideas described in the
earlier paragraphs—which the foresters developed into a ‘forestry creed’.
The debates at the Empire Forestry Conferences were an attempt to
defend this ‘creed’ practically in the wake of several challenges, such as the
Depression and provincial devolution, both of which had resulted in state
policies that tended to de-emphasize the priorities and agendas of
foresters, and the wider subordination of forestry to agriculture. It is
important to note here that there was, throughout the debates on policy,
a tension between paradigmatic generality and the broad agenda of mod-
ern forestry, and the need for pragmatic location and context-sensitive
speciﬁcity. Foresters however sought to resolve local issues in a pragmatic
manner without losing sight of their bigger disciplinary and professional
perspective. This observation is signiﬁcant in light of recent attempts by
some scholars to paint colonial forestry practices as purely site speciﬁc
and, in doing so, deny the wider ideological continuities across both
region and time.¹⁹⁵
Last, but by no means least, the debates on policy at the Empire
Forestry Conferences point to a conﬂict between foresters and other
communities that constituted the colonial state, a conﬂict that had been
brewing from the days when modern forestry ﬁrst began in the empire.
At the heart of this conﬂict were three factors—land, money, and status.
As argued repeatedly earlier, implicit among the forestry community was
the desire to assume control over the enormous empire of forests and be
recognized as the only agency responsible for deciding and regulating
how that empire was to be run. Despite growing governmental support
for the forestry agenda, foresters were frustrated that they were not receiv-
ing what they believed was their due. Some of this frustration was
undoubtedly borne out of the fact that forestry initially received a
relatively small slice of the colonial research and development cake. In the
Colonial Development Act of 1929–39, for example, only 15 of the 641
schemes funded were related to forestry.¹⁹⁶The resolutions on legislation
and publicity, therefore, were strong statements to the effect that foresters
must be given more resources and, deﬁnitely, more control.
The debate on forest policy is also indicative of important ﬁssures
between the colonial forestry communities. In particular, there were
differences, in the views of foresters on the issue of whether education or
coercion was the best method of state intervention while enforcing
The Empire Strikes Back
157
¹⁹⁵ This argument will be pursued in greater detail in the next chapter.
¹⁹⁶ Havinden and Meredith, Colonialism and Development, 164.

--- Page 175 ---

forestry policies. Here, the political context in which the forester worked
determined his perspective: there were marked differences between
foresters from Canada and other dominions, tropical dependencies, and
settler-dependency mixes. Collectively, these ﬁssures—between foresters
and the state on the one hand, and to a lesser extent between colonial
foresters themselves, on the other—show that the domain of imperial
forestry was as stratiﬁed as it was homogeneous. While there were indeed,
generally speaking, broad, imperial priorities, there was, at the same time,
a constant tug-of-war among professional communities. The picture
created by the ideal-type ‘state forestry’ used by most environmental
historians today therefore needs to be expanded, taking cognizance of the
perpetual contests over policy and perspective within the colonial state
and within the empire-wide forestry community.¹⁹⁷ Nowhere were such
contests more manifest than in the speciﬁc ecological debates on forestry
and land management—and it is these that Chapter 5 addresses.
The Empire Strikes Back
158
¹⁹⁷ The term ‘ideal-type’ is used in the sense developed by Max Weber in ‘Objectivity
in Social Science and Social Policy’, in Edward Shils and Henry Finch (eds.), Max Weber on
the Methodology of the Social Sciences (New York: Free Press, 1949), 50–112.

--- Page 176 ---

5
The Imperial Environmentalist
Being big occasions attended by leading colonial ofﬁcials, the Empire
Forestry Conferences were, to a considerable degree, political events and
provided foresters with opportunities to lobby for their agendas and
promote their professional status and interests. However, when the glitter
and bustle of the opening plenaries—studded with a star cast of leading
politicians—faded, the conferences assumed a different role, of engaging
in a detailed stocktaking discussion of empire forestry practices and
problems.
There was a reason why the conferences took such a course. During the
process of constructing the agendas for the Empire Forestry Conferences,
the Standing Committee had sought precisely such a comparative, cross-
regional discussion of the state of empire forestry. In pressing for such an
agenda, they had argued that the varying ecological and societal condi-
tions prevalent across the vast empire posed a number of challenges as
regards the implementation of silvicultural, production, protection, and
utilization policies. Further, they had pointed out that there was, as of
then, no empire-wide consensus on some of the vexing human-ecological
issues such as shifting cultivation and soil erosion. The overwhelming
sense among the agenda setters was that a lot of wheels were being rein-
vented, and that all concerned would proﬁt from learning about their
respective approaches to empire forestry problems. The conferences, in
their view, offered an opportunity for foresters to listen to diverse experi-
ences on the management of the colonial forests. They also helped clarify
differences over a number of issues and forge a common agenda.
Moreover, they enabled a protracted cross-regional discussion on how to
adapt their paradigmatic framework on forest management to the differ-
ent ecological and socio-economic and political conditions prevalent in
the empire.
If the debates on forest policy discussed in the previous chapter are
indicative of the growing technocracy and political savvy of the empire

--- Page 177 ---

forestry community, the deliberations on forest management provide a
crucial insight into their ecological agendas. Moreover, the discussions on
shifting cultivation and soil conservation helped provide a vivid picture of
how foresters approached complex human-ecological problems. The
conference proceedings also reveal a great deal about the wider environ-
mental attitudes of the forestry community—toward nature and resource,
toward local peoples, and to science, technology, and development.
The purpose of this chapter is to explore the forest management
agendas and environmentalist ideologies of colonial forestry as expressed
at the Empire Forestry Conferences during the ﬁrst half of the twentieth
century. In keeping with the broad structure of the conference delibera-
tions, the discussion here will comprise two parts: the ‘classical’ problems
associated with paradigm articulation, and the policy dilemmas posed by
agro-forestry challenges such as shifting cultivation and soil erosion.
5.1. PARADIGM ARTICULATION
5.1.1. The ‘Classical’ Problems¹
As the agenda setters for the ﬁrst conference began to deliberate on the
slate of questions to put on the table before the participants, they set the
principal task as that of developing a consolidated picture of colonial
forestry. In broad terms, there were two aspects to this task—an imperial
environmental audit, and institution building to enable colonial forestry
to grow and mature as a discipline.
At the time that the ﬁrst Empire Forest Conference was conceived,
there was a deﬁnite shortfall in forest products across the empire. India,
Australia, and South Africa, for example, did not meet their own
demands of softwoods, each depending on Canada for their require-
ments. Similarly, hardwood supplies were on the decline in West Africa
and India could barely meet the hardwood needs imposed upon it by a
ravenous empire. During the discussions at the conferences, the review of
the existing statistical data also revealed that there was considerable
uncertainty over how much and what types of forests there were in differ-
ent parts of the empire. Indeed, there was very little reliable information
The Imperial Environmentalist
160
¹ The word ‘classical’ is used in the sense of a ‘static, long-established, well recognised
and highly valued approach on which particular policies in the future might be based’
(Blaikie, The Political Economy of Soil Erosion in Developing Countries, 53). In this particu-
lar context, ‘classical’ refers to the German forestry paradigm.

--- Page 178 ---

on how much woodland existed, what forest types there were, and what
their rate of decline was.²
The foresters participating in the discussions therefore unanimously
agreed that it was urgently important to generate adequate information.
Accordingly, they resolved that the foundation of a stable forest policy for
the empire depended on the
collection, co-ordination and dissemination of facts with regard to the existing
forest resources of the empire, the opportunities for their utilisation, their rate of
growth and regeneration, the balance between increment on the one hand and
utilisation and wastage on the other, and the requirements of the empire for all
forest products.³
Another resolution went on to stress the importance of systematic
surveys, which, it claimed, would serve the dual role of providing the basis
of forest policies locally and reviewing the forestry position of the empire
as a whole.⁴
In addition to addressing the issue of information uncertainty, the
deliberations at the ﬁrst and second conferences also pondered the ques-
tion of whether existing forest use practices were sufﬁcient to ensure
sustainable production and efﬁcient utilization. The resultant debate on
forestry practices centred around two issues—the methods of production,
and the problems of utilization. In both instances, the thrust of the
discussion was on comparing notes and discussing best practices. The
deliberations on production focused on the question of whether artiﬁcial
or natural regeneration was more economic and practicable. While there
were some, like Professor Troup from north India and H. M. Thompson
from Nigeria, who preferred artiﬁcial regeneration, others, such as
A. J. Gibson from India and G. E. S. Cubitt from the Federated Malay
States and the Straits Settlements, did not agree. Their disagreement
stemmed largely from the ecological differences between the places they
came from: while natural regeneration, in some places, yielded the kinds
of woodlands desired by the foresters, it did not in others. The upshot
was that there was no conclusive evidence at the conferences that could
settle the issue. The foresters therefore made a case for the need for more
information and research.⁵
Likewise, on utilization, discussants agreed that there was very little
ability among imperial forest departments, as yet, to exploit and develop
The Imperial Environmentalist
161
² First Empire Forestry Conference, 156–81.
³ Resolution on Forest Surveys, ibid. 216.
⁴ Ibid.
⁵ Ibid. 105–15; Second Empire Forestry Conference, 114–64.

--- Page 179 ---

their timbers and to put them on the market. On an issue as important as
forest terminology, for example, both technical names and trade names
were extremely variable across the empire, leading to a great deal of
confusion.⁶ Moreover, empire-wide trade in forest products was itself in
its infancy, thereby not creating the conditions for the adequate and
efﬁcient use of empire forests. Another problem was that forest depart-
ments saw themselves largely as producers and had historically developed
an antipathy to private utilizers of forests, particularly private forest
industries. Many delegates argued that such an outlook hampered
efﬁcient utilization. Last, but by no means least, there was a great short-
fall in trained staff. Indeed, those that did exist did not enjoy much status
in the imperial hierarchy.⁷
Based on the above analysis, the delegates at the conferences passed
three unanimous resolutions on the question of utilization and trade.
They resolved that timber and forest products manufacturing organiza-
tions ought to be consulted in making forest policy and that terminology
and trade names needed to be standardized.⁸ Next, they agreed that the
requirement for timber and other forest products needed to be met from
within the empire itself and that active steps needed to be taken through-
out the empire to organize and foster trade in forest products.⁹ They also
decided that colonial governments in different parts of the empire should
be asked to conserve and augment their resources of softwoods, hard-
woods, and other forest products.¹⁰
In addition to information, production, and utilization, the confer-
ence also took up the question of how waste mitigation was being
approached in different parts of the empire. The discussions on waste in
the ﬁrst two conferences predictably dwelt on the three issues that had
preoccupied foresters since the onset of scientiﬁc forestry: ﬁre, insects,
and disease. The session on ﬁre did much to inﬂame the passions of the
discussants. Since outbreaks often had severe economic consequences,
there was complete consensus that ﬁre was the ‘forester’s greatest enemy’¹¹
and, when uncontrolled, evil.¹² Delegates argued that ﬁre control
was ‘pure insurance against loss through ﬁre of capital stock in soil and
The Imperial Environmentalist
162
⁶ First Empire Forestry Conference, 156–60.
⁷ Ibid. 156–83, 275–8, 288–97; Second Empire Forestry Conference, 91–113, 278–99,
370, 411.
⁸ First Empire Forestry Conference, 218.
⁹ Resolution on Empire Trade in Forest Resources, Second Empire Forestry Conference, 372.
¹⁰ Resolution 2, ibid. 370.
¹¹ Owen Jones, Forestry Commissioner, Victoria, Australia, ibid. 175.
¹² C. G. Trevor, Conservator of Forests, UP, India, ibid.

--- Page 180 ---

timber’.¹³ Such arguments were similar to the attitude held by mainstream
foresters trained in the continental tradition elsewhere. For example, in the
United States in the late nineteenth century, Gifford Pinchot had pro-
claimed that ‘like the question of slavery, the question of forest ﬁres may be
shelved for a time, at enormous cost at the end, but sooner or later it must be
faced’.¹⁴ It is interesting to note that even John Muir, the apostle of preser-
vation in America, and a person often described as an advocate of wilderness,
had described ﬁre damage as ‘ten times worse than that caused by logging’.
In their search for the causes of ﬁre, the discussants at the conferences
quickly arrived at a consensus that ‘Man’ was at the core of the ‘ﬁre prob-
lem’. In Australia, more than 70 per cent of ﬁres were caused by human
carelessness: settlers’ ﬁres, the camper, and the grazier who burned off old
grass to generate tender grass for his ﬂock. In Canada, 90 per cent of
the ﬁres were caused ostensibly by human agencies: railways, campers,
settlers burning slash, and lumbering. In India, it was not only human
carelessness but also deliberate incendiarism, caused during periodic
outbursts against the regime. Everywhere, the problem was the ostensible
ignorance and apathy of the common man, a ‘pertinent commentary on
our present state of civilization’.¹⁵
It is important to point out here that in deploying such rhetoric and
reasoning, the British colonial foresters at the conferences were very
much in tune with the mainstream attitude among foresters trained in the
continental European tradition. To take one example, Bernard Fernow, a
Prussian forester, writing in the context of the American forestry debate,
described ﬁres as the ‘bane of American forests’, and blamed civilian popu-
lations, whom he accused of possessing ‘bad habits and loose morals’.¹⁶
The similarities in outlook between British colonial foresters and their
counterparts elsewhere continued in the discussion on how ﬁre ought to
be managed. Like their counterparts in the US forest service, who,
embracing the spirit of the progressivist era, had declared that ‘ﬁreﬁghting
is a matter of scientiﬁc management, just as much as silviculture or range
improvement’,¹⁷ the delegates at the conferences spent some time dis-
cussing ﬁre as a technical problem, exploring measures such as ﬁre breaks,
ﬁre lines, watch towers, and patrols. However, the discussion on ﬁre
The Imperial Environmentalist
163
¹³ Report of the Committee on Fire Protection, ibid. 38.
¹⁴ Stephen Pyne, World Fire: The Culture of Fire on Earth (Seattle, 1997), 184.
¹⁵ D. R. Cameron, Dominion Forest Service, Canada, Second Empire Forestry
Conference, 178.
¹⁶ Pyne, World Fire. See also Williams, Americans and their Forests.
¹⁷ Pyne, World Fire, 186.

--- Page 181 ---

control very quickly turned to the question of what to do with common
people, whom, as pointed out earlier, the foresters held fundamentally
responsible for outbreaks. Although delegates explored legislation as a
possible solution, many foresters argued that the difﬁculties posed by
enforcement were considerable. Accordingly, they stressed the importance
of educating people, and of enlisting governments in this cause.
The deliberation on insects and disease was on necessity different
from that on ﬁre, because the ostensible offenders were not common people
but bugs and pathogens. The discussants in the session on forest insects,
Dr J. W. Munro, an entomologist of the Forestry Commission,
Dr J. M. Swaine, an entomologist of the Department of Agriculture of the
Canadian government, and Mr C. G. Trevor of the Indian forest service,
each stated emphatically that the knowledge of the causes of insect outbreaks
in forests was no better in the 1920s than it had been in the 1830s, when
forest entomology ﬁrst began to make signiﬁcant strides in Germany. To
complicate matters, approaches developed in Europe had failed in other eco-
logical conditions, such as in India. Moreover, entomologists could not by
themselves hope to devise strategies to combat forest insect outbreaks: col-
laboration with silviculturalists and mycologists and an ecological approach
were essential. There was therefore a critical need felt to institutionalize a
collaborative programme of fundamental research, involving foresters, ento-
mologists, and mycologists. Munro, Swaine, and Trevor also made a strong
case for organizing an empire-wide information exchange on forest insect
pests and their control. Other speakers on forest disease similarly empha-
sized the need for more research, especially of a collaborative nature.¹⁸
In the conferences that followed in the wake of London and Ottawa,
debates on the ‘classical’ issues of forestry—production, utilization, and
protection—continued to hold a primary place on the agenda. The delib-
erations, however, consisted, in large measure, in technical brainstorming.
As in the ﬁrst two conferences, they focused on two main issues: surveys of
resources¹⁹ and silvicultural techniques, including artiﬁcial and natural
regeneration, techniques of afforestation, and working plans.²⁰ The main
thrust was on examining speciﬁc issues relating either to innovations in
technique or to problems posed by particular geographical and ecological
contexts. The conferences also exposedleading forestry experts in different
The Imperial Environmentalist
164
¹⁸ The discussions on insects and disease, Second Empire Forestry Conference, 216–30.
¹⁹ Third Empire Forestry Conference, 1–3, 236–8.
²⁰ Ibid. 122–3, 171–85, 201–11, 218, 253–81; Fourth Empire Forestry Conference,

parts of the empire to problems in areas other than their own. Field tours,
an intrinsic part of the conferences, were therefore an important aspect
of the proceedings. They enabled overseas foresters to study unfamiliar
technical problems ﬁrst hand.
The debates on the ‘classical’ forestry problems also explored the issue of
utilization further. The questions considered included the supply and mar-
keting of timber, including timber nomenclature;²¹ timber quality and its
relation to silviculture;²²and efﬁciency in utilization, including protection,
the minimization of waste, and better harvesting methods.²³Also discussed
were issues relating to organization and institutionalization, especially for
education, research, training, and exchanging information.²⁴
5.1.2. Organization and Institution Building for Research
The deliberations on research and training in forestry at the ﬁrst and second
conferences identiﬁed three main topical issues. The ﬁrst of these concerned
the growth of trees, the question of how the natural growth factors could be
improved and maintained and the cost of production reduced without
impairing the capital value of the growing stock of the forest. The second
main issue was forest engineering and economics, including knowledge in
valuing logging operations and reducing waste by understanding what was
wrong with systems of utilization. The third problem was forest utilization,
with most foresters decrying the fact that it had erstwhile been neglected. An
agenda for research was therefore proposed, which included investigations
into the quantities, uses, and identities of different types of timber; research
into cheaper and more abundant timber substitutes; studies on the value of
the various timbers lying unused in different parts of the world; and research
into the chemical properties of various woods as part of a wider search for
energy alternatives given the growing depletion of coal and oil supplies.²⁵
The Imperial Environmentalist
165
²¹ Third Empire Forestry Conference, 103–20, 212–17, 241, 284, 307; Fourth Empire
Forestry Conference, 75, 249–60; Fifth Empire Forestry Conference, 254–357; Sixth

--- Page 183 ---

Together, these agendas of research and training had a common object,
to develop ‘the most efﬁcient means of supplying in the best form pos-
sible forest products applicable to the needs of man’.²⁶ The resolution on
forest research adopted at the second conference endorsed this broad
agenda. It stressed the need for research and education in silviculture;
statistical investigations into the rate of growth and volume of production
which would provide the data essential for rational and economic forest
management; forest botany, including ecology and mycology; forest
entomology; soil investigations; and meteorology. It also drew attention
to the importance of utilization, particularly wood technology—including
the testing, seasoning, and preservation of timber and investigations into
products other than timber.²⁷The resolution particularly emphasized the
importance of applied research. It envisaged a deﬁnite innovation chain,
extending from laboratories to experimental plots and on to the forest.²⁸
Finally, the resolution urged governments to recognize that research into
forestry was an issue of national importance and that it involved long
periods of work and co-ordinated effort. The state alone, it argued, could
make forestry research possible and sustain it in the long run.²⁹ In making
this argument, the resolution reinforced the position adopted in the
debate on the question of control.
Having articulated a research agenda in this broad manner, the confer-
ences sought to implement it by establishing appropriate institutions.
Delegates began by endorsing the two main proposals made by the
Canadian Forestry Association when it had proposed the convening of
the conferences in the ﬁrst place. Thus, the second conference unani-
mously endorsed the ideas of holding periodic Empire Forestry
Conferences and the setting up of an Empire Forestry Association with its
own journal. The conferences also voted for the establishment of a
Standing Committee on Empire Forestry, with a view to maintaining
continuity of action between meetings of the Empire Forestry
Conferences.³⁰
The principal thrust of the debate on organization was however on the
subject of setting up a central institution for education and research in
forestry.³¹ The discussion on this subject was begun during the ﬁrst
conference by Claude Hill, a member of the viceroy’s executive Council
The Imperial Environmentalist
166
²⁶ Dr A. W. Borthwick, Second Empire Forestry Conference, 307.
²⁷ Ibid. 249–50.
²⁸ Ibid. 249.
