--- Page 184 ---

in India. Hill argued that the proper administration of empire forest
assets necessitated the establishment of more fully equipped institutes for
research. He argued, further, that the best people ought to be employed
to train the ‘relatively small body of experts’ who should comprise the
upper controlling and administrative staff for forestry. Moreover, this
corps d’élite ought to be equipped as highly as possible to enable it to
formulate and understand how to bring either virgin or (as in the case
of India) ostensibly maltreated and semi-ruined forest to the point of
highest economic efﬁciency.³²
Hill argued that the basic question was whether it was best for each
part of the empire separately and individually to evolve its own research,
organization, and policy or whether it was possible to
create a machinery by which the whole empire will be in a position to ensure that
the larger problems relating the administration of forestry shall be submitted to
and advised upon by an authority recruited from all parts of the empire, and
which should organise the training of the forest experts and specialists of the
whole empire. Will efﬁciency, economy and enterprise be stimulated by such a
scheme or will it be preferable to leave each part of the empire to evolve its own
machinery for research and higher education?³³
Hill stated that the Indian view was that whereas local research and train-
ing for the lower grades should be provided for locally, there would be
great advantages in arranging that the higher branches of the various forest
services should receive their training, whether in fundamental principles or in
post-graduate studies, at a central institute for the whole empire, where access
would be had to the results of the highest research affecting forestry throughout
the world, and where the greatest living experts are congregated.³⁴
Hill argued further that the Indian forest service also believed that it was
best for every part of the empire if the training of the highest controlling
staff took place in the same locality as the central research institute. It was
also important to create a central clearing house of information and locate
it in this central institute. The establishment of such an institute, he said,
would mean that an esprit de corps would be created among forest ofﬁcers
throughout the empire, leading subsequently to mutual assistance and
interchange of ideas. It would also result in providing trainees with a
sound foundation in the general principle of the science of forestry and
methods of research which, he said, were ‘more or less constant’ and thus
adaptable and applicable in the varying colonial conditions.³⁵
The Imperial Environmentalist
167
³² Sir Claude Hill, First Empire Forestry Conference, 125–8.
³³ Ibid.
³⁴ Ibid.
³⁵ Ibid. 128–9.

--- Page 185 ---

According to Hill, the Indian forest service preferred Britain as a site
for such an institute. Britain was ideal not only because it was the seat of
the empire and had the ‘best choice of scientiﬁc teachers anywhere in the
empire to equip such a school’ but because it was close to the Continent.
Proximity to Europe was important because continental forests were
illustrative of the best methods of forest management and because it was
there that the theoretical works could be best illustrated with complete
object lessons. He argued that it would take a century for Indian or
dominion forests to reach a stage in any way comparable with the highly
developed and intensively worked woods of Europe.
There was broad unanimity among most discussants on the proposals
made by Claude Hill. Professor Stebbing, for example, argued that in addi-
tion to providing basic training at an advanced level before a forester went
to a colony to take up his ﬁrst appointment, such an institution could pro-
vide advanced specialized courses to men coming home on furlough after
ten to twelve years of service. Such people, he said, had previously gone to
Germany or France for the purpose. A central institute would also allow
such ofﬁcers to conduct research work and would, in fact, be the centre of
research in Britain and the place where research carried out at research
institutes in different parts of the empire could be collated.³⁶
Other foresters argued similarly. W. F. Perre, of the Imperial Forestry
Research Centre in Dehra Dun, M. A. Grainger, chief forester of the
Provincial Forest Service of British Columbia, and A. W. Borthwick, the
educational, research, and publicity ofﬁcer of the British Forestry
Commission, for example, argued that a central institution was necessary
in order to ‘collect and co-ordinate the work of other isolated research
centres’³⁷ and to ‘have one agency behind all the work and experimenta-
tion that would visualise the thing in a broad way’.³⁸
Yet another argument for the establishment of a central institution
located in Britain was advanced in the conference by Professor Groom
of the Imperial College of Science and Technology, London, and
C. E. Lane-Poole, conservator of forests, Western Australia. Groom
claimed that such an institution would enable research to take place in
Britain, where timber was being used most extensively. Moreover, a pool
of experts would be created in Britain who could advise on substitutes
when they were wanted and who, because of their familiarity with timbers
of the empire, would be able to resolve difﬁculties of nomenclature and
The Imperial Environmentalist
168
³⁶ Prof. E. P. Stebbing, First Empire Forestry Conference, 142–3.
³⁷ Dr Borthwick, ibid. 196.
³⁸ M. A. Grainger, ibid. 156.

--- Page 186 ---

certify quality to satisfy the needs of timber merchants who would other-
wise not trust documents coming from the colonies.³⁹ Lane-Poole argued
in a similar vein by stating that a centre located in Britain would mean
that forest departments in different parts of the empire would be able to
get in touch with the ‘great marketing centres’. It would also result in the
standardization of methods and a universal method of certiﬁcation of
forest products which would help the export of forest products and the
globalization of forest trade.⁴⁰
The upshot of the debate on organization was a set of resolutions that
were presented to parliament. There were three broad recommendations.
At the outset, there was one on education, which stated that systematic
schemes for forestry education ought to be established by the forest
authorities throughout the empire and that these schemes ought also to
meet the needs of those parts of the empire unable to meet their own
requirements. The resolution on education also recommended the estab-
lishment of an institution in the United Kingdom for training forest
ofﬁcers possessing natural science degrees and for organizing supplement-
ary courses for forest ofﬁcers from any part of the empire. The second
recommendation was on research, and stated that systematic research
into the formation, tending, and protection of forests ought to be associ-
ated with the training institution. The third resolution was on the estab-
lishment of an imperial forestry bureau, independent of any agricultural
bureau, with the task of collecting, co-ordinating, and disseminating
information and advice on the development of the forest resources of the
empire.⁴¹
Each of these resolutions was soon translated into imperial policy.
Soon after the ﬁrst Empire Forestry Conference, its resolutions recom-
mending education, research, and a bureau were presented to parliament
in a command paper.⁴² An interdepartmental committee on imperial
forestry was set up in autumn 1920 to prepare a scheme for giving effect
to the resolutions regarding the central institution for the training of
forest ofﬁcers. The committee was charged with determining the loca-
tion, the system of organization, including the constitution, the control
of the institution, its costs and methods of ﬁnancing it, and its research
role, in addition to deciding how best to select its students. The
committee undertook its task with vigour and presented its report to
parliament.⁴³
The Imperial Environmentalist
169
³⁹ Prof. Groom, ibid. 142.
⁴⁰ C. E. Lane Poole, ibid. 153.
⁴¹ Cmd. 865 of 1920.
⁴² Ibid.
⁴³ Cmd. 1166 of 1921.

--- Page 187 ---

The committee noted that most of its witnesses were in favour of
establishing a central institution for higher forestry training. Its report
then discussed the background of the students. It concluded that the
needs expressed by the conference would be met by accepting an
approved forestry degree course at any university as a preliminary to a
higher forestry training later at the central institution. It recommended
further that the probationers should specialize at the institution in two
out of a dozen suggested subjects. In doing so, the committee attached
great importance to special courses at the central institution.
Another of the committee’s recommendations was that while it was for
individual governments to decide what opportunities should be given to
their ofﬁcers, it ought to be a recognized part of the duties of a forest
ofﬁcer to attend at least one special course at the institution during the
earlier part of his service. Moreover, the committee endorsed the resolu-
tion of the forestry conference proposing a system of reciprocity between
the training institutions at home and abroad, whereby probationers or
forest ofﬁcers could proceed to the institution of another country for
study. The committee also recognized the beneﬁts of close association
between teaching and research and recommended a close relation
between them. Accordingly, it recommended that training and research
be combined in one institution and that trained scientiﬁc investigators be
appointed to forge a research agenda for the proposed subjects of
research—entomology, mycology, and soil science.
The committee turned its attention next to the question of the location
of the central institution. It considered three broad alternatives: as one of
the schools of a university; as an institution apart from a university but
within a university town; and ﬁnally, in the country near a forest. After
prolonged deliberations, it concluded that it would be advantageous to
locate the institution as a constituent part of a university. During the
consultations, Cambridge had objected to the idea of a central institu-
tion, while Oxford had supported the scheme and proposed to enlarge its
buildings for botany and forestry. Accordingly, the committee recom-
mended that the central institution be established there.
5.1.3. Resonant Themes
The deliberations on forest management in the ﬁrst two conferences
thus turned out to be what they were meant to be—an attempt at
imperial environmental stocktaking. Implicit was a commitment by the
empire forestry community to extend the continental European model
The Imperial Environmentalist
170

--- Page 188 ---

throughout the empire.⁴⁴ According to their vision forestry was a
production system, meant to serve the needs of a growing empire. They
deemed their own job to be serving the imperial economy. Such an out-
look is evident in the emphasis, during the deliberations, on resource
inventories, economically practicable agendas of production, forest
industries, and empire trade in forest products. The discussion on waste
was similarly a strong normative assertion of value, aimed at gaining cred-
ibility for an attitude to forest resource that had hitherto been peripheral
to the imperial mentality.
The debates on resources, production, utilization, waste, and research
also resonate in some ways with the discussions on the issue of state
control discussed in Chapter 4. At the outset, there was the ﬁrm percep-
tion that the ‘common man’ everywhere was ‘ignorant’ and ‘apathetic’.
Signiﬁcantly, as the discussion on the issue of ﬁre illustrates, this percep-
tion extended not only to non-European communities but also to white
settlers. Another important undercurrent throughout the discussion was
a strong expression of technocracy, an explicit aspect of which was the
constant reafﬁrmation of the idea that forestry policy was best made by
forestry experts with the backing of the state. Nowhere did these three
issues combine more potently than in the discussion on the big agro-
forestry dilemmas—shifting cultivation and soil erosion.
5.2. AGRO-FORESTRY DILEMMAS
Although foresters had grappled with soil erosion and shifting cultivation
in various degrees since the onset of forestry in the British Empire, it was
only in the late 1920s, the 1930s, and the 1940s that these issues blew up
into a major colonial environmental crisis. In taking up these concerns for
discussion, the conferences sought to be a centre of calculation to deter-
mine an effective pan-colonial strategy.
5.2.1. Empire Foresters and the Debate 
on Shifting Cultivation
Shifting cultivation has been a recurrent theme in recent work on colo-
nial environmental history. This is so because it offers an excellent
example with which to explore some of the pet themes of contemporary
The Imperial Environmentalist
171
⁴⁴ See, for example, RHO MSS Afr. S. 1389; RHO MSS Afr. S. 1364; RHO MSS. Ind.
Ocn. S. 168 (1–3).

--- Page 189 ---

historians—such as the hegemony of the colonial state, governmentality,
and social protest and resistance. Not surprisingly, this literature takes, as
its starting point, the idea that the colonial sciences were an intrinsic part
of an assumed homogeneous state. Consequently, little attention has
been paid to the views and standpoints of particular interest groups and
epistemic communities that worked under the auspices of the state. The
debates on shifting cultivation at the conferences are therefore an excellent
site to begin to address this important gap.
The deliberations on shifting cultivation at the Empire Forestry
Conferences began with a strong articulation of the kinds of perspectives
alluded to by most historians when they refer to colonial approaches to the
issue.⁴⁵This perspective was set forth by J. R. Ainslie, a forest ofﬁcer from
Nigeria, and R. M. White, a forest ofﬁcer from Ceylon. Ainslie began by
comparing shifting cultivation with what he referred to as ‘real agricul-
ture’. Whereas the latter was the ‘cultivation of the soil on a rotation of
crops’, the former was the ‘cultivation of crops on a rotation of soils’.
Shifting cultivation was thus the very reverse of ‘normal’ agriculture. It was
the setting ﬁre to a piece of forest during dry weather, the cultivation for a year or
two of the ground thus cleared, and, when the crops begin to fail owing to soil
deterioration, the moving on to another piece of virgin forest land, there to
recommence the process.⁴⁶
Ainslie argued that shifting cultivation was a process that caused immense
destruction. The forests of Nigeria alone had halved over the previous
eight decades and approximately 2,000 square miles were lost in Nigeria
each year. ‘One has only to pass through the numerously repeated areas of
high forests destroyed and blackened by this ﬁre-cultivation’, he said, ‘to
see in a practical and vivid form the almost incredible extent of the dam-
age done’.⁴⁷ On the one hand, this meant severe ecological disruption:
shifting cultivation caused soil erosion and deterioration. On the other, it
had major economic consequences. In local terms, it meant the creation
of timber and fuel famines. In national terms, it meant ‘an annual drain
on resources’ and, in West Africa as a region, the successive replacement
The Imperial Environmentalist
172
⁴⁵ Pouchepadass, ‘British Attitudes to Shifting Cultivation in Colonial South India’;
Rangarajan, ‘Swidden Cultivation and Forest Management in the Central Provinces’;
M. Gadgil and R. Guha, ‘The Problem of Shifting Cultivation’, in Guha and Gadgil, This
Fissured Land 150–7; and Raymond L. Bryant, ‘The Rise and Fall of Taungya Forestry’,
The Ecologist, 24/1 (Jan./Feb. 1994), 21–6; id.; ‘Shifting the Cultivator: The Politics of
Teak Regeneration in Colonial Burma’, Modern Asian Studies, 28/2 (1994); id., ‘The
Political Ecology of Forestry in Burma, 1826–1993, (Delhi, 1997).
⁴⁶ J. R. Ainslie, Second Empire Forest Conference, 325.
⁴⁷ Ibid. 326.

--- Page 190 ---

of a ‘superior’ forest type by ‘considerably inferior and more xerophytic
types’.⁴⁸ Shifting cultivation, Ainslie argued, betraying his sedentary bias,
also caused a great deal of ‘political damage’: the ‘prevention of perman-
ent settlements or dwellings’, and the
prevention of permanent improvements of all things, and in the consequent
retarding of civilisation, the encouragement of nomadic instincts and vagrant,
indolent, and thriftless habits among the people, whether they were inclined that
way or not.⁴⁹
Shifting cultivation was thus ‘as vitally important to West Africa as ﬁre
control was to Canada’. It was in fact of ‘imperial importance’: West
Africa had 14 per cent of empire forests which contained a great palm-oil
wealth, kept its rivers navigable, and protected its agricultural products to
such an extent that without it Nigeria alone would lose half its export
trade.⁵⁰ Ainslie argued, however, that shifting cultivation was a practice
that was very difﬁcult to eliminate. The ‘peculiar system of land tenure
prevailing in West Africa’ meant that wastelands and forests were not
state but communally owned.
In essence, Ainslie’s point was that reservation was a lengthy process. At
the outset, an agreement had to be reached with the chiefs and councils of
each commune or tribe. This took several years. Next, a preliminary
proclamation had to be made. This took almost a year and, since there
was no legal protection in this phase, actually served as an advertisement
to forest destroyers that ‘here is a place of convenient and compact virgin
land awaiting shifting cultivation’. The preliminary proclamation phase
was succeeded by a prolonged period of settlement and demarcation,
which took almost a decade. Only then could a ﬁnal proclamation be
made. Ainslie argued that even such legal protection did not often prove
entirely effective.⁵¹
R. M. White, who worked at that time in Ceylon, agreed with Ainslie
on the issue of the dangers of shifting cultivation. It meant, he argued, not
only the loss of invaluable timber, but the loss of ‘an agent indispensable
for building up and maintaining natural soil fertility which in Ceylon is
the ultimate source of almost all production and wealth’.⁵² Moreover,
shifting cultivation was regarded as destructive even by traditional
‘enlightened’ Sinhalese opinion. It was continual war that forced this
practice and the result was that Ceylon, from once being an exporter of
food, had now become an importer.⁵³
The Imperial Environmentalist
173
⁴⁸ Ibid. 325.
⁴⁹ Ibid. 326.
⁵⁰ Ibid. 328.
⁵¹ Ibid. 326.
⁵² R. M. White, ibid. 328.
⁵³ Ibid.

--- Page 191 ---

Ainslie and White concluded that shifting cultivation could, in princi-
ple, be allowed to proceed in areas where adequate control could be
exercised, provided a long enough rotation was insisted upon to prevent
undue soil and forest deterioration. They urged that steps be taken to
expedite control by increasing facilities for forest survey, settlement, and
demarcation.⁵⁴They argued, however, that there was often, as in Ceylon,
not enough land available to ensure sufﬁciently long rotations and that
this resulted in increased shifting cultivation in low jungle on short
rotations, thereby increasing the acreage of once fertile land annually
converted into abandoned scrub.⁵⁵ More importantly, Ainslie and White
contended that although expedient, systems of forest management
involving shifting cultivation were silviculturally unsound.⁵⁶They there-
fore argued for a total ban on shifting cultivation, especially in high
forests, especially where existing land-tenure rules and other reasons did
not enable the extension of forest reservation.
While sharing the reservations of Ainslie and White, other ofﬁcers
adopted a different perspective on the issue. One such person was Edward
Battiscombe, a forester who worked then in Kenya. Battiscombe
contended that the forest ordinance of 1911 gave the governor the right
to declare any area of Crown land a forest area and have it demarcated.
Once this was done, the district forest ofﬁcers and the political ofﬁcer
could order the local people not to do certain things. After the passing of
the forest ordinance, every ‘native’ was registered and all cultivated land
marked. Moreover, state control was established to such an extent that the
natives plant exactly where we tell them to cultivate. When wood is required for
example to make a railway, we divide the land and give it to the natives. They
plant their crops in the ﬁrst year. In the second, we plant and they put in their
crops with the trees. According to their arrangements, which are endorsed by the
magistrate, they have to look after the trees; if they fail to do so they are ﬁned. In
the third year they again plant crops and in the fourth they can also plant if the
trees are not too large by that time.⁵⁷
Battiscombe stated that such a system of forest management was indeed
shifting cultivation but argued that it was indispensable because it pro-
vided a supply of reserve labour and resulted in an increase in food
production. In Kenya, illegal shifting cultivation had been abolished but
‘legal shifting cultivation was the mainstay of forest management at the
moment’.⁵⁸
The Imperial Environmentalist
174
⁵⁴ R. M. White, Second Empire Forest Conference, 327.
⁵⁵ Ibid. 328.
⁵⁶ Ibid.
⁵⁷ E. Battiscombe, ibid. 329–30.
⁵⁸ Ibid. 330.

--- Page 192 ---

The discussion that followed was led by Professor Troup, who began by
agreeing with the views expressed by Ainslie and White. He conceded
that shifting cultivation was indeed very destructive: it left land derelict
and badly eroded soils. He argued however that it could serve a useful
silvicultural purpose in certain conditions. In Burma, for instance, planta-
tions were raised with the aid of shifting cultivation at very little cost.
In Bengal, the natural regeneration of Sal, the most important timber tree
of that country, had failed. Nothing except to cut and regenerate artiﬁ-
cially seemed possible and this would have been out of the question
except for shifting cultivation. Thus,
men who had been turned out of the reserves years before as being the destroyers
of the forest were induced to come back. The regeneration there is now almost
entirely carried out with the aid of shifting cultivation.⁵⁹
Again in East Africa, the abolition of controlled shifting cultivation
would mean doing away with the most effective means of regenerating
forest.⁶⁰
Troup argued that there were two basic issues that needed to be appreci-
ated. The circumstances under which the practice of shifting cultivation
took place needed to be checked ‘at all costs’, if any forests in the countries
concerned were to be left. However, it ought to be understood that
controlled shifting cultivation was
undoubtedly the surest and in many respects the cheapest method of carrying out
plantation work in the tropics and in dry climates. In the Deccan of India and in
many parts of the Bombay Presidency and Berar—in fact, in all climates with a
low rainfall—it is now recognized as the best method of forming plantations.⁶¹
A similar view was expressed by Owen Jones, a forestry commissioner
from Victoria, Australia. Uncontrolled shifting cultivation, he said, was
absolutely bad but ‘more or less controlled shifting cultivation’ combined
with planting would yield the desired results. Controlled shifting cultiva-
tion, along with an increase in the forest staff so that ‘where you have this
controlled shifting cultivation you may really be able to control it’, could
thus be the framework of pan-colonial policy.⁶²
The idea of controlled shifting cultivation as an empire-wide policy
was however not accepted by Ainslie. In Africa alone, he argued, a
perspective such as the above may be applicable in Kenya but not in West
Africa. He advanced two reasons, one political and the other scientiﬁc, in
The Imperial Environmentalist
175
⁵⁹ Prof. R. S. Troup, ibid. 333.
⁶⁰ Ibid.
⁶¹ Ibid.
⁶² Owen Jones, ibid. 335.

--- Page 193 ---

support of his argument. The political reason was that the native of West
Africa was, unlike his East African counterpart, a ‘member of a civilised
community’. It was therefore not possible to ‘suddenly enforce upon him
distasteful legislation and thereby control his activities’.⁶³
The scientiﬁc reason was that the cutting down of a rainforest, whether
by clear felling and burning or by chena cultivation, meant that the result-
ant forest would be of a more xerophytic type. The original evergreen
forests could not be regrown without ﬁrst working, manuring, and,
generally, intensively cultivating the forest. Ainslie argued that the same
was true for the deciduous forests in the region. Ecological progression in
this region normally meant that, inﬂuenced by geological and planetary
causes, mangrove swamps would slowly move to the sea, the rainforest to
the belt previously occupied by the mangroves, the deciduous to the
rainforest belts, the savannah to the deciduous, and the desert to
the savannah. Shifting cultivation, practised on the scale that it was in this
region, however, enormously accelerated this normal ecological progres-
sion of plants. Ainslie exclaimed: ‘I have myself seen rain-forest clearings
reverting not to deciduous forest, which is the next progressive xerophytic
grade, but straight to savannah.’⁶⁴
The discussion on shifting cultivation was thus split between advocates
of controlled shifting cultivation as an intrinsic aspect of silvicultural
policy, such as Battiscombe and Owen Jones, and those, like Ainslie and
White, who did not accept the idea that shifting cultivation could be part of
a sound silvicultural practice. In order to resolve this, the conference, on
the suggestion of Major Ralph Furse, the recruiting ofﬁcer of the Colonial
Ofﬁce and a leading proponent of forestry, decided to set up a committee
of ‘neutrals’—Furse himself, Troup, Jones, and W. A. Robertson, deputy
conservator of forests, Burma—to examine this question.
The committee began its report by deﬁning shifting cultivation as ‘a
system of temporary cultivation under which areas of forest are felled and
burned. The clearings are temporarily cultivated with agricultural crops
for one year, or a few years, and then abandoned’.⁶⁵ The committee
identiﬁed two types of shifting cultivation, the recurrent and the nomadic.
In the former ‘a sequence of felling is adopted, the same areas being cleared
and cultivated again after a period of rest, during which the forest is
allowed to recover to a certain extent’.⁶⁶ In the latter, ‘no regular sequence
of felling is adopted, the same areas being permanently abandoned’.⁶⁷
The Imperial Environmentalist
176
⁶³ Ainslie, Second Empire Forestry Conference, 333.
⁶⁴ Ainslie, ibid. 334.
⁶⁵ ‘Report of the Committee on Shifting Cultivation’, ibid. 407.
⁶⁶ Ibid.
⁶⁷ Ibid. 408.

--- Page 194 ---

The committee agreed with Ainslie and White that shifting cultivation
caused both forest destruction and political damage. It concurred that
shifting cultivation did result in the destruction of forest wealth as repres-
ented by timber, and induce a regrowth of less valuable species and
vegetation of a more arid type. The committee reasoned further that in
the recurrent type, population pressure tended to reduce the rest period,
and if carried too far, resulted in the complete sterility and often denuda-
tion of the soil, whether for forestry or for agriculture. Moreover, it
argued, shifting cultivation also caused political damage by inducing
nomadic habits on the part of the local population, discouraging agricul-
tural progress, and facilitating the evasion of taxation.⁶⁸ The committee
was however pragmatic in its recommendations. In laying down a pan-
colonial policy guideline, it struck a compromise between the perspect-
ives of White and Ainslie, on the one hand, and Battiscombe and Owen
Jones, on the other. In doing so it differentiated between geographical
contexts in the empire and was particularly cognizant of strategic
economic and political issues.
Addressing the case of West Africa ﬁrst, the committee thus began by
stating that the destruction of North American forests and its proximity
to European and American markets meant that it was a potential source
for the hardwood demands of these regions. Moreover, in times of war, it
contained the nearest large forests to the United Kingdom under British
control. The committee identiﬁed shifting cultivation as the major cause
of forest destruction in the area, affecting not only timber production,
but palm-oil production, which demanded moist soil conditions, and
agriculture—on account of the ‘sterilization’ of soil. It went on to recom-
mend that shifting cultivation be replaced by systems of permanent agri-
culture. It recommended further that the procedure to constitute forest
reserves be accelerated; that the extension of existing rights or the accrual
of new rights to shifting cultivation be prohibited in areas proposed for
reservation; and that forest ofﬁcers be given power to deal summarily with
the infringements of regulations concerned with the control of shifting
cultivation.⁶⁹
The committee, however, urged that, where possible, full use be made
of shifting cultivation as an aid to the propagation of valuable species over
suitable areas after exploitation. To this end, it recommended the forma-
tion of forest villages for the local supply of forest labour. The committee
made a similar recommendation for Ceylon, for which it recommended
The Imperial Environmentalist
177
⁶⁸ Ibid. 407–8.
⁶⁹ Ibid. 408–10.

--- Page 195 ---

that shifting cultivation be used to assemble marketable timber stands in
forest reserves, and gradually be eliminated and replaced by permanent
wet- and dry-soil cultivation in other areas.⁷⁰
For East Africa, the committee ratiﬁed the existing policy of extending
controlled shifting cultivation in reserved forests, stating that it would
materially assist in increasing the forest wealth of the colony by affording
a cheap and efﬁcient means of propagating the more valuable species of
trees and by increasing the supply of local forest labour.⁷¹ Finally for
India, it stated that existing legislation was adequate and recommended
that protection against shifting cultivation could therefore be extended
by reserving the land which ought to be maintained under forests. It also
urged the formation of forest villages for the practice of controlled shift-
ing cultivation inside reserves as a silvicultural operation.⁷²
The resolution adopted by the conference unanimously approved the
recommendations made by the committee. It stated that the practice of
shifting cultivation was a serious menace except when controlled as an
integral part of forest management. It stated however that if controlled
from a forestry point of view, shifting cultivation was a useful and neces-
sary tool in silvicultural operations. It went on to urge governments to
‘take such measures as may be necessary to deal with the situation’ in
cognizance of the particular demands of each geographical and ecological
area and political context in the empire.⁷³
The debate on shifting cultivation policy was basically about trying to
reconcile the paradigmatic goals of forestry with regionally speciﬁc prob-
lems. While there was consensus over the former, there was considerable
dispute over the latter. The disputes were resolved by agreeing on the
paradigmatic goals of forestry while being pragmatic when it came to the
speciﬁcs. Thus, shifting cultivation was deemed bad because it potentially
affected the revenue that the state could accrue from forests—from the
sale of hardwood and other products. However, if controlled, it could be
useful in some cases, especially in contexts where the cost of employing
labour for artiﬁcial plantations could be avoided by a contractual agree-
ment with shifting cultivators, whereby they would provide labour for
plantations free in exchange for being allowed to cultivate in forest lands
chosen by foresters. The basis of such an agreement stemmed from
silvicultural considerations that reﬂected the predominant ideological
orientation of continental forestry which, needless to say, resonated with
The Imperial Environmentalist
178
⁷⁰ ‘Report of the Committee on Shifting Cultivation’, 410.
⁷¹ Ibid.
⁷² Ibid. 410–11.
⁷³ Resolution 10, ‘Shifting Cultivation’, ibid. 386–7.

--- Page 196 ---

the priorities of the colonial state, even at the expense of the local peoples.
It is this shared ideological orientation that allowed foresters like
Battiscombe on the one hand and White on the other, coming as they did
from different regional experiences, to agree on a pan-colonial policy
guideline. It is also this orientation that informed their approach to the
second of the issues being discussed in this section, soil erosion.
5.2.2. Foresters and the Politics of Soil Erosion
In 1943, the soil conservation board of the government of Palestine
issued an annual calendar with a rather unusual theme. The four scenes
therein, and the captions beside them, sought to draw public attention to
soil conservation. They offered the reader a choice between the utopia of
a garden and the dystopia of a desert. In essence, the choice rested on
whether to allow goats—deemed a symbol of bad human husbanding of
nature—to destroy the vegetation, or indeed to take proactive measures,
such as tree plantations on sand dunes and ﬁelds.
This calendar was symptomatic of an important Zeitgeistduring the inter-
war years globally. In the colonial world from the mid 1930s onward,
colonial ofﬁcials—bureaucrats, agriculturists, irrigation engineers, and
foresters—had begun to short themselves hoarse about the problem, which,
they claimed, had stated to threaten not just the physical environment but
ultimately the colonial economy.⁷⁴Similarly, in the United States, following
the Dust Bowl, the issue of soil erosion became part of popular culture,
thanks, in part, to the writings of literary luminaries like John Steinbeck. Soil
erosion was also the locus of a new environmentalist sentiment, expressed,
for example, in the work of the inﬂuential William Vogt, a sentiment that
began to rival, in terms of policy impact, that of the conservation ideologues
of the progressivist era.⁷⁵ Moreover, the work of the newly established soil
science research centres at the major land grant colleges began to attract
worldwide attention, creating an invisible college of scientiﬁc consensus.
Many British colonial scientists corresponded with their American counter-
parts, and some even arranged to visit and work in the labs of senior scientists
in institutions such as the University of California at Berkeley.⁷⁶
The Imperial Environmentalist
179

Medit. S. 23; RHO MSS W. Ind. S. 58; RHO MSS Medit. S. 22; RHO MSS Afr. S. 1741
(6); and RHO MSS Brit. Emp. S. 481.
⁷⁵ William Vogt, A Road to Survival (New York, 1948).
⁷⁶ See e.g. the correspondence between G. Milne and Clements Gilman (RHO MSS
Brit. Emp. S. 457 (20)).

Given the sheer intensity and global nature of the problem of soil
erosion, it is not surprising that there is a substantial body of historical
literature on this phenomenon. The work on the British Empire thus far
has focused largely on East, Central, and Southern Africa.⁷⁷ It begins by
noticing the extent to which the problem of soil erosion had become an
important issue in colonial environmental politics by the mid 1930s
across these regions. In Rhodesia, for example, declining crop yields had
prompted farmers to raise the issue of ‘soil wastage’ at the 1931 congress
of the Rhodesia Agricultural Union. This had eventually resulted in the
formation of the Soil Conservation Advisory Councils in 1934, which
served as the ﬁrst formal framework for communication between farmers
and various governmental ofﬁcials.⁷⁸ Throughout East and Central
Africa and in the Colonial Ofﬁce similarly, the problem of soil erosion
had become an important issue of governmental concern throughout
the 1930s.⁷⁹
In seeking to explain the origins of the ofﬁcial concern about soil
conservation in the British Empire, the above-mentioned literature has
identiﬁed four key issues. First, there were anxieties about security and land
ownership among the white settler communities in the wake of the Great
Depression.⁸⁰ Secondly, there was a wider international scientiﬁc concern
with soil conservation following the dust bowl in the United States during
the period.⁸¹ Thirdly, there was a great deal of worry about demographic
increases (of both human and animal populations) and the consequence of
this on the carrying capacity of land.⁸² Last, but by no means least, there
were fears about desiccation, aridity, and impending drought.⁸³
The Imperial Environmentalist
182
⁷⁷ Among the most important of the historical studies that appeared in the 1980s on
soil conservation were: Anderson, ‘Depression, Dust Bowl, Demography and Drought’;
W. Beinart, ‘Soil Erosion, Conservationism and Ideas about Development: A Southern
African Exploration, 1900–1960’, Journal of Southern African Studies, 11/1 (1984);
R. Whitlow, ‘Soil Conservation History in Zimbabwe’, Journal of Soil and Water
Conservation, 43/4 (1988); J. E. G. Sutton, ‘Irrigation and Soil Conservation in African
Agricultural History’, Journal of African History, 25 (1984); K. B. Showers and G. M.
Malahleha, ‘Oral Evidence in Historical Environmental Impact Assessment: Soil
Conservation in Lesotho in the 1930s and 1940s’, Journal of Southern African Studies, 18/2
(1992).

The response of colonial states to soil erosion, according to this analysis,
was shaped by an approach that emphasized scientiﬁc research on various
aspects of soil conservation. These studies, in turn, were oriented toward
demonstrating the need for better husbandry under strictly controlled
conditions of land management. Across East and Central Africa and other
parts of the empire, therefore, such studies ‘demonstrated’ the need for
enforcing speciﬁc technical methods of land management and in some
areas, forcibly destocking overgrazed pastures.⁸⁴
According to this literature, the issue of soil erosion was seen by the
colonial state largely as a technical problem with technological and
engineering solutions.⁸⁵ As a result the entire debate on soil conservation
was couched in terms that emphasized the role of the scientiﬁc expert.
Moreover, given the social roots of the technological experts, the nature of
their technical interventions was by no means value-neutral. On the
contrary, they reﬂected the priorities of settler populations—mainly
increasing agricultural production—and the biases of settlers against
indigenous peoples. An important aspect of the conservationist ideology
perpetrated at this time, therefore, was a concern to effect what might be
termed technocratic closure, to restrict and control the access of local
populations to natural resources under the guise of ecological improve-
ment. The welfare of the soil, it has thus been argued, emerged as the
‘cutting edge of justiﬁcation for intervention in peasant agriculture’.⁸⁶
The discussions on soil erosion at the Empire Forestry Conferences
largely corroborate such an analysis. They began by painting a picture of
impending ecological doom and were premissed upon a unanimous
agreement that there was ‘no doubt about the effect of deforestation on
erosion’.⁸⁷ Speaker after speaker from different parts of the empire
declaimed about the seriousness of erosion in his region of the world.
In India, the river beds in the Himalayan foothills and elsewhere had
dried up because of deforestation.⁸⁸ In Ceylon, there was ‘not a river’
which had clear water during the rains. Rivers and streams were mostly
‘highways for conveying thousands of tons of the ﬁnest soil to the sea’.⁸⁹
The Imperial Environmentalist

⁸⁴ Anderson, ‘Depression, Dust Bowl, Demography and Drought’, 334–8; Showers

60; Michael Stocking, ‘Breaking New Ground’, in Melissa Leach and Robin Mearns (eds.),
The Lie of the Land: Challenging Received Wisdom on the African Environment (Oxford,
1996).
⁸⁶ Beinart, ‘Soil Erosion, Conservationism and Ideas about Development’, 83.

In parts of Australia, the destruction of forest cover had caused serious
erosion on the hilly areas and water levels had been greatly lowered.⁹⁰
In New Zealand, steep hillsides were alternations of bare landslides and
strips of scrub and bracken. Large areas of shingle moved slowly onto the
ﬂats and gradually raised the river beds, resulting in serious deterioration
of the lands and frequently also in the aggrading of river beds followed by
the wandering of the stream course. Moreover, the loss of the plant cover-
ings on these slopes increased ﬂoods in rivers.⁹¹ In Canada, there were
many instances where the effect of deforestation, particularly on run-offs,
was pronounced.⁹²
In Kenya and East Africa, entire hillsides had been denuded, and very
little soil was left. Rivers that were once perennial were now only seasonal.⁹³
In South Africa, desiccation had been a threat ‘for a long time’. Hippo
pools, for example, were no longer in existence except in the form of dried-
up holes in the river beds. Forests which had once formed the habitat of
various wild animals such as buffalo and lions had disappeared and had
been replaced by different types of vegetation, accompanied by arid condi-
tions. Moreover, actual decline in rainfall had recently been measured: there
was a marked diminution in rainfall over the past four to ﬁve decades.⁹⁴
Again, in Nigeria, particularly in the regions with average rainfall
around ten to twelve inches per annum, there was terrible erosion. Strong
winds in the winter months drove along, picked up soils from deforested
areas, and deposited them either in dry stream beds to form sand drifts or
in the larger rivers from where they washed down to form the Niger delta.
In the rainy season that followed the long dry summer, the torrential rains
carried the soil to the rivers which, after one downpour, resembled liquid
mud, with the ‘consistency of thin porridge’. This porridge-like water was
then carried down the rivers and, as the gradient decreased, either spread
over the agricultural land or ﬁlled up the river beds. This in turn exposed
a bigger surface area to evaporation and caused further deposition, thus
creating a multiplier effect. The result was that most of northern Nigeria,
which had once been covered by forests, was now drastically suffering
ecological degradation and assuming desert-like conditions.⁹⁵
Having described the extent and seriousness of soil erosion across the
empire, the discussants dwelt on the ecological and economic importance
of soils. Soil, argued one forester, was ‘man’s capital’. Its deterioration led
The Imperial Environmentalist
184
⁹⁰ E. Julius, Third Empire Forestry Conference, 192.
⁹¹ Phillip Turner, ibid. 189.
⁹² E. H. Finlayson, ibid. 186.
⁹³ H. M. Gardner, ibid. 191.
⁹⁴ J. D. Keet, Fourth Empire Forestry Conference, 114–15.
⁹⁵ J. R. Ainslie, ibid. 118.

--- Page 202 ---

the way to a lower standard of living and, eventually, desertiﬁcation.
Erosion cut at the very roots of an economic structure. Soil and water
conservation were thus issues that affected the community at large.⁹⁶
In making these points, many foresters had recourse to history. For exam-
ple, C. G. Trevor, inspector-general of forests for India, argued: ‘If one
compared the grandeur of ancient kingdoms with their present day decay
and their ancient wealth with the amount produced today, one could only
be driven to one conclusion, that the present decay of these countries was
largely based on the deterioration of the moisture that lay in the earth.’
The palace of the ‘King of Kings’, Darius, in Susa was now in an unin-
habited wilderness desert. Mesopotamia, which once produced all the
revenues of Persia, had degenerated into a dreary waste. The hanging
gardens of Babylon were now a ‘rubbish dump’, a result of the destruction
of the forests of the hills and thus of the ‘ﬁnest irrigation system in the
world’. Trevor claimed that the lessons of history showed that deforesta-
tion, by lowering the moisture content of the soil and thus decreasing the
water supplies of the country, had done ‘more damage than any war and
had resulted in the destruction of the greatest empires’.⁹⁷
In their diagnosis of the causes of soil erosion and depletion, the
participating foresters invoked the Malthusian spectre. They pointed
their ﬁngers ﬁrst at agricultural populations who, they claimed, were
encroaching on forests. They argued that with demographic increase and
the unavailability of more land for agriculture, there was an increasing
pressure on marginal lands, including forests, for agricultural purposes
and grazing. In Nyasaland, for example, ‘a dense and rapidly increasing
population’ was increasingly moving towards lands that were of only
limited and temporary agricultural value.⁹⁸ In the Gold Coast, the
growth of population meant a rapid increase in the demand for land, and
for the consequent destruction of woodlands. Forests there, it was
claimed, were receding at the rate of 300 square miles per year.⁹⁹ The
problem was similar in Nigeria and other parts of tropical Africa.¹⁰⁰
The ostensibly increasing conversion of forests and other marginal lands
to agriculture was seen by foresters as producing the problem of soil erosion
in three important ways. First, the use of forests and other lands not ideally
suited for agriculture and the overcultivation of traditional agricultural
lands meant that soils, across the tropical empire, were beginning seriously
to deteriorate. The process of periodic reconditioning of the soils was being
The Imperial Environmentalist
185
⁹⁶ A. P. F. Hamilton, ibid. 80.
⁹⁷ C. G. Trevor, ibid. 109–10.
⁹⁸ J. B. Clements, ibid. 214.
⁹⁹ H. W. Moore, ibid. 220.
¹⁰⁰ J. R. Ainslie, ibid. 217–19.

--- Page 203 ---

critically disturbed.¹⁰¹Secondly, methods of cultivation normally associated
with sparse populations in regions of unlimited forests were increasingly
being used by large anchored populations in areas of sparse forest. In
Nyasaland, for example, ﬁnger millet was being grown by the ‘soil burning’
method although the people had become more or less anchored and had to
use two- to three-year regrowths for burning rather than loppings from
large trees. This led to an acceleration in the indiscriminate removal of
natural vegetation and consequently in erosion and desiccation.¹⁰²Thirdly,
overgrazing caused a great deal of destruction of vegetation and often
prevented fresh seedlings from taking root.¹⁰³In addition to identifying the
cause of soil erosion as the ‘encroachment’ on forests by agricultural
populations, foresters argued that it was also a consequence of poorly
planned policies of settlement¹⁰⁴ and the unrestricted operations of timber
companies whose practices drastically affected stream ﬂow.¹⁰⁵
There was a considerable degree of unanimity among colonial foresters
from different parts of the empire on how the problem of soil conservation
was to be addressed. Three broad and often complementary types of
approaches were suggested. The ﬁrst envisaged technological ﬁxes, such as
bore-well digging, to generate irrigation. The second involved adopting
various forms of coercive practices to ensure that local peoples co-operated
with governmental policies. Such practices included measures such as the
forced redistribution of entire populations. They also included the enact-
ment of legislation to protect sensitive lands, such as watersheds, from
agriculture and to curb practices seen as ecologically destructive, such as
grass burning early in the season which led to ﬁerce and uncontrolled ﬁres.
The third type of approach was to co-opt local peoples and practices.
This approach varied considerably from one context to another. There
was, to begin with, a call for education and practical demonstration. There
was, in addition, the advocacy of methods of environmental management
that involved local labour, such as the taung-ya system of regulating shift-
ing cultivation. There was similarly an enthusiasm for methods such as the
‘three-ﬁeld system’, wherein local cultivators were allowed to place two out
of three ﬁelds under crops each season, allowing the third to regain some
of its fertility; and systems such as rotational grazing, where, similarly,
a third of the area was to be left ungrazed during the summer months.
The Imperial Environmentalist
186
¹⁰¹ J. R. Clements, Fourth Empire Forestry Conference, 214.
¹⁰² Ibid. 214–15.
¹⁰³ See. e.g. Dr Unwin, Third Empire Forestry Conference, 189 and J. R. Ainslie, Fourth
Empire Forestry Conference, 118.
¹⁰⁴ Phillip Turner, Fourth Empire Forestry Conference, 189.
¹⁰⁵ E. H. Finlayson, ibid. 120.

--- Page 204 ---

Finally, there was the encouragement, in certain areas, of policies that
encouraged the selection and allocation of village forest areas, using land
least suited to agriculture. The idea was to give local peoples, in areaswhere
scarcity of wood was being experienced, an interest in protection by
placing forested lands directly under the control of village headmen.¹⁰⁶
In discussing possible approaches to alleviating the problem of soil
erosion, foresters were guided by two main considerations. The ﬁrst was
to ensure maximum productivity and the best possible use of forest lands.
In order to do this, foresters advocated the conducting of detailed surveys
of land classiﬁcation to determine the most economic use to which each
portion of the catchment should be put, and a delineation of the sectors
which needed to be maintained permanently as forests.¹⁰⁷ There was,
argued a forester from India, a need for ‘land planning schemes to pro-
duce best land use, get the necessities of life from land to get maximum
production out of it and to put each land to the right purpose’.¹⁰⁸
Similar views were expressed by a forester from British Guinea who
argued that the issue was largely one of economics, a question of which
crop and practice was best suited given social and ecological conditions.
There was, he argued, a need for a proper study of the economics of mar-
ginal lands. There was no reason why under certain conditions practices
such as shifting cultivation could not be made more proﬁtable economic
propositions than forest crops. It was therefore urgent, he argued, for
proper economic surveys to be undertaken to determine what the
optimum yield from these lands was.¹⁰⁹
Underlying the perspectives on the problem of soil erosion advocated
by the empire forestry community at the conferences was the conception
that forests constituted a productive entity distinct from others, especially
agriculture. A related idea was that agriculture was not necessarily the best
form of land use. Such perspectives, as pointed out in earlier chapters, had
been articulated by supporters of forestry from the early nineteenth
century and had become an important element of the colonial forester’s
mentality since the days of Brandis.
The Imperial Environmentalist
187
¹⁰⁶ What is particularly notable is the unanimity of approaches from foresters from
different parts of the empire. Each of the above approaches was suggested in different ways
by the various foresters participating in the discussion: J. B. Clements, from Nyasaland; J.R.
Ainslie, from Nigeria; H. W. Moor, from the Gold Coast; R. W. Thornton from Basutoland,
Bechuanaland and Swaziland; Prof. R. S. Troup of the Imperial Forestry Institute and
formerly, the Indian forest service; E. A. Garland from India; G. N. Sale from Trinidad;
D. G. Thomas from Sierra Leone; F. M. Oliphant of the Colonial Forestry Services.
¹⁰⁷ A. V. Galbraith, Fourth Empire Forestry Conference, 113.
¹⁰⁸ A. P. F. Hamilton, ibid. 81.
¹⁰⁹ C. Swabey, ibid. 83.

--- Page 205 ---

Signiﬁcantly, similar ideas began to be expressed by forest ofﬁcers
recruited directly from the empire, as opposed to the United Kingdom.
Harnam Singh-Pathania, a forester of Indian origin, for example, argued
that good land management had to be based on proper surveys. He
argued that land ought to be examined by units of areas and that any areas
not ﬁt for cultivation ought to be set aside for afforestation. The bigger
lands, he argued further, ought to be transferred to the forest department
and the smaller ones converted into village forests. He advocated the
establishment of land utilization boards to oversee such processes.
Moreover, he urged that attempts be made to convince other departments
about the need to set up small forest areas which would meet the fuel-
wood needs of local populations, thereby saving farmland manure now
being used as the only fuel.¹¹⁰
Another aspect to the foresters’ perspective on the issue of soil erosion
stemmed from their understanding of the ‘indirect effects’ of forests. They
argued that mature forests are the best preserver of both climate and soils,
and good soil the best reservoir of water.¹¹¹The solution of the problem of
erosion therefore was to create forest reserves wherein denuded areas
would be reforested. Here the basic principle was to preserve vegetation
and to increase the area of forests. Another principle was to create sub-
sidiary control measures, such as river plantings, and to establish minor
structures on the upper reaches of streams to prevent further erosion,
pending improvement to highland sectors.¹¹² There was concern also for
the adequate protection of forest areas from ﬁre and for the revocation of
methods under which highland areas were leased for grazing.¹¹³
The response of foresters to agriculture-related issues such as soil
erosion was largely shaped by the belief that they were simultaneously eco-
nomic producers and environmental guardians. Such a mindset, as argued
earlier, has its roots in the very disciplinary ethos of continental forestry, in
which colonial foresters were trained and which they imbibed. Colonial
foresters therefore saw their duty as that of diagnosing such problems
quickly and solving them effectively. To adapt a phrase initially used to
describe colonial conservationists in general, theirs was an ideology of
‘doom and resurrection’ stemming from the fear that but for urgent action
marginal lands, especially forests, would be taken over and destroyed.¹¹⁴
Moreover, implicit in their vision—as vividly illustrated by the govern-
ment of Palestine calendar—was the conception of themselves as saviours.
The Imperial Environmentalist
188
¹¹⁰ Harnam Singh-Pathania, Fourth Empire Forestry Conference, 104.
¹¹¹ J. D. Keet, ibid. 116.
¹¹² A. V. Galbraith, ibid. 113.
¹¹³ Ibid.
¹¹⁴ Beinart, ‘Introduction: The Politics of Colonial Conservation’.

--- Page 206 ---

The approach of the imperial forestry community also reﬂected their
underlying technocratic rationality. To begin with, they identiﬁed the
problem of soil erosion as primarily an environmental one. They never
raised the question of the social reasons, other than demographic, which
forced people to engage in ostensibly ecologically destructive practices.
Their diagnosis was often couched in patronizing terms, blaming tradi-
tional peoples for mismanaging the lands. Their purported solutions in
turn were either directly technological or involved various forms of
technocratic closure, ranging from outright exclusion to systems that
controlled and channelled local ecological practices. In this, they were
similar to other colonial technological communities which began to
advocate conservation at that time. Indeed, their approach very clearly
ﬁts the chart made by Piers Blaikie illustrating what he calls the ‘classic’ or
‘colonial’ approach to agro-ecological conﬂicts (Table 5.1).
