5.2.3. Wider Issues
The diagnosis of the nature and causes of colonial agro-ecological crises
made by foresters at the Empire Forestry Conferences during the period
1923–52 indicates a sharp critique of the attitudes adopted by other colo-
nial governmental agencies, technical and non-technical. There was, to
begin with, belief that governmental bureaucrats could not comprehend
the extent of such problems. It was argued that governments did not
appreciate the indirect advantages of forests since they seemed inclined to
regard their forest properties ‘more and more from the monetary or ﬁnan-
cial point of view’.¹¹⁵ The public were similarly perceived as not being
aware of the importance of the connection between erosion and deforesta-
tion and the impact of this on water supply.¹¹⁶ Delegates pointed out that
there were, in some parts of the empire, alarmists who argued that forests
were not the most efﬁcient ways of conserving the water supplies.¹¹⁷
In addition to governments and the public, foresters at the Empire
Forestry Conferences also blamed other colonial technical communities,
such as engineers and geologists. In Nigeria, one forester claimed, geolo-
gists had begun to argue that there were no records of fall of water level
and that there was no evidence of desiccation. However, he pointed out
that the old historical evidence corroborated the beliefs of the foresters
and not that of the geologists.¹¹⁸ Similarly, a South African delegate
claimed that in his country, reservoir engineers, realizing that all plant
growth, like all living matter, must use up water to live and grow, had
begun to demand that catchment areas be paved with bare rock or land
covered with malthoid. He pointed out that such colonial ofﬁcials did not
realize that in the absence of ﬁltered storage, which made up water supply
and also the control and utility thereof, it was not vegetation, but soil, as
a product of decomposition of vegetation and of climate, that was the
reservoir for rainfall.¹¹⁹ Finally, in Canada, a great deal of the problem of
stream ﬂow caused by timber operations and pulp, lumber, and paper
manufacture was, argued foresters, a result of engineers not appreciating
the importance of forests.¹²⁰
The worst criticism was, however, reserved for agricultural ofﬁcers and
populations engaged in agriculture. Speakers from across the empire
claimed that agricultural scientists often ignored the advice given by
The Imperial Environmentalist
190
¹¹⁵ Richmond, Third Empire Forestry Conference, 188.
¹¹⁶ Gay, ibid. 193.
¹¹⁷ J. D. Keet, Fourth Empire Forestry Conference, 116.
¹¹⁸ J. R. Ainslie, ibid. 119.
¹¹⁹ J. D. Keet, ibid. 114.
¹²⁰ E. H. Finlayson, ibid. 123.

--- Page 208 ---

foresters. They also argued that agriculturists were ignorant and apathetic
about the importance of forests and forestry. This perception was
furthered by the frustration that colonial governments often preferred the
views of agricultural ofﬁcers over those of foresters on land. Some
delegates therefore launched themselves into a severe condemnation of
agriculturists:
At the very beginning, man existed without agriculture; he subsisted upon the
forests. It was the agriculturist who dispossessed the forest and raped the earth
wherever he went. The forest services must now accept the responsibility to repair
the sins of the agriculturists. I suggest that foresters should resume their place of
being the complete conservationists . . . When I recall how . . . forests were
pushed aside, how governments of the day, through their land departments, threw
open sections to all and sundry with the idea merely of dispossessing the unem-
ployed and of cashing the capital resources of the country, then I ﬁnd myself
believing that only by expression of the utmost energy of the Forest Services both
in defence and offence can we redeem our traditional responsibilities to the earth.
Although colonial foresters were quick to blame other communities,
they themselves refused to entertain criticism of their practices made by
colonial ofﬁcials who were not foresters. A case in point was an exchange
between foresters, agriculturists, and other scientists on the issue of the
impact of exotics in Southern Africa, and particularly their role in
producing aridity and erosion. John Phillips, a professor of the University
of Witwatersrand, argued that in the semi-arid parts of South Africa,
stands of exotic species such as gums, wattles, and some pines resulted in
washing and erosion whereas those of natural species did not.¹²¹ He
argued that while springs had ceased ﬂowing in exotic plantations,
springs and streams in the open veld, where that veld had been looked
after, did not cease ﬂowing. Further, research had explicitly shown that
gums and wattles had water requirements and rates of water usage and
transpiration considerably greater than indigenous species. This was true
especially of the pines. Moreover, argued Phillips, agriculturists, pastoral-
ists, and horticulturists could point out that grass herbage and fruit trees
were either stunted and poorly bearing or that they died altogether when
within twenty to thirty feet of shelter belts of gums. In addition, there was
evidence that much of the litter that fell from these exotics was not
incorporated into the soil, but lay on the surface for a long period,
ultimately largely disappearing in a gaseous form, not being incorporated
into the humus of the soil. Given this kind of evidence, argued Phillips, it
The Imperial Environmentalist
191
¹²¹ Prof. John Phillips, ibid. 124.

--- Page 209 ---

was imperative that these matters be looked into ‘scientiﬁcally and
dispassionately’.¹²²There was, he contended, need for critical, long-term
experimental research into these matters, including issues such as the rela-
tion of planting to water supply, the effect of habitat development studies
on the soil, and the relation between afforestation and succession.¹²³ In
particular, he argued that it was a ‘mark of defeat’ if, being charged with
the silviculture of the native forests, exotics had to be invoked for
management when other methods could be found for the purpose.¹²⁴
A similar sentiment was echoed by others. Roberts, a South African
engineer, for example, stated that from the point of view of anti-erosion
work, indigenous bush was of great advantage because indigenous forests
did not draw on the water in the soil as heavily as the exotics did. Also, he
argued, their growth was such that they did not stand alone. They were
accompanied by all forms of shrubs, grasses, and other vegetation which
blended together to form a complete protective mat.¹²⁵
Foresters, however, totally dismissed such ideas. J. A. Keet, a member of
the South African forest department, who spoke ﬁrst, made a spirited
defence of exotics, which he claimed had been criticized by many of his
compatriots. Denying that in planting exotics South African foresters had
ignored an ecological outlook, he argued that just as a farmer does not plant
the exotic apple in the low veld or the exotic citrus on the high veld, foresters
did not plant exotic custer pine on the coast land of Zululand or the exotic
gum on the mountains of the Free State. The issue, he claimed, was not
whether exotics were good or not but under what conditions they were
appropriate. Indeed, the South African forester could ‘teach the world
something about the knack of matching species to growing sites’. The
South African forester, he claimed, worked in conformity with ecological
principles. He stated further that there was no evidence to indicate that
exotic trees acclimatized to their new home were any more desiccators of
their climate than they were in their native habitat. Moreover, he claimed,
the exotic trees in South Africa, the gum, the pine, and the wattle, often
acted as ‘pioneers’ to indigenous species, even to the moisture loving ones,
and showed more clearly than any measure of soil measure, light intensities,
and temperatures that ‘we are on the right lines with our exotic species’.¹²⁶
In response, some delegates who were non-foresters steered a middle
course. Carlson, an elderly South African agricultural ofﬁcer, for exam-
ple, stated that the issue was not one of indigenous versus exotic species
The Imperial Environmentalist
192
¹²² Prof. John Phillips, Fourth Empire Forestry Conference, 125.
¹²³ Ibid. 127.
¹²⁴ Ibid. 126.
¹²⁵ Roberts, ibid. 131.
¹²⁶ J. D. Keet, ibid. 117.

--- Page 210 ---

but of the right mixture of species. Exotics, he argued, could be deployed
provided they were mixed adequately with others.¹²⁷ The forestry com-
munity however rejected such arguments and provided unanimous
applause for the hard-liners like Keet.¹²⁸
5.2.4. Resonant Themes
The discussions on shifting cultivation and soil erosion at the Empire
Forestry Conferences show that empire foresters approached such ques-
tions largely from their disciplinary standpoint as foresters. They emphat-
ically articulated and argued for an explicit paradigm of dealing with the
forests of the empire, one that emphasized the silvicultural goals of contin-
ental forestry—in turn geared to the needs of a modernizing empire.
The discussion on shifting cultivation shows that rather than being
unilaterally opposed to shifting cultivation, as existing historical studies
have claimed, colonial foresters located the issue within wider priorities of
forest management, and especially the question of the political economy
of forest regeneration. Their approach ultimately was to control and
adapt what was otherwise considered an ecologically retrograde practice
to enable them to manage forests according to the principles of contin-
ental forestry. Shifting cultivation, with appropriate pragmatic controls,
thereby emerged as a mainstay of pan-colonial forest policy.
Again, in the case of the problem of soil conservation, it was a
commitment to continental European forestry principles that broadly
dictated the approach of British colonial foresters. In short, what distin-
guished the approach of foresters to the issue of soil erosion, as with shifting
cultivation, was their predominant concern with promoting the cardinal
forestry principles and their self-perception as environmental guardians.
The deliberations on shifting cultivation and soil conservation at the
conferences also reafﬁrm the traditional biases of foresters against local
peoples. People on the move, such as swidden cultivators, were especially
seen as doubly immoral—in the sense of being bad citizens (evading
taxes) and in being environmentally proﬂigate. What is interesting,
however, is that there were a different set of biases against those of their
own kin who were not foresters. For example, white settlers were castig-
ated for their ostensibly wasteful and apathetic attitudes towards forest
resources, as were governments and civil servants who did not as yet sub-
scribe to the vision of the forestry community. The discussions thus posed
The Imperial Environmentalist
193
¹²⁷ Carlson, ibid. 131.
¹²⁸ The discussion on exotics, ibid.

--- Page 211 ---

the differences in attitudes between foresters and other people—white or
native—in categorical terms: non-foresters, including members of legis-
latures, were wrong or misguided and therefore had to be educated and
civilized. Such views arguably had their roots in the growing scientism
and technocracy of the empire forestry community. It is also possible that
the increasing convergence of scientiﬁc disciplines in the forestry realm in
the twentieth century contributed to this feeling of superiority.¹²⁹
The differences between foresters and others on shifting cultivation and
soil erosion also show that the colonial state was as differentiated and deseg-
regated as it was homogeneous. Indeed, although the basic response of colo-
nial foresters was in many respects similar to that of the ‘colonial ofﬁcials’
described in the analysis made in recent work on colonial environmental his-
tory, their motivations lay in a concern systematically to manage classiﬁed
forest lands (actual and potential) according to the principles of forest
science. Seen from this overall perspective, the approaches of other colonial
ofﬁcials, such as agriculturists, appeared to foresters as fundamentally ﬂawed.
5.3. CONCLUSION
Perhaps the most signiﬁcant data that emerges from the analysis of the
debates at the conferences is the incredible degree to which foresters in the
British Empire in the ﬁrst half of the twentieth century pursued and tried
to implement a silvicultural, management, and utilization agenda that
had ﬁrst been developed within the context of continental Europe and
then transplanted to British India in the later nineteenth century.
Implicit within this agenda was a total commitment to the wider matrix
of relations between science, technology, the economy, society, and the
natural environment, embedded within the continental European
programme. Thus, the discussions on these technical aspects of forest
management were largely an elaboration and articulation of the German
forestry paradigm—an attempt to solve particular technical problems
The Imperial Environmentalist
194
¹²⁹ Of particular importance here is the role of ecology. There was active correspon-
dence between R. S. Troup and A. Tansley on teaching botany and plant ecology to
foresters in 1932 (OU FOR/SF/8/2). Reading the papers of the various colonial scientists
of the period (see n.75 for references), it is also evident that the issue of environmental
change was being approached with an increasing emphasis on interdisciplinary work.
Another point of note is that this was also the era of great surveys in the environmental sci-
ences—almost everywhere, there were studies that explored the terrain, both intellectual as
well as physical. There was also a sense of crisis and conﬁdence—stemming from a coding
of agro-ecological problems as signiﬁcant, as well as the sense that they could be addressed
through good science and public policy based on scientiﬁc advice.

--- Page 212 ---

arising out of an attempt to adapt German forestry methods to the differ-
ent ecological contexts in the empire.
Such problem-solving attempts were largely attempts at resolving the
tension between the generality of the programmatic mandate—to imple-
ment the continental system in the empire—and the particularity of
the ecological problems in speciﬁc regions. It is important to note that the
resolution of this tension almost always afﬁrmed the paradigmatic
mandate. This observation is signiﬁcant in light of attempts by contem-
porary historians to argue that imperial forestry practices were site
speciﬁc, varied from one colonial context, and were not generalizable.
While these commentators are indeed correct to argue for the importance
of place and time, their region-speciﬁc focus biases, and often blinds
them to the bigger drama being played out across a more global stage.¹³⁰
The existence of the bigger drama is evident across a range of topics
discussed at the conferences. To take one example, on education and
research, the debates show imperial foresters explicitly thinking beyond
the speciﬁc needs and issues of their respective regions. On the contrary,
they cogently articulated a framework that was centrally co-ordinated
and, in doing so, continued a tradition of policy thinking which, as
argued in Chapter 4, had been an important element of the empire
forestry community’s agenda since the late nineteenth century.
The wider relevance of the empire-wide drama for local problems was,
however, most evident in the deliberations over shifting cultivation and soil
erosion. In the case of the former issue, the manner of resolution is itself illus-
trative: in resolving the problem, the foresters were driven, not by dogma, as
suggested by some historians, but purely by pragmatic considerations that
reﬂected both their indoctrination in the continental forestry tradition and
their wider commitment to developing forest resources for industry and
imperial expansion in speciﬁc regions of the empire. Signiﬁcantly, despite
their biases against swidden cultivators, foresters engaged in a protracted
debate on the silvicultural utility of shifting cultivation and, for some parts
of the empire, resolved to adopt shifting cultivation as an intrinsic aspect of
silvicultural policy. Again, on the issue of soil erosion, it was a similar
commitment to German forestry principles that broadly dictated
the approach of the forestry delegates at the conferences. Indeed, what
distinguished the approach of foresters to such issues was their pre-eminent
concern to promote the cardinal forestry principles of the balance sheet,
sustained yield, and as environmental protection for the long term.
The Imperial Environmentalist
195
¹³⁰ See especially, Saberwal, Pastoral Politics, and Sivaramakrishnan, Modern Forests.

--- Page 213 ---

The latter point, the emergence of a strongly articulated sense of stew-
ardship based upon the ethic of efﬁciency and conservation, is yet another
theme that resonates through time. What is signiﬁcant, though, is that by
the ﬁrst half of the twentieth century, this ethic had—perhaps bolstered
by successes in science and technology and the growing clout of profes-
sional scientiﬁc communities—metamorphosed into an ideological
commitment to technocratic modes of solving human-ecological
problems. An example in this regard was the manner in which the soil ero-
sion problem was approached.
Concomitant with a technocratic mindset was a growing scientism.
The biases against people on the move, such as swidden cultivators—
whose land use practices were traditionally looked upon by foresters as
immoral and environmentally proﬂigate—were reafﬁrmed. Signiﬁcantly,
it is worth reiterating that the conference deliberations also reveal biases
against almost anyone who was not a professionally trained forester. They
included white settlers, civil servants, and other governmental ofﬁcials.
The deliberations on ﬁre, shifting cultivation, and soil erosion, for exam-
ple, illustrate the extent to which foresters saw themselves as fundament-
ally superior to other communities—native, white settler, governments,
and legislatures—on the question of how best to manage forests.
Speciﬁcally, the discussions on soil erosion underlined sharp differences
in approach and perspective between foresters, in partnership with some
agricultural scientists—both of whom had internalized the sense of crisis
provoked by the Dust Bowl and the Depression in the United States—
and other elements of the colonial technical and non-technical bureau-
cracy. Unlike the case of shifting cultivation, wherein the tensions
between programmatic generality and problematic speciﬁcity were
resolved by negotiation, empire foresters were joined in consensus on
both how to deﬁne the problem of soil erosion as well as how to resolve it.
In the process, foresters increasingly blamed other colonial ofﬁcials,
including those from other technical communities, for perpetrating eco-
logically destructive practices. Such attitudes, articulated strongly for the
ﬁrst time in these conferences, have endured to this day, and thereby set
the context for the great contests over the control of forests that were
played out in the second half of the twentieth century.¹³¹
The Imperial Environmentalist
196
¹³¹ See e.g. Westoby, The Purpose of Forests, 21.

--- Page 214 ---

¹ C. Alvares, ‘Science, Colonialism and Violence: A Luddite View’, in Nandy (ed.),
Science, Hegemony and Violence, 90.
6
The Contested Legacy
Amidst the turmoil of popular uprisings against the draft forest bill in
India in the mid 1980s, the venerable Claude Alvares wrote about the
continuities between the past and the present in the following terms:
Without the ideology of modern science, imperialism would have been compelled
to appear without its clothes . . . Colonial science has survived colonialism’s
formal departure from the third world in the form of the Trojan Horse.¹
This contested legacy of colonial eco-development was the point of
departure of this book. The purpose of this chapter is to undertake a
quick stocktaking of the major ﬁndings and contemplate some of their
wider implications.
6.1. THE NATURE OF COLONIAL FORESTRY: 
A REVISIONIST ACCOUNT
A good place to start is one of the main goals of the monograph, to explore
the agendas of resource management in colonial scientiﬁc forestry insti-
tutions. The central discovery here was the vital importance of the con-
tinental European forestry tradition. From the latter part of the early
modern period, there was, in European science, a tradition of thought
and research premissed upon hypotheses about adverse anthropogenic
environmental and climatic change. The colonial scientists campaigning
for forest conservation in the ﬁrst half of the nineteenth century were
informed by this tradition.
The agendas of the professionally trained foresters who succeeded the
early forestry advocates, and who staffed the forest departments from the

--- Page 215 ---

mid nineteenth century onward, reﬂected their priorities as progeny of
the continental European forestry tradition. Not surprisingly, the con-
sequences of their commitments—large timber monocultures and coercive
and repressive practices toward local peoples—bore signiﬁcant similarities
to the events that unfolded following the spread of the modern forestry
paradigm within Europe.
Imperial forestry in the twentieth century was, in large measure, an
attempt to consolidate on the achievements of the nineteenth. The ﬁrst
two Empire Forestry Conferences saw foresters emphatically articulating
the agenda put forward by their forebears in the previous century, albeit
with some new embellishments that twentieth-century science provided
them. This broad outlook did not change much, even as the century wore
on. The post-war context did introduce new variables into the discussion,
such as provincial devolution in different regions of the empire and,
following the Great Depression, the spectre of ecological fragility and
agrarian collapse. The debates on forest policy were however largely an
extension of those that had taken place in the ﬁrst and the second Empire
Forestry Conferences. In essence, they were attempts at reiterating the
basic conviction held by the forestry community—that the best way to
manage empire forests was to place them under the strict control of colonial
forest departments backed by strong legislation. Notably, this conviction
had been born in continental Europe and was one of the cornerstones of
that tradition.
The signiﬁcance of the continental European forestry paradigm for the
colonial context was particularly evident in the deliberations on the tech-
nical aspects of forest management, such as silviculture, production, and
utilization. These debates were effectively about what Thomas Kuhn has
termed ‘paradigm articulation’, the attempt to solve speciﬁc technical
problems arising out of an attempt to adapt European forestry principles
to the varied ecological contexts prevalent within the empire. Again, it is
important to recognize here that colonial foresters approached novel
socio-ecological problems such as shifting cultivation and soil erosion
from the paradigmatic mindset of continental forestry. The advent of the
continental system of forest management in the British Empire also
resulted, to use Lewis Mumford’s terminology, in the introduction of
an authoritarian technics in the realm of forests.² An example is the
attitude of colonial foresters toward local populations, whose claims were
The Contested Legacy
198
² Mumford, Technics and Civilization.

--- Page 216 ---

deemed illegitimate because of their ostensible scientiﬁc and technological
backwardness.³ Such perceptions and biases had their origins in Europe
and were transported by foresters when they went to the colonial periphery.
They were buttressed by the growing scientism and technocracy of the
empire forestry community, resulting often in a feeling of superiority
justiﬁed by the idea that, as scientists, they were better educated on matters
of forest use.
An important consequence of this hubris was the outbreak of conﬂicts
between foresters and the other bureaucratic and technical professional
communities of the state administrative apparatus. The bone of con-
tention was political authority for the forester.
Foresters responded by organizing themselves into an effective and
potent lobby group. The manner in which the Empire Forestry Conferences
were mooted and staged is an illustration of this approach. The confer-
ences were the outcome of effective lobbying, which strategically preyed
upon the wartime concerns about a wood procurement crisis. Once the
Empire Forestry Conferences were sanctioned, colonial foresters used the
fora to make their case before the leading politicians and decision makers
of the empire. In doing so, they adopted strong moral language to criti-
cize British attitudes toward forests and argued that Pax Britannica had
thus far been an ecological disaster with explicit economic ramiﬁcations.
Implicit in the critique was the contention that had it not been for the
efforts of the empire forestry community, the degree of the problem
would have been greater.
6.2. POSTSCRIPT: WIDENING THE ARGUMENT
The aforementioned ﬁndings speak forcefully to three widely held and
interrelated theories about the history of imperial science and environ-
mentalism. At the outset, they problematize a commonly believed notion
that imperial policies in the colonized tropical world resulted in a socio-
ecological watershed.
Although the ecological and social relations deﬁned by forestry in the
British Empire were extractive and ‘colonial’, respectively, their implicit
modes of production cannot be understood simply by invoking cultural
The Contested Legacy
199
³ For a full blown analysis of such a perception in world history, see Adas, Machines as
the Measure of Men.

--- Page 217 ---

or economic imperialism as an explanandum, as is so often done in the
historiography of forestry in the British Empire. At the cost of repeating
the point, it is worth remarking that the ‘environmental imperialism’ of
colonial forestry was at least partially a result of the transplantation into a
colonial realm of the social and political contexts that had deﬁned forestry
in Europe in the ﬁrst place. Put differently, there are signiﬁcant family
resemblances between continental and colonial forestry—and these simi-
larities force a more critical examination of the socio-ecological system
that characterized continental forestry.⁴
Such an examination indicates that forestry as a technology was not
merely a product of colonial expediency. On the contrary, it provided
a context for colonial politics just as it was shaped and moulded by it.
Similarly, colonial foresters were not mere ‘servants of the state’; they were
carriers of a culture of technology that had its origins in another setting,
even if the imperial context exacerbated the impact of their actions.
Moreover, against the backdrop of the debate over whether or not colonial
forestry constituted a watershed in the environmental history of the
British Empire, it is important to note that scientiﬁc forestry, as a regime
of resource management, was designed to be a major watershed even in
continental Europe, its place of birth.
A second important theoretical assumption in the historiography of
colonial science that the foregoing account problematizes is the distinction
traditionally drawn between western and non-western science. In seeking
to distinguish European sciences and technics and non-western know-
ledge systems and practices, scholars often argue that modern European
scientiﬁc and technological systems were characterized by an attention
to method and a concern with universalizing knowledge claims. Non-
western, indigenous knowledge systems, in this view, are looked upon
as having practical, local, relevance, but little possibility of universal
validity.
The analysis in Chapter 2, however, helps stake out a different approach.
To recapitulate, it argued that the birth of continental European forestry
meant a great displacement—the disembedding of indigenous know-
ledge systems in Europe from their traditional or historical contexts. It
also entailed the reassembly of some pieces of earlier practices, such as
local knowledge of the properties of particular species and soils, within
the socio-economic and political grid deﬁned by newly emerging frame-
works of political economy and state making, such as cameralism. The
The Contested Legacy
200
⁴ The term ‘family resemblance’ is used here in the sense deﬁned by Ludwig Wittgenstein
in Philosophical Investigations.

--- Page 218 ---

novelty of the new forestry, thus, lay in its explicit socio-economic
construction—as a scientiﬁc and technological system designed to pro-
mote the goals of the emerging doctrines of modern political economy.
This construction produced what it was designed to. It created new
landscapes, characterized by monocultures; and it spawned new relation-
ships between nature, national economy, science and technology, local
people, and the state. It also nurtured a new social cadre—professional
experts, whose mandate answered, not directly to local peoples or contexts,
but instead to the goals of a reconstituted national economy.
The great transformation of nature and society brought forth by
cameralist political economy was designed to emancipate people, among
other things, by scientiﬁcally mobilizing resources and, thereby, developing
the economy. A product of the enlightenment, it drew deeply upon the
classical idea of perfectibility and the theological traditions of dominion
and responsibility. As a result, doctrines of political economy, such as
cameralism, were justiﬁed and promoted as harbingers of progress. The
very fact that they brought material prosperity in their wake served further
to bolster the progressivist ideology. However, as the French contestations
over German silviculture indicate, the socio-ecological impact of technics
based on and embedded within modern political economy also spawned
what became a long tradition of dissent, or a double movement against
modernist approaches to resource management.⁵
Put differently, although modern forestry developed novel obser-
vational, inferential, and validation techniques, its fundamental difference,
relative to the preceding indigenous practices, was in the manner in
which it related society and nature, science and technology, and economy
and governance. The transfer of modern European technics such as forestry
to colonial contexts brought forth similar transformations in relation-
ships between economy and science and nature and society. The fact that
these technologies, knowledge systems, and economic regimes were
embedded simultaneously in the extractive inequities of imperialism
meant that they were doubly colonial and coercive. Although the ‘western’
versus ‘non-western’ distinction drawn by historians is undoubtedly salient,
it is equally important to recognize that underlying it is a critical distinction
between ‘modern’ and pre-modern technics.
A third important and commonly held distinction that the foregoing
account blurs is that between the colonial and the post-colonial. This
The Contested Legacy
201
⁵ The phrase ‘double movement’ is used in the sense deﬁned by Karl Polanyi in
Primitive, Archaic and Modern Economies: Essays of Karl Polanyi, ed. George Dalton
(New York, 1968).

--- Page 219 ---

distinction is drawn upon the belief, often more imagined than real, that
the decline of formal empires marked the beginning of the end of the
socio-economic relations that had been engendered within imperialism.
Needless to say, the claim of such a disjuncture often formed the basis for
the legitimacy of the nationalist governments of the newly independent
states. Indeed, such governments, which rode on the backs of anti-
colonial struggles, rhetorically claimed power on manifestos of facilitating
emancipation.
There is a signiﬁcant and legitimate history to support such claims. In
the aftermath of the First World War, the goals of colonial science began
to be focused and deﬁned. The question, put crudely, was how to do the
business of colonial exploitation without inviting a backlash from the
natives. As Lugard put it in articulating his dual mandate argument with
reference to the African case:
That the economic development of African territories is no less a duty than that
of securing the welfare of natives is not questioned. The problem is how these two
duties should be reconciled without on the one hand subordinating policy to a
purely utilitarian outlook, or on the other hand adopting a standpoint too
exclusively philanthropic.⁶
By the end of the Second World War, a new set of concerns began to
inﬂuence the debate about science policy. These were mooted by ofﬁcials
inﬂuenced by Fabian socialism.⁷ Driven, among other things, by guilt
stemming from the recognition of the ‘generosity’ of the colonies, which
were contributing approximately £17 million a year, members of the
inﬂuential Fabian Colonial Bureau engaged in a great deal of soul search-
ing about Britian’s tropical responsibilities.⁸They concluded that the way
forward was to help contribute to the ‘development’ of the colonies. An
excellent illustration of their conception of colonial development is the
following excerpt from the ‘Partners in Progress’ speech to the Africa
The Contested Legacy
202
⁶ See Lugard (4): RHO MSS Afr. S. 1829, Box 2, ﬁle 2 (1925), p. 39. It is important to
note here that in the African context, the question of science policy was also closely tied
to concerns of demographic collapse owing to diseases contracted either due to travel or
through the new labour regimen that Africans ostensibly could not bear. Moreover, there
were new emerging diseases, and it was feared that all these factors contributed to a shrink-
ing labour pool. It was widely believed that the answer lay both in regulating labour supply
and in spreading development. See e.g. ‘Notes of Conversation with Dr. Wicliffe Rose, 27th
October 1925’ (MSS Lugard (4), RHO MSS Afr. S. 1829, Box 2, ﬁle 2, pp. 49–51). See
also Malinowski, Memorandum on Colonial Research (MSS Lugard (4), RHO MSS Afr. S.
1829, Box 2, ﬁle 2, pp. 56–64.
⁷ The Fabian socialist connection is evident in reading through the ﬁles of several of the
individuals involved with colonial science policy during this period.
⁸ See e.g. RHO MSS Brit. Emp. S. 365, FCB 46/3/12, FCB 46/3/144–9.

--- Page 220 ---

Conference in 1948, by the secretary of state for the colonies, Arthur
Creech Jones:
We all want a stable and peaceful world . . . You want good standards and con-
ditions. The world wants what you can make and produce. You need what Western
Civilization can offer. From the reservoir of our experience and knowledge in the
Western world we contribute as partners in supplying the things essential to your
development.⁹
Jones went on to say that social progress was only possible through
‘improving agriculture, establishing industries, exploiting minerals, expand-
ing production and selling a proportion of the production overseas’.¹⁰
It is very signiﬁcant that such sentiments were expressed around the
same time that President Truman, in his inaugural address, made a call for
the ‘development of backward areas’. Jones himself wrote a note acknow-
ledging Truman’s speech and calling for co-operation between the United
States and Britain on the colonies.¹¹ However, the advent of the United
States as a player in colonial politics, and the fact that it had actually begun
contributing by means of aid for development, was a source of concern for
members of the Fabian Colonial Bureau and the Labour Party, more gen-
erally. They were worried by the possibility both that Britain’s inﬂuence in
the colonies would wane and that the American agenda would be contrary
to their own progressive goals—of ensuring a socialist world order.¹²
Colonial science in the British Empire in the aftermath of the Second
World War was thus tied up with both the geopolitics of third world
development and the seductive paternalism of Fabian socialist attitudes
to decolonization. Consequently, there was a concerted attempt to ensure
that the imperial scientiﬁc network that had been built over several decades
would serve the needs of the empire and survive the transition to the post-
colonial era. An ofﬁcial statement in 1948 therefore explicitly deﬁned
the purpose of British colonial policy to be ‘to guide the colonial terri-
tories to responsible government within the Commonwealth’.¹³ Many
colonial scientiﬁc ofﬁcials interpreted this to ‘mandate “the sacred trust of
civilisation”—to bring all native peoples who were under their suzerainty
The Contested Legacy
203
⁹ Creech Jones Papers. See RHO MSS Brit. Emp. S. 332, Box 23, Item 3, p. 7.
¹⁰ Ibid.
¹¹ Creech Jones Papers. See Box 44, ﬁle 1.
¹² Ibid. The sense of redemption through science and of the moral burden of Britain for
ensuring colonial development resonates through Creech Jones’s papers as well as those of
E. B. Worthington. Moreover, there is a hint of a strategic competition with the USA
which, it was feared, was extending its sphere of inﬂuence in the tropics, among other ways,
through its philanthropic institutions.
¹³ Jefferies, A Review of Colonial Research, 43.

--- Page 221 ---

to a state of self-determination’.¹⁴ For some imperial scientiﬁc ofﬁcers
working on natural resource management, this meant ensuring, often as
an urgent priority, that they left an institutional and ideological legacy
that would endure and beneﬁt the peoples of the colonies. A good illus-
tration of this spirit is the following poem, cited by one colonial forester
as exemplifying the spirit of their sense of mission and the justiﬁcation for
their very presence in the empire:
young men in your pride make haste
The wrong to right, the bond to free
And plant a garden in the waste.¹⁵
Signiﬁcantly, some ex-colonial scientiﬁc ofﬁcials believe that the scient-
iﬁc institutions of resource management established during imperial rule,
and their constitutive disciplines, traditions, and agendas, have indeed
carried over into the post-colonial era. An example is the following obser-
vation made by an ex-colonial forester in the mid 1970s:
It has been encouraging to read the progress reports for the period 1966–1972
presented to the Tenth Commonwealth Forestry Conference by the African
countries. They show no diminution of forestry activity . . . All refer to forest
policies, the continuing development of forest resources under management
plans, research and local education and training of technicians and workers.¹⁶
It is by no means surprising that the colonial scientiﬁc legacy survived
formal decolonization. Indeed, many post-colonial leaders actively sought
the return of colonial experts.¹⁷ Some, like Julius Nyerere and Jawaharlal
Nehru, actively embraced the ideology of Fabian socialist development.¹⁸
Herein lies one of the great paradoxes of the second half of the twentieth
century. Although nationalist movements embraced political freedom,
their chosen paths of socio-economic development were enthusiastic and
often uncritical embracement of the scientiﬁc, technological, and economic
policies put in place within a colonial context.¹⁹ Indeed, British experts
who, with the lowering of the ﬂag, sailed back to their motherland were
wooed back as consultants, either through intergovernmental exchanges
such as the Colombo plan, or within international schemes under the
auspices of the various organizations of the United Nations system.
As a consequence, while nationalist governments paid at least rhetorical
homage to the principles of equity, distribution, justice, and emancipation,
The Contested Legacy
204
¹⁴ R. G. Sangster, IFCAS Symp. 71.
¹⁵ Ibid. The poem is attributed to John Buchan.
¹⁶ Ibid. 78.
¹⁷ For an instructive source on this see D. Barton’s letter to Julius Nyerere, RHO MSS
Afr. S. 2118.
¹⁸ R. W. Shenton and Michael Cowen, Doctrines of Development (London, 1996).
¹⁹ Ibid.

--- Page 222 ---

they failed to explore the possibility that the relationships encoded within
the socio-economic and ecological matrices of European technics might, in
some contexts, hinder the realization of precisely such objectives. Put differ-
ently, in their uncritical acceptance of the rhetoric of emancipation through
modern science and technology, they failed to recognize that the design of
knowledge and technological systems needed to be reﬂectively understood
and carefully calibrated. Consequently, as soon as the honeymoon period
was over and the euphoria of independence faded, local peoples all over
the third world began to protest at the continuance of coercive practices.
It should be noted here that the legitimacy of the post-colonial govern-
ments; their rhetorical equation of social and economic emancipation with
modernization, and the power of local elites that thrived on colonial prac-
tices, allowed for passionate and coercive enforcement of state policies by
resource management technocracies such as forest departments. With the
re-emergence of coercion, a new wave of protest movements began.
The nature and dynamics of such social movements have, in the case of
forestry, been thoroughly explored by scholars in a variety of contexts.
However, there is one aspect of this contested legacy that has not garnered
much attention. This aspect has to do with the critiques of science with
which this book began. The point, in essence, is that there has been more
to third world environmental social movements than public protest. For
every tree-hugging activist or an everyday struggle in villages, there has
been an equal and complementary double movement, produced by non-
governmental organizations, intellectuals, scholars, and scientists. The locus
of these complementary revolts has been the claim that there are alternat-
ive routes to socio-economic emancipation, pathways that are ‘more’
equitable and, in the long run, ‘more’ ecologically sustainable than the
extant order promoted by the state institutions.²⁰
Broadly speaking, the rhetoric of the advocates of such new visions of
development spins around the felt need to strengthen the capacity of local
citizens to access and nurture their resource bases and life-supporting
socio-ecological systems. They have emphasized the need to promote the
erection of governance frameworks wherein decision-making power is
democratic and, where possible, devolved to local peoples and institutions.
Such programmes have also concomitantly attempted to create ‘locally
relevant’ technologies, knowledge systems, and resource management
and conﬂict resolution frameworks.
The vision of social and economic development in the third world
implicit in these so-called ‘environmental governance’ frameworks is also
The Contested Legacy
205
²⁰ It is useful to notice that at the roots of such claims are attempts at re-embedding
knowledge making and technological design within new social and moral paradigms.

--- Page 223 ---

characterized by attributes such as ecological viability, sustainability, social
equity, democracy, and public accountability. Over the past decade, this
vision has been translated into a number of ‘success’ stories across Asia,
Africa, and Latin America. In the case of forestry, Joint Forest Management
in India, and Campﬁre in Mozambique, to take two examples, are, to
many in the global environmental community today, blueprints for an
ecologically sustainable and socially equitable new world order. The
range of institutions now that, at least rhetorically, seek to replicate such
models spans everything from local NGOs to organizations such as US
AID and the World Bank.
While the question of whether the alternative hybrid programmes
being practised on the ground are actually successful is moot, the very fact
of their existence is signiﬁcant to historians of science, technology, develop-
ment, and the environment.²¹ Indeed, they point to the idea that know-
ledge and artefact making, economic design and institution building, in
the name of socio-economic emancipation do not need to follow the
cookie-cutter models established amidst the revolutionary fervour of the
enlightenment. On the contrary, the enlightened ideals of that era can be
harnessed toward a grand project of reﬂexive experimentation—one that
iconoclastically re-examines and reworks every brick and ediﬁce of devel-
opment theory.
In the case of forestry, such a process has already begun, for hybrids
such as joint forest management, in theory, advocate neither the classical
modernist agenda nor a return to the past. Instead, at least rhetorically,
they experiment with new, reﬂexive modernities, which seek to redraw
the old contract with nature, and replace it with new matrices that rede-
ﬁne the relationships between local peoples and nation-states, between
the state and the economy, and between economy and nature. If the story
of the history of modern forestry was that of the social construction of a
technological system, the unfolding history of forestry is a tantalizing
pointer toward the possibilities implicit in a deliberated and reﬂexive
reconstruction.²²
The Contested Legacy
206
²¹ See S. Ravi Rajan, ‘Disaster, Development and Governance: Reﬂections on the
“lessons” from Bhopal’, Environmental Values, 11 (2002), 369–94.
²² I use the term ‘reﬂexive’ in the sense developed by Anthony Giddens and Ulrich Beck
in their various writings on risk society and reﬂexive modernization. See Ulrich Beck,
Anthony Giddens, and Scott Lash, Reﬂexive Modernization: Politics, Tradition and Aesthetics
in the Modern Social Order (Stanford, Calif., 1995).

APPENDIX 2
Brief Proﬁles of Some Colonial 
Scientist-Conservationists in India in the Period
1800–1850
Ainslie, Sir Whitelaw (1767–1837)
Education Studied medicine in Edinburgh.
Main career Doctor employed by the East India Company.
Important work as colonial scientist and environmentalist Wrote Materia
Medica of Hindoostan and Artisans and Agriculturalists Nomenclature in 1813 and
Materia Indica: Or, Some Account of those Articles which are Employed by Hindoos
and Other Eastern Nations in their Medicine, Arts and Architecturein 1826. Became
an important propogandizer of the need for a permanent forest policy in India.
Balfour, Edward Green (1813–89)
Education Studied surgery in Edinburgh.
Main career Went to India in 1834 where he joined the medical department
of the Indian army. Established himself as an authority on public health by the
mid 1840s and published a number of statistical studies on diseases and health.
Important work as colonial scientist and environmentalist Wrote a book on
the impact of climate on the health of troops in 1845. Wrote a major report in
1849, ‘On the Inﬂuence Exercised by Trees on the Climate of the Country’.
Established the Government Central Museum in 1850 and, as its superinten-
dent, published many scientiﬁc works including a classiﬁed list of the Mollusca,
and reports on the iron ores and the coals of the Madras Presidency, among
others. Published his Cyclopaedia in 1857. This book went through many
subsequent editions. In 1866, published Timber Trees, Timber and Fancy Woods,
as also the Forests of India and of Eastern and Southern Asia. Wrote two pamphlets
before leaving India under the general title ‘Medical Hints to the People of
India’. Returned to England in 1876 where he published Indian Forestry (1885)
and The Agricultural Pests of India and of Eastern and Southern Asia (1887).
Beddome, Henry Richard (1830–1911)
Education Charterhouse.
Main career Joined the Indian army, served in Jabalpur from 1848 and the
Madras Infantry from 1856.
Important work as colonial scientist and environmentalist Devoted to botan-
ical and zoological studies while in the army. Appointed senior assistant to the

--- Page 228 ---

newly organized forest department in 1857 and conservator of forests in
Madras from 1860 to retirement in 1882. Wrote ‘Report on the Vegetable
Products of the Pulney Hills, Madras’ and ‘Flora of Pulney Hills’ (Madras
Journal of Literature and Science, 19, 1858); ‘Contribution to the Botany of
Southern India’ (Madras Journal of Literature and Science, 22–3, 1861–2); The
Trees of the Madras Presidency (1863); Report of the Conservator of Forests
(1862–3); The Ferns of South India and Ceylon (1864); The Ferns of British
India (3 vol., 1865–70); A List of Exogenous Plants Found in the Annamalai
Mountains (1865); Flora Sylvatica for South India (1869); ‘The Forest Flora of
the Nilgiris’ (Indian Forester, 2, 1876); ‘The Forests and Flora of the
Tinnelvelly District’ (Indian Forester, 3, 1877); ‘The Jeypore Forests’ (Indian
Forester, 3, 1877); Report on the Nelambur Teak Plantations (1878); and On
Eucalyptus Plantations in India (1885).
Cleghorn, Hugh Francis (1820–95)
Education MD, Edinburgh, 1841.
Main career After graduation in 1841, proceeded at the age of 22 to Madras
where he was attached to the Madras General Hospital to study Indian
diseases. This job entailed a great deal of travel with regiments and Cleghorn
used this as an opportunity to undertake botanical research. In 1851
appointed professor in botany in Madras. Appointed conservator of forests,
Madras Presidency, in 1856 and joint commissioner for the conservancy of
forests, along with Dietrich Brandis, in 1862. In 1867, a year in which Brandis
was in England on leave, he acted as the inspector-general of forests of India.
After retiring from the Indian service in 1869, he taught botany at the
University of Glasgow and promoted forestry in Britain, serving as president
of the Royal Scottish Arboricultural Society, and giving evidence before the
Forestry Committee appointed by the House of Commons.
Important work as colonial scientist and environmentalist Was arguably
the most important campaigner for forest conservancy in India in the ﬁrst half
of the nineteenth century. In the early 1860s he examined the forests of the
Himalayas, including Kashmir and the Trans-Indus territory, at the request of
the governor-general. Wrote Forests and Gardens of Southern India in 1861 and
Report upon the Forests of the Punjab and the Western Himalaya in 1864 and
ﬂoras of the Annamalai hills and the Sutlej valley. Visited several forestry
schools in Europe, including the Royal Forest School in Vallombrosa, Italy, to
assist in the setting up of a forestry school for British colonial foresters. In
1848, when he returned to Britain on sick leave, he assisted in the preparation
of the catalogue of raw products for the Great Exhibition of 1851.
Dalzell, Nicol Alexander (1817–78)
Education MA, Edinburgh.
Main career Customs ofﬁcer.
Important work as colonial scientist and environmentalist Practised
botany in his leisure time at customs. Joined the Indian forest service in 1841
Some Colonial Scientist-Conservationists
211

--- Page 229 ---

and worked there until 1870. Became conservator of forests in Burma and in
Bombay, when he succeeded Alexander Gibson (see below). Wrote
‘Contributions to the Botany of Western India’ (1850); Forest Reports for the
Bombay Presidency (1860–1; 1867–8; 1868–9); The Bombay Flora (1861—
co-authored with Gibson); wrote ﬂoras of western India.
Falconer, Hugh (1808–67)
Education MD, Edinburgh, 1829.
Main career EIC surgeon (1830); superintendent of the Saharanpur
Botanical Garden (1832–41); superintendent of the Calcutta Botanical
Garden (1848–55); professor at Calcutta Medical College (1848–55).
Important work as colonial scientist and environmentalist Wrote
Memorandum Respecting Timber Trees and Materials for Fuel (1848); Report on
the Teak Forests of the Tenasserim Province (1852); Report on the Teak Plantations
of Bengal (1857); ﬂoras of the Upper Gangetic Plains. Important ﬁgure in the
introduction of tea in India; responsible for the introduction of quinine-yielding
cinchonas to India.
Gibson, Alexander (1800–67)
Education MD, Edinburgh.
Main career Appointed to the EIC medical service in 1825. Appointed a
vaccinator for Deccan and Khandesh in 1936. Appointed superintendent of
the Dapoori Botanical Garden in 1836. Elected a Fellow of the Linnean
Society in 1853.
Important work as colonial scientist and environmentalist While vaccina-
tor he acquired a widespread ﬁeld knowledge of the biota and the rural
economies of the region. While superintendent of the Dapoori Botanical
Garden he produced several drugs for the use of the medical service. His report
on teak deforestation submitted to the Bombay government in 1840 led
directly to his appointment as conservator of forests in the presidency.
Kyd, Robert (1746–93)
Education Unknown.
Main Career Bengal Infantry (1765; Lt.-Col. 1782; superintendent of the
EIC Dockyard and secretary of the Military Board of Fort William; hon.
superintendent of the Calcutta Botanical Garden (1786–7).
Important work as colonial scientist and environmentalist Founder of
Calcutta Botanical Garden following the 1796 famine ‘not for the purpose
of collecting rare plants (although they also have their uses) as things of
mere curiosity or furnishing articles for the gratiﬁcation of luxury, but for
establishing a stock for disseminating such articles as may prove beneﬁcial to
the inhabitants’.
McClelland, John (1805–83)
Education Unknown.
Appendix 2
212

--- Page 230 ---

Main career Bengal medical service (1846–65); superintendent of the
Calcutta Botanical Gardens.
Important work as colonial scientist and environmentalist Editor of the
Calcutta Journal of Natural History (Vols. 1–8—1840–7). Set up herbaria in
West Bengal (Birgham, 1849) and Burma (Pegu, 1855). Contributed to the
Calcutta, Edinburgh, and Kew Botanical Gardens.
Roxburgh, William (1751–1815)
Education Studied botany.
Main career East India Company botanist who ﬁrst went to India in
1776. From 1781 he was in charge of the Samalkot Botanical Garden in
the Madras Presidency where pepper, sappam, tobacco, and cardamoms were
grown. His task on joining the botanical garden was to make an exhaustive
survey of India’s ﬂora. From 1789 to 1793, he was the EIC botanist in the
Carnatic.
Important work as colonial scientist and environmentalist Distinguished
himself by studying the ﬂora of the northern Sirkars and the Coromandel
coast. Also began descriptions of Indian ﬂora giving a number to each plant
and at the same time having a life-size drawing made to which the number was
given. By the time he retired in 1813, 2,542 paintings had been made by his
team. He contributed substantially to Hortus Bengalensis, Flora Indica, Wight’s
Incones Plantarum Indiae Orientalis, and Plants of the Coromandel, published
by Joseph Banks at company expense.
Royle, John Forbes (1799–1858)
Education MD, Edinburgh.
Main career Was in Bengal in 1818–23 where he became particularly inter-
ested in the medicinal properties of plants and their geographical distribut-
ing. Appointed to the Saharanpur Botanical Garden where under him plants
were listed and drawings made in the same way as at Sibpur. Was professor
of materia medica and therapeutics at King’s College, London, from 1837
to 1856.
Important work as colonial scientist and environmentalist While posted
at Saharanpur he studied the ﬂora of the Himalayas in the area between
Saharanpur in the south and Kashmir in the north. He made some of the ear-
liest collections in the Himalayan area which later were arranged at his herbar-
ium. He also commissioned paintings of plant specimens which were kept
along with the dried plants. He wrote an eleven-part book: Illustrations of the
Botany and Other Branches of the Natural History of the Himalayan Mountains
and of the Flora of Cashmere, between 1833 and 1840. After retirement in
1831, he published a number of learned papers on Indian botany and
medicine. During this time he continued to be closely associated with Indian
matters. He was also in charge of the Hall of Indian Products at the Great
Exhibition of 1851 and the Paris Exhibition of 1855.
Some Colonial Scientist-Conservationists
213

--- Page 231 ---

Wallich, Dr Nathaniel (1786–1854)
Education Studied medicine in Copenhagen.
Main career Surgeon at Serampore, 1807. Joined the British colonial med-
ical service at Calcutta in 1814. Joined the Sibpur Botanic Gardens in 1815
and was its superintendent until 1846.
Important work as colonial scientist and environmentalist Made an enor-
mous impact on tropical botany, having classiﬁed dozens of species, especially
the palm genus Wallichia. Under his aegis, Roxburgh’s scheme for adding to
the systematic knowledge of India’s ﬂora was continued. In 1825 he was
dipatched to the Upper Provinces to enquire into and watch over the extensive
forests of the empire. His team, known as ‘The Plantation Committee’,
produced 1,070 pages of manuscript on forest cultivation.
